NIGERIA
SUPPLEMENTARY ANNEXES TO
CENTRAL AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECTS
Preface
This report contains annexes which are common to two agricultural
development projects in Central Nigeria. These are:
- Ayangba Agricultural Development Project in Benue State, and
- Lafia Agricultural Development Project in Plateau State. 1/
Since these two projects are very similar, material common to
the two projects has been collected in this separate volume in order to
avoid duplication, and only brief summaries are presented in the respective
appraisal reports.  Coverage in this report is extensive as this allows
detailed back-up of the analysis in the two appraisal reports. This should
be especially useful for project management and supervision. The report,
therefore, is a collection of various important working documents and has been
edited as such; it has not been distributed as an appraisal report, but is
available upon request.
Supplementary annexes were prepared by the appraisal mission com-
posed of Messrs. van der Lugt, Pease, Draper (Bank), Reader, Hallgrimson
(Consultants), Tagoe (WARDA), Deprez (Trainee CPDCET) and Dalton (FDL expert),
which visited Nigeria in May/June 1976 to appraise the above two projects.
1/   See Report 1369a-UNI, dated May 20, 1977, Appraisal of Ayangba Agri-
cultural Development Project and Report 1368a-UNI, dated May 20, 1977,
Appraisal of Lafia Agricultural Development Project.






SUPPLEMENT 1
NIGERIA
CENTRAL AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECTS
AGRICULTURE IN NIGERIA
Background
1.        There are a number of Bank reports on Nigeria, although updating
is required in some cases. For example, in the general economic field the
following have been published:
(i)  Report No. AW-35a, dated June 9, 1972, The Current Economic
Position and Long Term Prospects of Nigeria (four volumes). 1/
(ii)  Report No. 416a-UNI, dated August 5, 1974, Current Economic
Position and Prospects of Nigeria (three volumes).
(iii)  Report No. 1235-UNI, dated June 30, 1976, Nigeria Economic
Memorandum.
2.        The first economic report assessed the postwar reconstruction work
needed and formed the basis for increased Bank assistance.  In contrast,
the latest economic memorandum briefly assesses the latest five-year develop-
ment plan (1975-1980) from the macroeconomic point of view. Initially the
"oil boom" had a major impact on the Nigerian economy, but, with a more
realistic assessment of future oil wealth, financial projections are becoming
increasingly pessimistic.
3.        In agriculture .the main Bank report is:
- Report No. PA-115(A), dated January 26, 1973, Agricultural
Sector Survey Nigeria (in three volumes).
In addition food production was extensively treated in the 1974 economic report:
- Report No. 416a-UNI, Current Economic Position and Prospects of
Nigeria, Volume III, Problems and Prospects of Food Production.
4.        Another report 2/ which recently became available in Nigeria is:
1/   This report was published by Johns Hopkins University Press as "Nigeria,
Options for Long-Term Development", 1974.
2/   This supplement uses this report to a large extent as its base.



SUPPLEMENT I
Page 2
- Agricultural Development in Nigeria 1973-1975, published by the
Federal Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources 1/, Govern-
ment-Planning Committee, Lagos 1974.
This report arose from a National Agricultural Development Seminar held at the
University of Ibadan in 1971 whose findings were published by the joint plan-
ning committee of the Federal Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources.
Some of the projections in this report have already been challenged (see also
Supplement 11 on demand and supply projections) and further updating is
inevitable. For these reasons this supplement can, at this stage, only pro-
vide a background to the Nigerian agricultural sector.
The Economy
5.        Following the recent creation of seven new states, the Federal
Republic of Nigeria now has 19 States. It covers an area of 924,000 km2.
Reliable population figures are absent as the 1973 census was never completed.
However, it is believed that Nigeria's population is now 75 million (1975
estimate). GNP per capita (around US$327 in 1975) remains low and most of the
Nigerian people earn less than this. For example, in rural areas, economic
activities are based primarily on smallholder farming with low-level tech-
nology for which average household incomes would not exceed US$320 per annum.
In urban centers average household incomes are much higher but unequally
distributed.
6.        Economic growth in Nigeria over the last six years was in two phases:
from January 1970 (the end of the war) until December 1972 was a period of
rehabilitation and recovery as the economy responded quickly to pent-up demand
and realization of postponed investment and production; following a year of
consolidation and slower growth in 1972, the second phase was characterized by
a resumption of growth, generated by increased public sector activities,
accompanied by increased inflation, supply shortages and infrastructural
congestions as excess capacity was eliminated. Although the performance of
directly productive sectors has been disappointing, the rapid economic growth
continued due to phenomenal expansion of the service sectors, and average GDP
increase was 6.5% per annum from 1972-1975. Nigeria's economic development
has been increasingly dominated by the oil sector which by 1974 accounted for
41% of GDP, 93% of export earnings and 82% of total Government revenues,
surpassing the agricultural sector in all areas except employment.
7.        The Third National Development Plan covers the five-year period
April 1975 - March 1980. Plan allocations are US$53 billion, but due to
slippage the effective program is projected at US$32 billion. The plan has
been reviewed by the current Government and more emphasis has been given to
health, housing and agriculture. Additionally, Government recently launched an
"Operation Feed the Nation" program to encourage food production in the
country.
1/   Since early 1976 renamed Federal Ministry of Agriculture and Rural
Development (FMARD).



SUPPLEMENT I
Page 3
The Agricultural Sector in the Economy
8.        Although economic growth has been high, performance in the agricul-
tural sector has been disappointing. The average growth rate in the agricul-
tural sector over the period 1970-1975 was 1.3% 1/ compared with 7.0% for GDP
and 6.2% for nonoil GDP. Serious drought conditions in the northern part of
the country led to a sharp decrease in output in 1972 and 1973, and output of
millet and sorghum, major food crops in the north, was an estimated 30% lower
in 1973 than in 1970; for groundnuts the output was almost 50% lower. Due to
favorable weather conditions in 1974 and 1975, millet and sorghum output
recovered significantly, but groundnuts continued to suffer from disease and
other problems. Production in the south was less affected. However, agricul-
tural sector growth continues to be impeded by the low level of technology,
declining productivity on existing farms and substantial migration of the
rural labor force to urban centers. Despite this some 75% of employment is
in the agricultural sector. Agriculture's share in GDP has declined from 48%
in 1970 to 37% in 1975 (in constant 1973 prices) and from 49% in 1970 to 28%
in 1975 in current terms. Details are in Table 1.
9.        Foodcrop production over the last decade has not kept pace with
population growth. Output in recent years is estimated to be 25-30% higher
than the average 1961-1965 level compared with a population increase of
some 35-40% in the same period. Per capita income also increased during this
period by about 50%; assuming an income elasticity of demand of about 0.5 and
the above-mentioned population increase, the increase in demand must have been
twice the estimated increase in output. Even allowing for a wide margin of
error in the assumptions, they point to a substantial gap between output and
demand growth. This is confirmed by sharp increases in both food prices and
food imports; the latter rose from about N 50 million in 1970 to around N 300
million in 1975. 2/  For details see Table 2.
10.       The long-term trend in export crop production is equally unsatis-
factory. Recently, Marketing Board policies were changed to provide incen-
tives for investment in export crops, especially tree crops, but the impact of
these policies (if any) will not be felt for many years. Cocoa output is at
the same level as in the early sixties; oil palm production is some 10-15%
lower and groundnut production has decreased by some 70-80%. Due to decreased
output and increased domestic demand, palm oil and groundnuts are no longer
exported and only cocoa remains as a significant export crop. Details are in
Table 3. Agricultural commodities have become insignificant in terms of total
exports from Nigeria, decreasing from 37% of total exports in 1970 to 5% in
1975. However, they are still important when oil exports are excluded,
accounting for 89% in 1970 and 73% in 1975.
1/   The annual growth rates for various years were 1971 5.2%; 1972 -7.3%;
1973 -1.9%; 1974 6.7%; 1975 4.5%
2/   As a percentage of total imports, food imports remained fairly constant
at 8-10% during the 1970-1975 period, but this is due to the massive
overall increase in imports during the period.



SUPPLEMENT 1
Page 4
The Setting
11.       With an area of 924,000 km2, Nigeria has its greatest east-west
length over 1,127 km and greatest north-south length over some 1,046 km.
The coastline is 960 km. Nigerian Atlantic territorial waters extend to
48 km offshore. 1/ The continental shelf is narrow (12-28 km), but fairly
wide in the area of the Niger Delta (80 km).
12.       The Nigerian coastline consists of a series of surf-swept sandy
beaches (0.3-16 km wide). In the west, this is very regular; towards the
east it becomes an intricate network of creeks which form the Niger Delta.
The coastline is backed by a belt of mangrove swamps that extend up to 96 km.
Beyond the marshlands is an expanse of undulating country with scattered hills
progressively going to hilly ranges. Further inland, the topography develops
into an undulating plateau with hills of granite rock and sandstone at an
elevation of about 600 m. This rises to the central plateau (over 1,830
meter). The northern extremities stretch in rolling but sandy topography.
13.       There are three major drainage systems. The first and major drainage
system consists of the Niger and its main tributary the Benue. Together with
all their tributaries they form the Niger-Benue drainage system (see map
12135(3R) attached to this supplement). The second drainage system comprises
the Komadugu Yobe river and its tributaries which empty their water into Lake
Chad. The third system consists of the many rivers flowing southwards into
the Atlantic.
14.       Nigeria's climate is tropical with year-round high temperatures.
Climate and vegetational zoning are given in a map attached to this supple-
ment. The rainy season is as short as three months in the far north and
as long as 11 months in the far south. Rainfall varies between 50 cm (far
north) and 400 cm (far south). In the south temperatures and humidity are
high with little variation; the range increases further north and the dry
season there is accentuated by the "harmattan" winds bringing cold air
from the Sahara.
15.       The vegetational zoning largely reflects the climatic zoning.
There are three zones: the tropical rain forest zone of the south with
luxuriant tree growth; the northern Sudan Savanna zone with tall grasses
and acacia trees; and the Guinea Savanna zone which is the "middle" belt in
between the two first distinct zones. Cropping patterns follow the same
zoning: perennial treecrops and multiple cropping in the south to single
annual cropping in the north. See also a map attached to this supplement.
Crop possibilities in various ecological zones are in Table 4.
1/   This might be changed following changes in territorial waters in other
countries.



SUPPLEMENT 1
Page 5
History
16.       Agriculture.  During colonial times (1861-1950) emphasis was on
extension and research.  Moor Plantation was, for example, established in
1899. During the Second World War more attention was given to food production
and export crops, such as rubber, to satisfy war demand. During the period
of internal self-government (1950-1960), agricultural functions were divided,
with the Federal Department of Agriculture responsible for research and the
regional departments for extension. After independence there was increased
regional competition and heavy concentration on export commodities. The
marketing boards were used as taxing mechanisms for development finance and
there was a general feeling that food production activities could take care of
themselves without Government intervention.
17.       Livestock.  Livestock production in Nigeria was characterized by
nomadic and seasonal pastoralism long before the advent of the colonial admin-
istration. The immediate interest of the colonial administration was the
health and hygiene of domestic cattle, and the Veterinary Department was
established in Zaria in 1974. 1/ The research center at Vom was established
in 1924. The department was reorganized by the end of the Second World War
and livestock activities have been well organized. Initial breeding policy
was concentrated on local available breeds, but in the fifties there was
increased attention on international breeds. In the sixties many dispersed
activities were undertaken throughout the sector.
18.       Fisheries.  The history of fisheries is fairly recent.  Definite
efforts to develop the country's fish resources date back to the Second
World War when because of the blockade of the high seas it was decided to
develop local resources including fisheries. A fishery office was established
in 1941. Initially, attention was given to brackish-water fish culture, canoe
fishing and small-motor fishing craft for lagoon and creek fishing. Fish
farming was also promoted and the Panyam fish farm was established in 1951.
From the mid-1950s to mid-1960s the coastal trawler fleet was expanded (one
in 1956, and 13 in 1960) and the catch increased, but declined following heavy
exploitation. However, commercial quantities of prawns were discovered
in eastern areas and many vessels were converted for prawn fishing. By 1970
the fishing industry had recovered and expansion is so rapid that plans are
being made to regulate fishery.
19.       Forestry.  Involvement in forestry dates back to the beginning of
the century when officials became worried about uncontrolled forest des-
truction. A forest law was passed in 1908 under which forest reserves could
be established. The aim of the lawmakers was to acquire 25% of the country,
but actually declared reserves at present do not amount to more than 10%
of total area. Details are in Supplement 5. Forestry passed through several
stages: exploitation without replacement; plantations of exotic species;
natural generation; and finally plantation forestry.
1/   The Federal Department of Livestock (FDL) is now headquartered in Kaduna.



SUPPLEENT I
Page 6
Resources
20.       The resources for further agricultural development are available,
but changes will be required. For example, labor is available and it is
estimated that up to 90% of all farm operations are manual. However, labor
availability might be critical at certain periods of the year and intermediate
mechanization and technology could almost certainly be applied to relieve
labor shortages at peak periods. With rapidly increasing wage rates throughout
Nigeria, it is also important that returns to labor in agriculture increase to
avoid wide disparity between the agricultural and other sectors. Land is also
an abundant resource and it is estimated that about 75% of the total land area
could be cultivated. On that basis, the land-population ratio is 1 ha per
person; however, there are large regional variations, the ratio being 0.2 ha
per person in the southeast and about 2.5 ha in the northeast. However, if the
present system of bush/fallow continues, land will be insufficient. River
systems and groundwater provide the water resource base, but these have not
been fully investigated or exploited. Capital is an abundant resource at
present, but it is important that savings be created-in the agricultural
sector itself to provide a source of continued investment. As in other
developing countries, management and entrepreneurship are scarce resources.
Development Potential
21.       There is little controversy about Nigeria's development strategy:
the importance of agriculture and rural development to the future growth
prospects of the country is now generally recognized. Equally noncontro-
versial is the potential of the agricultural sector in terms of available
land for future development, room for improved productivity and yields through
new technology improvements, and the size of the market for both food and cash
crops. However, institutions and available manpower are inadequate compared
with the needs and potential of the sector. There is therefore a role for
external institutions to assist governments in preparing and implementing
rural development projects and in agricultural research and manpower training.
The Bank has increased its assistance in this area.
22.       The Third Five-Year Development Plan covers the period 1975-1980.
Total plan allocation amounted to N 32 million but the effective program is
set at N 20 million due to expected substantial slippage. Public sector
investment in agriculture is aimed primarily at providing the infrastructure
and incentives necessary, particularly for smallholder development. Growth
in agriculture is set at 5%, about the same rate of growth as achieved
in the 1950s and early 1960s but considerably higher than recent growth rates.
Planned direct public investment for agriculture is about US$4 billion and
includes the development of several major river basins for large-scale as
well as smallholder farming; an accelerated foodcrop development program
aimed at adopting and disseminating modern farming techniques to smallholder
farmers; programs to improve input distribution and marketing of food and
export crops; and the several agricultural development projects with which
the Bank is associated. While the allocation is smaller than for other



SUPPLEMENT 1
Page 7
sectors (6-7% of total) it represents a sevenfold increase over the previous
plan allocation and might be rather ambitious in view of the low technology
and smallholder nature of Nigerian agriculture and the institutional and man-
power constraints. In addition there is a program to promote rural develop-
ment mainly based on complementary investments in infrastructure and social
services. It includes rural electrification of 500 towns and villages, and
construction of 20,000 km of secondary and feeder roads, including 6,000 km to
open up inaccessible areas; a large portion of health and other social services
is also planned for the rural sector.
23.       Government is acutely aware of the need tQ improve performance in
the agricultural sector. This is demonstrated by numerous measures which
are continuously announced. Although the effectiveness and scope of some
measures are open to dispute, they do reveal Government's concern. Recently,
Government initiated "Operation Feed the Nation" to increase food production.
The program has no specific planning and organization base, and it is no more
than an ad hoc collection of measures. The most important is an increase in
fertilizer subsidy. New prices for a 50 kg bag of fertilizer throughout
Nigeria have been set at between N 1.00 to N 2.00, which amounts to an 80%
subsidy. The subsidy is unnecessarily high and is bound to create misalloca-
tion of fertilizer. However, fertilizer subsidies will not create a fiscal
burden on Nigeria in the foreseable future, and can generally be supported by
the need to transfer oil revenues to the rural sector. The marketing board
system has also been completely overhauled (see Supplement 11) and floor
prices for most crops have been announced recently. What is missing, however,
is a consistent framework for agricultural incentives, and the Federal Ministry
of Agriculture and Rural Development (FMARD) has appointed consultants to
study agricultural incentives and make recommendations.
Bank Operations
24.       Since 1971 a total of ten projects in the agricultural sector have
been approved; they are listed in Table 5. Following the end of the Civil
War, a sector survey was undertaken and a first cocoa project was approved
in 1971. This was followed by a second cocoa project. in 1974. The cocoa
projects are working well and are completely under Nigerian management. The
first cocoa project is completely disbursed. Some reorganization of the
second cocoa project is required, since new States have been created and
the Cocoa Development Unit of the former Western State is now divided among
three States. Substantial project preparation work was undertaken by the Bank
in the early seventies. In 1974 a livestock project, a rice project and three
agricultural development projects were approved. The latter three Northern
Agricultural Development Projects are now being implemented successfully and
are models for similar projects in and outside Nigeria. The rice and livestock
projects were slow to start, but are now operational.



SUPPLEMENT I
Page 8
25.       Three oil palm projects were approved in 1975.  There were long
delays in getting the loans signed; however, operations have already started,
and two of the three projects are under satisfactory management.
26.       An irrigation project and rubber project have been appraised and
negotiated. However Bank involvement in those projects is no longer con-
templated, since FMG has withdrawn the irrigation scheme and since increased
costs made the rubber project no longer viable in the form as appraised.
27.       Monitoring and evaluation is an important component of all projects.
A unit has been established in Kaduna for the agricultural projects; this is
working well and is being expanded under the proposed project. A unit has
also been established in Benin for the tree crop projects but is not completely
staffed. The rice project has a small evaluation and second phase study
component, but is not yet formally part of the evaluation unit "network." In
addition, as part of project supervision, the Bank is engaged in a continuous
dialogue with the Nigerian Government regarding agricultural policy.

SUPPLEMENT 2
NIGERIA
CENTRAL AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECTS
CROP DEVELOPMENT
A. General
1.        The project areas lie in the transitional zone between the south-
ern root and tree crop and the northern cereal and livestock farming systems.
The relatively long rainy season, 1/ April to October, followed by a pro-
longed dry period from November through March (hardly any precipitation is
received between mid-November and mid-March), allows a diversity of food
crops to be grown along with some tree crops such as citrus and mangoes.
2.        Crop production exploits these ecological conditions by growing
such long-duration and semiperennial crops as yams, cassava, cocoyams and
pigeon peas; medium-duration crops such as local rice and sorghums; and
short-duration crops such as maize, millet, cowpeas, groundnuts, benniseed
and melons.
3.        While yams usually take first place in the cropping system after
clearing from a long fallow period, cassava is usually planted as a last crop
after 3-4 years of cropping and before land is reverted to bush/fallow for
regeneration.
4.        In between the yam and cassava crops, a system of undersowing,
relay cropping and intercropping is practiced, depending primarily on the
farmer's priority and to some extent on the fertility of the soil. The
"compound" farm which receives all the household wastes and is of adequate
fertility at all times is usually planted to maize (and sometimes early millet
in the Lafia area) followed by tall sorghum. Also within the compound farms
can be found such crops as cocoyams, watermelons, groundnuts and cowpeas
(the leaves of which are used as vegetable). Compound farms are rarely
fallowed.
5.        "Bush" farms or farms away from compounds where nutrient status
declines with each year of cropping tend to follow a loose pattern such
as yams followed by early maize undersown with tall sorghum and/or pigeon
peas. This is then followed by early millet or cowpeas, followed by tall
sorghum or late maize. Sometimes when the fertility of the soil is con-
sidered adequate another yam crop is sandwiched into the second and third
1/   Rainfall data are summarized in the appraisal reports, but
rainfall charts for various towns are presented in the charts
attached to this supplement under Appendix 6.



SUPPLEMENT 2
Page 2
crops; in such a case the yam mounds are made in the standing sorghum plants
which are panicle harvested, leaving the sorghum stalks to act as stakes for
the yam. Cassava is then planted and before the field is allowed to fallow a
second crop of cassava may be taken if the tubers from the first crop indicate
that another crop is possible. Within this broad pattern are a number of
combinations involving other crops such as melons, groundnuts, bambara nuts
and benniseed.
6.        The system shows such flexibility that with ample supply of ferti-
lizer the pattern is likely to be modified significantly, since fertility
limitations might be substantially reduced. This may be particularly true
in the case of yams which dominate the agricultural economy of both areas and
serve both as a cash crop and as staple food.
7.        Rice is important in the alluvial flood plain soils of the main
streams and in the heavier soils of flat lands bordering the main rivers.
Since rice soils are generally not suitable for other crops it is grown as
a sole crop in these areas.
8.        Few farm surveys have been undertaken in recent years in the pro-
ject areas, and this makes estimating crop acreages in such a complex system
rather difficult; the figures should be interpreted as such.
9.        Plant population densities are generally low, probably the result
of a combination of factors such as low soil fertility, high soil permea-
bility, the practice of intercropping and the tools and implements used in
farming. However, with an abundance of land and a limitation in labor, the
farm tools have been designed to allow for a rapid coverage of a relatively
wide area per man unit of time.
10.       Production is primarily oriented towards subsistence security;
poor soil conditions, seasonal vagaries and low average incomes combine to
create an environment in which technological adventure is undertaken only
when proved to yield significant economic returns.
B. Agronomic Aspects
11.       The importance and ecological requirements of the crops selected
for special emphasis in the projects are briefly described here and an
assessment made of the development potential for each of the crops. While
certain agronomic practices are advocated here, it must be realized that
because the project areas lie between the two main ecological zones of the
country where most research is carried out, these recommendations, extra-
polated from research results at the Institute of Agricultural Research (IAR)
Samaru, and the International Institute of Tropical Agriculture (IITA) Ibadan,
will have to be modified in light of future findings under the projects' ap-
plied applied research program.



SUPPLEMENT 2
Page 3
WHITE YAM (Diosconea rotundata oPoir")
12.       The white yam is grown in all zones and is the most important cash
crop in the area, although low soil fertility limits its proportionate area in
the rotation.
13.       Ecology.  Yam prefers fairly hot (30 C) temperatures with a long
(7-8 month) rainy season giving 1,100-2,000 mm of rain. Soils should be per-
meable, sandy loams with reasonable fertility and for this reason the yam
is usually grown as a first crop in the rotation.
14.       Current Practices.  The crop is grown on mounds usually constructed
from September to November which, however, could be extended to January either
on newly opened land or under a growing sorghum or pigeon pea crop. Planting
is by setts sectioned from harvested tubers, or from seed tubers that have
developed following careful harvesting of an earlier crop. Planting extends
from December to January, and the mounds are normally protected by capping
with vegetative mulch.
15.       The vine sprouts and develops with the onset of the rains but
the plant relies on food stored in the sett for the first month or two after
sprouting. Although the vine is often allowed to trail on the ground, higher
yields are obtained if trained to climb on stakes. Since staking for a
large number of vines is not an easy task, farmers often use standing crops
such as maize interplanted in the yam field, or sorghum stalks which have
been panicle harvested the previous year, or pruned pigeon pea stands of
the previous year. Weeding is important and is often combined with reshaping
of mounds washed down by rain.
16.       Some harvesting is carried out in August and continues until Octo-
ber. During this early harvesting, tubers are carefully cut and removed,
leaving the crown and vine undisturbed, from which will grow a second, smal-
ler tuber. The main harvest period, however, is during November and Decem-
ber following the drying and dying back of the vines. The tubers are stored,
either lying or standing on end, in airy thatched shelters. They are suscep-
tible to high storage losses-due to drying out, sprouting and decay, which
may amount to an overall weight loss exceeding 30% over a long storage per-
iod. Average losses for the project have been estimated at 20%.
17.       Pests and Diseases.  The major pest is the yam beetle (Heteroligus
meles) which bores into the tuber, damaging the growing point and reducing
tuber size.  Nematodes (mainly Scutellonema bradys) are serious pests which
reduce yields and allow entry of fungi which reduce the value and keeping
quality of tubers. The nematode problem is aggravated by using already in-
fested tubers as planting material. Dieback, a disease causing premature
drying of tubers and loss of yield of up to 60%, is associated with the in-
cidence of nematodes. "Shoestring" can also reduce yield by up to 50%; the
causal agent is unidentified at present but the disease is less common than
dieback.



SUPPLEMENT 2
Page 4
18.       Development Potential.  Existing yam yields can be more than doubled
by the simple application of nitrogenous fertilizer to the'tune of about
50 kg of elemental nitrogen per hectare, staking the vines, and introducing a
number of improved husbandry practices.  In the first place, one of the main
drawbacks to increased production is the low plant population; field observa-
tion shows a total of 5,000-7,000 mounds per hectare, and this can be increased
to 10,000 mounds per hectare without adversely affecting yields. Competition
among yam plants is more for light than for soil nutrients since the crop
usually occupies the more fertile fields in the rotation. A program aimed at
getting farmers to provide adequate staking should be accompanied by a higher
plant density than presently practiced. Experience in IITA shows that very
large tubers are not only prone to harvesting, handling and storage injuries,
but are more fibrous than medium-sized yams.
19.       Considerable research attention has been given to the yam crop at
IITA and the prospects for substantial production improvements are high.
Crops have been successfully prepared from vine cuttings and yam seeds; the
latter offer immediate opportunities for the plant breeder while both allow
high yielding crops to be grown without risking heavy and expensive demands on
yam yield. Breeding attention is being directed towards a range of objectives
including yield, disease resistance and growth habits to reduce the need for
staking; yields already achieved at IITA have reached 90 tons per hectare.
Within the next three years IITA confidently expects to have produced a number
of stable high yielding lines with production technology suitable for dissemi-
nation to small farmers.
20.       Although the prospects for advanced technology are high, production
forecasts under the projects are based on existing technology with which
yield improvements of up to 300% over existing levels are possible. This
would be achieved by higher planting rates, closer spacing, efficient staking
and application of muriate and potash.
21.       Two levels of technology are proposed:
Traditional              Improved
a)   Planting material        Yam setts                Yam setts
b)   Plant population            6,000                   8,000
c)   Staking                                            Staking
d)   Fertilizer                 nil                     200 kg CAN
50 kg MP
e)   Insect control             nil                     Seed dressing
Aldrin 2.5%
f)   Anticipated yields         6,000 kg/ha             13,000 kg/ha
g)   Less setts                  1,500 kg                2,500 kg
h)   Net yield                  4,500 kg                10,500 kg



SUPPLEMENT 2
Page 5
CASSAVA (Manihot esculanta: crantz or M. utilissima. Pohl)
22.       In common with most humid tropical areas, cassava has assumed a
very important role in the farming systems and diet of Nigerians, largely
because of its ability to utilize the minute quantities of nutrients left
in the soil after a number of years of cropping, and because it stores
very well in the field and when processed as gari or flour.
23.       Ecology.  Cassava does well on most soils that are deep, well-
drained and not too stony. It is adaptable to a wide range of climatic
conditions and has a high tolerance to drought once established, although
in drier areas it requires a longer period to produce acceptable tubers.
24.       Current Practices.  Because of its capacity to produce a crop
from exhausted soils unsuitable for the production of-other crops and its
ability to compete successfully with weeds once established, cassava is
usually the last crop in the rotation before leaving the land to fallow.
The crop is established from stem cuttings and may be interplanted with
an early crop if soil fertility allows. One or two weedings are essential
during the early stages of crop growth, after which no weeding is done.
Harvesting normally commences about nine months after planting but this
varies according to plant growth and consumption requirements. If the first
crop is good, a second crop may be planted before allowing the land to revert
to bush/fallow.
25.       Pests and Diseases.  The main pest is the grasshopper (Zonocerus
variegatus) which causes severe damage when the young gregarious instars
eat the cassava leaves. Damage is most severe during January to April, as
the crop has little moisture to grow on and recovers very slowly. Mosaic,
which is transmitted mainly by the whitefly (Bemisia tabaci), is widespread
in the area and can cause yield reductions of up to 50% if plants are in-
fected at an early stage. Bacterial blight is a more recently recognized
disease which causes necrotic spots on leaves which spread, coalesce and
lead to defoliation, dieback, stunting or death. Some varieties are more
resistant than others and disease occurence appears to be associated with
mosaic.
26.       Development Potential.  Work ac IITA has produced improved and
disease tolerant varieties capable of yielding at least 15,000 kg per hec-
tare of tuber under present farming practices, and more than 40,000 kg with
fertilizer application on station plots. The present yields of 4,000 kg
per hectare can therefore be raised to 10,000 kg per hectare by simple mul-
tiplication and distribution of improved planting material, and for ad-
vanced farmers up to about 15,000 kg by using fertilizer and closer spac-
ing (1 m x 1 m). Because of cassava's high demand for potash and the low
general fertility of the soils, an application rate of roughly 50-45-90 of
NPK is recommended for achieving a high level of production by the advanced
farmers.



SUPPLEMENT 2
Page 6
27.       There should therefore be three levels of technology in the pro-
ject areas as follows:
Traditional         Improved             Advanced
a)   Planting          Locally produced   Resistant cuttings  Resistant cuttings
cuttings
b)   Plant population          6,500              6,500                10,000
c)   Fertilizer                 nil                nil,             200 kg CAN
-               100 kg TSP
150 kg MP
d)   Anticipated yields        5,000 kg          10,000 kg            15,000 kg
RICE (Orvza Sativa. L)
28.       Rice is grown mainly on the alluvial flood plains of the main
streams and rivers and on the low-lying fadamas which become inundated at
the height of the rains in August-September. Some upland rice is also
grown on flat heavy soils with impeded drainage, and most marketed rice
contains a proportion of red grained Or7za glaberrima which is still
grown, particularly on the uplands.
29.       Ecology.  Rice normally prefers warm temperatures (20-30 C), a
lot of sunshine and inundation of the soil over much of its growing period.
Varieties exist, however, and have been selected to suit a wide range of
ecological conditions.
30.       Current Practices.  Land preparation usually starts between May
and June. The land is cleanly hoed and all weeds removed by hand. A fine
tilth is achieved by breaking up clods, and even where land is plowed and
harrowed mechanically, farmers usually finish off seed bed preparation by
hand hoe. Planting is usually carried out by dibbling (making a hole with
the big toe, dropping in a number of seeds and then covering up the hole
again by foot), or by broadcasting rather thinly (about 25 kg/ha) and cover-
ing up seeds lightly by foot. Local varieties are generally red grained
(O. glaberrima Steud) but, through the National Accelerated Food Production
Program (NAFPP), improved varieties such as IR 20, FARO 15, TOS 78 and
TOS 42 for irrigated and fadama areas, and OS 6 and FARO 56/30 for uplands
are rapidly replacing the local varieties. One or two hand weedings are
undertaken during early growth stages but bird scaring is essential for
about four to six weeks from grain setting to harvesting, depending on



SUPPLEMENT 2
Page 7
maturation period. At harvest, the rice is tied into bundles and stacked
in a central area for drying and threshing. Surplus over domestic needs
is generally sold as paddy immediately after harvest. Parboiling before
commercial milling is almost universal.
31.       Pests and Diseses.  Stemborers, termites and army worms are com-
mon though damage is not extensive except on variety OS 6, which is suscep-
tible to stemborers. Rodent and bird damage is important and is reduced
by trapping and scaring. "Blast," particularly neck blast, has been reported
in the area on some of the improved varieties. Other fungal diseases such as
sheath blight and brown spot have also been observed although damage is not
serious.
32.       Development Potential.  As a result of the NAFPP, it should be
possible to assess easily the potential of the various varietes within each
ecological area and to reduce the number of varieties in the kit for multi-
plication for farmers. There is, however, room for improvement in the fer-
tilizer application rates and times. For swamp and irrigated rice, it is
likely that the NAFPP recommended rate of 80-30-30 NPK may be too high
in nitrogen, resulting in increased susceptibility to leaf diseases, while
for both upland and swamp it would be advantageous to split the nitrogen top
dressing to two applications, one after each hand weeding, i.e., about three
and six weeks after sowing respectively. Phosphate fertilizer application
can also be considered at the time when the final tilth is made just prior
to planting.
33.       With fertilizer application it should be possible to increase plant
population, particularly in the fadamas and irrigated areas, since rice is
sown direct and transplanting is not practiced. For uplands, where there is
a possibility of water stress during the middle of the season, the recom-
mended rate of 45 kg/ha should be satisfactory. However, for the fadamas
which receive seepage water from about July to September, increasing seed
rate should not pose serious problems of water stress in the crop and would
also help in the suppression of weeds.
34.       With work in progress at IAR on ULV (ultra low volume) spraying of
herbicides, it would be worth examining the use of such equipment for weed
control in rice. If successful, this would markedly reduce the present
high labor requirements for both weeding and planting, the latter by elimi-
nating the need of dibbling in straight lines at 20 cm apart.
35.       Although seed dressing for rice is not essential where direct
sowing is carried out, it is advisable to encourage seed dressing in the
project area since most of the seed is produced by farmers and seed dress-
ing would help in cases of low seed vigor.
36.       Levels of technology anticipated in the project areas are as
follows:



SUPPLEMENT 2
Page 8
Traditional         Improved              Advanced
(mainly fadamas)
a)   Planting         Local            Improved                Improved
material
b)   Seed rate       25-30 kg/ha        45 kg/ha            70 kg or more/ha
c)   Fertilizer         nil             100 kg CAN          200 kg CAN
50 kg TSP           75 kg TSP
50 kg MP
d)   Cultural       One weeding        Two weedings         2-3 weedings
practices
e)   Seed treatment    nil             Aldrex T 0.15 kg   Aldrex T 0.25 kg
f)   Yield             800 kg            1,800 kg           2,500 kg
MAIZE (Zea Mays L)
37.       Maize is the most important cereal crop and is grown both as an
early and a late crop.
38.       Ecology.  Although maize is grown on different soils and under a
wide range of climatic conditions, seedling development is adversely affected
by high soil temperatures. At the time of planting in April/May, soil tem-
perature in the project areas can be as high as 40 C and this affects the
germination and seedling vigor of the present available improved varieties,
the parents of which have been developed in warm temperate conditions where
soil temeratures at planting time are 30 C or lower. The problem is now re-
ceiving considerable research attention resulting in rather frequent changes
in recommended improved varieties.
39.       Current Practices.  The early crop is normally planted in April-
May, and harvested in July-August. Planting is usually on ridges in both
the compound and the bush farm. In the latter case it usually follows the
yam crop. Plant population is low (16,000-20,000 plants/ha), most probably
because it is usually undersown with sorghum before harvesting. The se-
cond season crop is usually planted in August and harvested in November.
It usually follows an early season crop like millet, groundnuts or melon.
40.       Pests and Diseases.  Major pest problems are caused by stemborers
(Eldana sacharina, Busseola fusca and Sesamia spp.) and grain weevils (Stio-
philus spp.), the latter occurring in both field and stores. Diseases in-
clude rust (Puccinia polysora), blight (Helminthosporium maydis), maize
streak virus, grey ear rot and downy mildew. Striga hermonthica is a common
weed pest.



SUPPLEMENT 2
Page 9
41.       Development Potential.  Present improved varieties of the TZA and
TZB crosses have been giving average yields of over 4,500 kg/ha in country-
wide trials. However, these trial results are from sole cropped maize
planted at high population densites which are not likely to be suitable for
the type of system adopted for the early crop. However, with the use of these
varieties and fertilization, accompanied by good cultural practices, the low
population densities might give rise to a high percentage of plants with two
cobs and would therefore increase yields significantly. Higher plant popula-
tion can be advocated for the second crop and this is likely to be accepted.
42.       Yields of the second crop are unlikely to reach the high national
average because although the seedlings would have the advantage of starting
in cooler soils they have the big disadvantage of growing in a period of
overcast skies and low radiation, and the crop is usually heavily attacked
by stemborers. A compromise would be to grow 120-day varieties which would
be planted in mid-July (i.e., immediately after harvesting the earliest
planted short duration crops) to enjoy 2-3 weeks of growth before adverse
weather conditions occur. Existing yields are estimated at 700-800 kg per
hectare for the early crop and 600 kg for the late crop. With seed dressing
and basic application of 75 kg of triple superphosphate and 25 kg of muriate
of potash followed by a top dressing of 100 kg of calcium ammonium nitrate, it
is estimated that yields of the early crops can be raised to 1,750 kg/ha while
with the same treatment and increased plant population (about 30,000 plants/ha)
yields of the second crop can be increased to about the same level as that of
the early crop. Experience in many parts of West Africa indicates that sulfur
is an essential chemical for upgrading crop development, especially for grain
crops. Because of this, some countries will not change from the use of
ammonium sulfate to other nonacidifying fertilizers. In Nigeria, however,
Goverament has decided to replace ammonium sulfate with calcium ammonium
nitrate. Should it be found in the project and by field trials that the
fertilizer recommendations in this report fall short of the sulfur needs for
optimum yields, it is recommended that provision should be made for sulfur
application, particularly to the grain crops, at a rate of about 15 kg per
hectare.
43.       Levels of technology anticipated within the project areas are:

WATERNELON (Citrullus Lantanus (Thunb) Mansf)
44.       Although grown in most parts of the world for its juicy pulp, in
Nigeria it is valued for its seeds, which are dried, dehusked, and either
ground for making soup or chewed as confectionery. It is an important cash
crop particularly in the Ayangba area, where it is said to be replacing
early millet in the rotation.
45.       Ecology.  Watermelons grow under a wide range of climatic and soil
conditions from the tropics to the subtropics, but prefer sandy, free-drain-
ing soils and do not do well with too much shading.
46.       Current Practices.  Watermelons are usually grown as an early crop
planted both around the homestead or in the bush farms. They may be planted
on the flat around the compounds or in ridges in the bush farms. Three to
four seeds, later thinned to one or two seedlings, are planted at very wide
spacing of between 3 m x 2 m and 3 m x 3 m. Very little cultivation is done
since the crop establishes itself very rapidly and provides a good ground
cover which reduces weed growth. Fruits are usually harvested early, about
3-4 months after planting, chopped and left to rot. The seeds are then
washed and dried. A few fruit may be left in the field to grow larger and
allow the pulp to ripen.



SUPPLEMENT 2
Page 11
47.       Pests and Diseases.  Varieties grown in Nigeria do not seem to
suffer much from the number of diseases and pests reported elsewhere such
as mosaic virus, fusavium wilt (Fusarium oxysorum schlecht), nematodes
(Meloidogyne spp.) and cucumber beetles (Diabrotica spp. and Acalymma tri-
vittata Maun).
48.       Development Potential.  The hardiness of the crop and its impor-
tance in Nigeria's agriculture has become so evident that research into find-
ing higher yielding cultivars has begun. It is reported by IITA that five
promising cultivars have been selected since work started on the crop two
years ago. Spacing trials are also in progress and yields of 1,750 kg/ha
of seed have recently been reported. No conclusive fertilizer trials have
so far been reported, but because of the poor soils of the project area some
nitrogen would help increase yields. Present yield in the area is estimated
at about 300 kg/ha of dried seed. With slightly closer spacing and fertili-
zer application, and using improved cultivars from IITA, it may be possible
to double this yield.
49.       The levels of farming expected in the project will therefore be:
Traditional                Improved
a)   Seed                 Local                Selections from IITA
b)   Seed rate             1 kg                       1.5 kg
c)   Fertilizer            nil                   100 kg CAN
d)   Yield                350 kg/ha              700 kg/ha
SORGHUM (Sorghum bicolar (L) Moench)
50.       Sorghum, generally known as guinea corn in West Africa, is another
very important cereal crop in the project area. Traditional sorghum is us-
ually undersown within an early crop and is important both for food and for
brewing local beer, while the tall stalks are used for building and fencing
and sometimes for staking yams.
51.       Ecology.  Sorghum is a crop for warm to hot tropical areas.  It
does not thrive under very humid conditions, but is very drought resistant.
Local varieties are long duration, with a growing period of about five
months. Local sorghum is photosensitive and matures in the dry season on
residual soil moisture.
52.       Current Practices.  Tall sorghum is planted in June/July and is
nearly always undersown within an early millet or maize crop. Plant popu-
lations are low (about 10,000-15,000 plants/ha) and the crop is commonly in-
terplanted, mostly with cowpea. Sorghum is tolerant to interplanting and it



SUPPLEMENT 2
Page 12
is unlikely that yield of the widely-spaced traditional crop is adversely
affected by the presence of the intercrop. Sole-planted sorghum is usually
designated for yams in the following season and mounds will be prepared
within the standing sorghum crop during October and November. Farmers grow
no dwarf sorghum and the results from a few trial plots planted have been
disappointing, mainly because of fungal diseases of the panicle.
53.       Pests and Diseases.  Stemborers (mainly Busseola fusca, but also
Sesamia spp.) are very common but appear to do little damage to the thick-
stemmed local varieties. Sorghum shoot fly can be troublesome and is best
avoided by early planting. Sorghum midge may cause some yield loss and
bird damage is a problem at flowering. Covered smut and loose smut are en-
demic and cause considerable crop loss but both can be controlled by seed
dressing. In addition to pests and diseases, striga, a common weed, causes
significant reductions in yield.
54.       Development Potential.  While higher-yielding, short-straw vari-
eties have been developed for the more northern areas of Nigeria, these have
yet to be successfully integrated into the farming systems and climatic con-
ditions of the project area. Although plant population is very low, since
the crop matures with residual soil moisture, plant population may be in-
creased only slightly because of the free-draining nature of the soils in the
project areas. The crop has, however, been found to benefit from fertilizer
application, and interviews with farmers indicated that sorghum came third,
after yams and maize, in terms of their priorities in fertilizer application
to crops.
55.       Existing yields are estimated at 600 kg/ha.  With seed dressing
and fertilizer application of a basal dressing of 50 kg of triple super-
phosphate and a split application with 100 kg of calcium ammonium nitrate,
yields can be increased to 1,200 kg/ha.
56.       The levels of farming expected in the project areas are as fol-
lows:

b)   Seed rate                                 5 kg                 8 kg

COWPEA (Vigna Unguiculata (L) Sari ex Hassk)
57.       The cowpea is a very important legume crop in the project areas
where it is grown both for its leaves, which are used as a vegetable, and
for its seeds. It is generally planted as a mixed crop but sole cropping
on bush farms is not uncommon. It is grown either as an early or late crop.
58.       Ecology.  Cowpea prefers warm climates with moderate rainfall
during the growing season and free-draining soils. It does well on moder-
ately rich soils but on soils that are too rich it runs to leaf instead of
seed.
59.       Current Practices.  The crop is generally grown as an intercrop
and though sometimes planted early, it is usually planted as a late crop.
Intercropping is preferred because it offers some protection against in-
sect attack. As a late crop, it is planted around August, and harvesting
would be around December. This is because local varieties flower unevenly
and ripen over a long period, requiring four to five pickings to complete
the harvest. Shelling is either by beating with sticks or by breaking pods
by hand.
60.       Pests and Diseases.  Leaf-eating beetles may cause serious defoli-
ation; thrips and leaf hoppers attack the plant at all stages and coreid
bugs suck from green pods, causing them to shrivel. The seed is attacked
both from the field and in storage by boring beetles and weevils. The root
knot nematode (Heterodera marioni) causes irregular swellings on the roots,
stunting the plant and reducing yield.



SUPPLEMENT 2
Page 14
61.       The most important disease is mosaic.  The vector for mosaic is
the beetle Otheca mutabilis. Other diseases are anthracnose, corynespora,
and cercospora leaf spot.
62.       Development Potential.  Strains showing some resistance to leaf
hoppers, thrips and pod borers have been isolated and are available. If
acceptable in the project area on grounds of taste and cooking qualities,
these should reduce the number of sprayings required to obtain high yields.
However, spraying is necessary for high yields and with the introduction of
ULV sprayers substantial yield improvements are now possible with compara-
tively less labor than with conventional sprayers. Current yields are es-
timated at 200-300 kg per hectare. With improved cultivars and spraying,
together with an application of 50 kg of calcium ammonium nitrate and 50 kg of
triple superphosphate, yields of 1,500 kg/ha would be possible.
63.       Since ULV sprays have to be introduced primarily for the devel-
opment of cowpeas, it would be necessary to encourage its production on
larger acreages than presently allocated for the crop. A possible method
of increasing acreage would be by encouraging intercropping with cassava,
tall sorghum or late maize. In the first instance, fertilizers applied to
maize or sorghum should benefit the cowpea and thereby demonstrate the
need to apply fertilizer to the cowpea crop.
64.       The levels of technology expected in the project areas are as
follows:
Traditional          Improved
a)   Seed                                    Local           Insect-resistant
cultivars

c)   Fertilizer                              nil                  50 kg TSP
50 kg CAN

BENNISEED (Sesamun Indicum L)
65.       Once an important schedule crop in the project area, benniseed is
now grown mainly for home consumption and local trade. It is included in
the project proposals because of the present attractive minimum price and
its relatively low labor requirements. Farmers' views, gathered in Lafia
Division, indicate that with improved marketing and increased soil fertility,
the crop could become important once more.
66.       Ecology.  Well-drained light soils are preferred, and the crop
is tolerant of short periods of drought though excessive rainfall can be
damaging. Shading can be beneficial during establishment and early growth,
but good sunlight is necessary from flowering onwards. Temperatures of
25-300C give optimum growth.
67.       Current Practices.  Benniseed can be grown either as an early or
late crop, but is more frequently grown as a late crop, planted in August. In
most cases it is sole cropped though mixed cropping with sorghum is practiced.
It is also sometimes planted on early-formed yam mounds which are reshaped
after benniseed harvesting and planted to yams. Harvesting commences just
prior to the lower capsule splitting and the whole plant is cut, tied into
bundles and stooked, occasionally over sacking.
68.       Pests and Diseases.  The leaf roller (Antigastra catelaunalis) and
the gall midge (Asphondylia sesami) can cause severe yield losses, but timely
planting and vigorous growth will usually reduce their effects to an accep-
table level. The most important disease is leaf curl, which is carried by
whitefly (bemisia tabaci). Second season crops are not seriously affected,
however, and some resistance is found on hirsute leaves.
69.       Development Potential.  Recent trials with improved varieties in
the Lafia Division have given encouraging results of between 750 kg/ha and
900 kg/ha. With fertilizer application and improved marketing the crop is
likely to draw much greater farmer attention than it now enjoys. The crop,
particularly the late crop, is moreover attractive to farmers in having low
labor requirements except for threshing, which can be programmed to avoid
peaks.
70.       Levels of technology expected in the projects are as follows:
Traditional          Improved
a)   Seed                                    Local              Improved
b)   Seed rate                                5 kg                10 kg
c)   Fertilizer                              nil                100 kg CAN
50 kg TSP
d)   Yields                                 230 kg/ha           600 kg/ha

OTHER CROPS
71.       There are a number of other crops in the areas, some of which are
useful in the diet of the population, but which have limited potential for
development at the present time and would require further investigation be-
fore any sound development plans could be formulated.
72.       Millet (Pennisetum typhoides (Burm. f.) Stopf and Hobbard).  Bul-
rush millet is an important cereal grown both as an early and late crop,
though the early crop is more common in the project areas. Research work
on millet is concentrated on improved varieties for-the more northerly parts
of West Africa where it is one of the very few cereals that will grow under
conditions of very low rainfall. Although it is likely that some of the im-
proved varieties developed for the more northerly areas may be suitable for
the project areas, few adaptive trials with these varieties have been con-
ducted. The crop is known to extract substantial quantities of nitrogen from
the soil during early growth but its response to fertilizers has not been
accurately measured.
73.       Local yields are estimated at 400-450 kg per hectare. In Senegal
local yields, using improved varieties, have been reported as 850 kg/ha for
improved practices without the use of fertilizers, 1,100 kg/ha with moderate
fertilizer application and 1,700 kg/ha with recommended fertilizer application.
74.       The project will try to find high yielding varieties suitable for
the areas and it is felt that the local crop will benefit from the general
rise in soil fertility as a result of project activities.
75.       Bambara Nuts (Voandzeia subterranea (L) Thon).  Bambara nuts are
popular in the plateau area of Ayangba where they are said to have replaced
cotton. They are also an important crop in the Keana area of Lafia. The
crop does well on poor soils and is generally free of pests and diseases.
It is also likely to beneift from general raising of soil fertility as a
result of project activities.
76.       Cotton (Gossypium hirsutum).  Once a useful crop in the project
areas, cotton has declined rapidly in importance. The decline in production,
like benniseed, is blamed on poor marketing facilities as well as on low
economic returns from the crop. Efforts by MANR to revive the industry have
met with poor response from farmers. In the Lafia area, two years' stocks of
seed remain undistributed in MANR stores because farmers will not take them
although they are given out free. Under the circumstances, the project will
therefore not concentrate its activities on cotton development.
77.       Pigeon peas (Cajanus cajan (L) Mill sp.).  The pigeon pea is a use-
ful grain legume crop in certain areas of Ayangba. It is an important end-
of-rotation crop, often left in the field for about three years. Although
yields decline after the first harvest, the plant continues to be harvested



SUPPLEMENT 2
Page 17
until after the third year, when yam mounds may be made in between the stand-
ing crop. The plants are then pruned to act as stakes for yam vines. Be-
cause of its qualities as an enhancer of soil fertility, pigeon peas can be
encouraged as an end-of-rotation crop.
78.       Groundnuts (Arachis hypogaea L).  Although commonly found in the
project areas, groundnuts are not grown on a commercial scale. They are
grown mainly for home consumption though some are sold at local markets.
They are usually planted as intercrop with maize, cassava or millet. Eco-
logically the crop prefers warm temperatures with rainfall of 500-1,000 mm.
The project areas cannot therefore be regarded as good groundnut growing
areas. In the project areas where they are planted in April/May, ripening
takes place in dull overcast conditions followed by harvesting during heavy
rains.
79.       Since any program of improvement would require changing dates of
planting which may not be readily accepted by farmers because of the rela-
tive insignificance of the crop in the whole farming system, it is not re-
commended that the project devote too much attention to it. The crop should
also benefit by the general raising of soil fertility through project acti-
vities.
TREE CROPS
80.       Extensive wild oil palm groves are found in the Ayangba area where
large quantities of palm kernels are purchased by the Nigeria Produce MIar-
keting Corporation. In the Lafia Division scattered groves are found in the
valley bottoms. Small areas of coffee and cocoa are also found in the Ayangba
project area although scheduled purchases are insignificant. There is a
small but growing citrus industry and farmers are becoming interested in
growing some kola nuts.
81.       The Benue State Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources
maintains three nurseries in the Ayangba area, one each at Ankpa, Ochaja and
Idah, where seedlings of oil palm, cocoa, coffee, coconut, guava, citrus,
mango, kola and, more recently, cashew and avocado pears are raised. In
Lafia, emphasis has been placed on citrus seedlings, most of which have been
used to supply farmers from the Local Government Council (LGC) nursery near
Lafia town. The adjacent Plateau State Ministry of Agriculture and Natural
Resources nursery, started in 1972/73, is being expanded rapidly to meet
increasing demands and will supply an estimated 20,000 citrus seedlings to
farmers by 1978. This nursery has also started to raise kola nut seedlings
and it expects to distribute over 2,000 seedlings in 1977.
82.       Oil Palm (Elacis Suineensis Jacg).  Despite the prevalance of wild
oil palm the area is not well suited to intensive oil palm production. An-
nual rainfall is around 750 mm, which is less than the minimum requirements
for successful oil palm culture, and given the free draining characteristics
of the soil, considerable moisture stress would result during the dry season.
Although oil palm is not included as a major development component of the



SUPPLEMENT 2
Page 18
project, improved oil palm seedlings would be made available to farmers from
the project nurseries for planting around the homestead.
83.       Coffee (Coffee caenophora Pierre).  A few fields of robusta coffee
have been established in the area and although somewhat neglected the plants
look healthy and vigorous. W4hile the climate in the higher elevations is
quite satisfactory for robusta coffee, the plant requires deep, well-drained
loams with ample humus and nutrients, and there is only limited scope for ex-
panding its presently insignificant area. Moreover coffee is more difficult
to maintain in good productive condition and to harvest and process for mar-
keting than most of the other tree crops in West Africa.
84.       Coffee cannot, therefore, be considered as a major development com-
ponent though guidance on improved husbandry would be provided by the project.
Typically the established coffee is suffering from poor management; trees
are unpruned; gaps are not refilled; weeding is haphazard; little fertilizer
is applied; and the trees have gone into biennial bearing. To rejuvenate
the plots the farmers need to prune, quite drastically in places, apply fer-
tilizers and attend to weeding. Improved seedling should continue to be
sold to farmers as a service operation.
85.       Cocoa (Theobroma cacao).  Cocoa requires an annual rainfall of
1,250-3,800 mm and deep, well-drained sandy clay loams or clay loams with
high organic matter. Small plantations have been established in the area
but are not thriving on the sandy soils. The GRIN Station at Ochaja con-
siders the area unsuitable for expansion of the crop, though the nurseries
in Ayangba will continue to raise seedlings for sale to farmers.
86.       Cashew (Anacardium occidentale).  There are no established cashew
plantations in the area though occasional "wild" trees are found. The crop,
however, was considered as a development possibility since it tolerates a
wide range of rainfall and will grow well on poor soil, provided it is not
impermeable. The crop, also, has a low labor requirement and benefits from
annual crop cultivation during establishment. With the main harvest occur-
ing in February, there would be little clash with annual crop operations.
Seedling raising would be expanded in Ayangba project area and started in
Lafia project area for sale to farmers.
87.       Citrus.  The area has a small, but flourishing, citrus trade in
which orange predominates although grapefruit and tangerines are also grown.
Most trees are grown as a compound crop by farmers and are flourishing and
healthy with no symptoms of distortion, excess suckering or reversion to the
rootstock that characterizes citrus in a hostile locality. A number of ab-
sentee farmers and business men have started substantial acreages of citrus
plantations and it is likely that this will be an increasingly attractive
investment in the future.
88.       The sources of seed and seedlings were variable and the varieties
could not be identified. The oranges, however, were green with tough tight
skins, but juicy and sweet to eat. Though unattractive to the international



SUPPLEMENT 2
Page 19
market as a dessert crop, it sells very well in Nigeria and the fruit would
be suitable for processing, particularly juice extraction.
89.       A total lack of information on existing tree populations and
yields, or the scope and ramifications of the domestic market, prevents any
formalized planning for the area's citrus industry at this stage. Quali-
tatively there is almost certainly scope for market and production devel-
opment but quantification would require detailed appraisal by project man-
agement. The project should endeavor to provide good quality orange, man-
darin and lemon seedlings to farmers with the facilities to expand distri-
bution if suitable fruit disposal arrangements can be identified. Seedling
production in Ayangba would be improved and expanded while the expansion of
the two nurseries in Lafia would be undertaken.
90.       Other tree crops worth raising and distributing to farmers are
kola (Cola spp.), coconuts (Cocos nucifera), avocado (Persea americana Mill)
and mango (Mangifera indica L.).
C. Cropping Practices
91.       Sequential Cropping.  Due to the long growing season of over 200
days, a wide range of crops with differing growing periods are observed in
the area. These can be divided into full season crops with growing periods
that span all or most of the growing season, and short season crops for which
rainfed double cropping is possible.
92.       Short season crops can be early or late according to their posi-
tion in the growing sequence; this may be one of relay cropping, whereby a
late season crop is planted immediately after the harvest of an early season
crop, or one of undersowing, when sowing takes place before the harvest of
an early season crop. The latter practice is commonly observed in maize,
millet, and sorghum combinations, with the sorghum being planted in June
within the standing millet crop which is usually harvested in late July,
after which the sorghum crop grows to maturity for harvest in November.
93.       Mixed Cropping.  Superimposed on the pattern of sequential crop-
ping is the practice of mixed cropping, referring to regular interspacing of
two or more crops with the growing period of each crop nested within the
growing period of another. Mixed cropping is often looked down upon as re-
presenting low level technology, but has been shown to offer a number of
advantages:
(a) Certain crop mixtures enhance utilization of prime
growth factors giving increased physical yield per
hectare. This can result from differing factor
requirements of component crops or from peak fac-
tor requirements occurring at different times in
the growing season.



SUPPLEMENT 2
Page 20
(b) A more complete and longer-term soil cover is made
possible and thereby reduces the risk of erosion.
(c) It offers the farmer a degree of security since
adverse growing conditions are unlikely to exert
themselves to the same extent on each component
crop.
(d) Labor inputs are reduced by combining field oper-
ations common to the component crops.
(e) Crops susceptible to pest attack can be afforded
some protection if grown within a pest-resistent
cover crop.
94.       Mixed cropping is now receiving increasing professional attention
but no satisfactory methodology has yet been devised for its analysis.
Within both areas, however, the mathematical problems posed by crop mix-
tures are not severe, for while many plots are mixed in that they contain
more than one crop, a single dominant crop is in most cases readily ap-
parent, with admixtures occurring as scattered plantings or rogue crops,
neither of which is likely to exert any significant competing effect on
the dominant crop.
95.       Cropping Patterns.  Applying these structural definitions of
cropping sequences and crop mixtures to the model practices observed in
the project areas, the following much simplied picture emerges:
Mainly          Sole &          Mainly

SUPPLEMENT 2
