96.       Estimates of existing cropping patterns, made on the basis of plot
counts during preparation and appraisal, are included in Table 1. Each crop
is expressed as a sole crop or as a principal crop in a crop mixture; with
the exception of cowpeas, the areas (and yield estimates dealt with later
in this supplement) can be equated with sole crop equivalent areas, since
the competing effect of any intercrop is considered insignificant.  Cowpeas
are widely grown as a subsidiary admixture and the area recorded in Table 1
represents only that grown as a sole crop; no attempt has been made to es-
timate the individual areas of 'other' crops for which specific technical
improvement packages have not been included, nor has an estimate been
attempted of the sole-cropped equivalent areas of cowpeas and other sub-
sidiary admixtures.
D. Crop Economics and Development
97.       The main development components of both projects are centered on
removing or reducing the main productivity constraints presently faced by
farmers in the area. These components, which involve crop input distribu-
tion, research discrimination, and improved crop transport and market in-
frastructure, are expected to attract a substantial response from the farm-
ing community, but the extent to which the response would be directed to-
wards specific crops can only be controlled to a limited extent by the pro-
jects. New technology in crop production would change the relative impor-
tance of each individual crop, and a prediction of these likely changes
would depend on the extent to which farmer motivation and production con-
straints can be defined and the degree to which these can be satisfied by
the output of each crop in relation to competing demands and the farmer's
resources.
98.       Accurate analyses and predictions of this sort are constrained
by a data base which is generally inadequate to support mathematical spe-
cification of the rather complex farming system described above. Data
assembly, therefore, has had to rely heavily on information and experience
gained outside the project areas and on observation and primary data col-
lected by the preparation and appraisal mission.
99.       Crop Budgets.  Crop budgets are detailed in Appendix 1.  Physi-
cal budgets have been compiled from analyses of control and treated exten-
sion demonstration plots, modified in certain cases by field observation,
and from research results collected from IAR and IITA, downgraded to re-
present development potential under smallholder management. The levels
of crop inputs used are generally conservative, representing average
application rates that are likely to prove most attractive to the farming
community. Economic and financial translations of the physical budgets
have been made using the commodity price forecasts which are detailed in
Supplement 11. The economic budgets, which are based on the 1980 forecast
economic farmgate prices expressed in 1976 constant Naira, include a



SUPPLEMENT 2
Page 22
shadow price for labor of N 1.00 per manday;l/ the economic budgets have
been used as the basis for projecting the economic crop production bene-
fits of each project.
100.      Financial budgets calculated at full cost have been used to re-
present the returns per hectare and per manday in a situation of no Govern-
ment subsidies on purchased inputs, with market prices competitive with
those of the world market. Financial budgets at actual prices represent
existing Government subsidies on purchased inputs, guaranteed prices for
export crops and market distortions which have caused some domestic farngate
prices to differ substantially from those derived from world market prices.
101.      Labor Requirements.  Within Lafia, and to a lesser extent in
Ayangba, labor availability is generally a more important constraint on
holding size than land availability. Since the improved and advanced tech-
nical packages demand a substantially higher labor input than under exist-
ing (traditional) production, the impact of technical change on overall
cultivated area must be considered.
102.      Detailed monthly labor requirements for each crop are given in
Appendix 2; these have been compiled by reference to data collected within
and outside Nigeria. Large differences among sources were found, with
apparent discrepencies within sources, when comparing labor inputs for
similar operations with different crops. For all crop operations, manday
requirements inevitably contain a high degree of natural variation. Those
detailed in Appendix 2 have been drawn selectively from sources to repre-
sent as closely as possible the relative requirements of different crops;
all exclude the potential of mechanization for improving labor producti-
vity and include all operations necessary to convert the standing crop into
the marketable form used as the basis of farmgate price projections in Sup-
plement 11.
103.      Production Development.  Development analysis is undertaken on
the assumption that farmers behave in an economically rational manner, though
the environment of risk and uncertainly will influence both the range of ob-
jectives to be maximized and the rate of progress along the path to optimi-
zation.
104.      The influence of improved technology on response rate and crop-
ping patterns will be dictated by financial returns at actual prices; the
assumption has been made that Government intervention in produce and input
prices will not substantially change during the project development period.
Reference to the farm budgets shows that the proposed technical packages
substantially increase the returns per hectare of all the listed crops;
this is particularly so in the case of yam, improving its already dominant
position as a cash crop for the area. The ranking order of returns per
hectare does not change in the case of full season crops but those for
1/   The current rural wage rate is estimated at N 1.40.



SUPPLEMENT 2
Page 23
cowpeas, and to a lesser extent for late maize, improve substantially, while
early maize and tall sorghum decline.
105.      In view of the constraint of labor on holding size, return per
hectare may prove less important than return per manday. For the latter,
new technology substantially improves the ranking order of cowpeas and
cassava largely at the expense of early maize and tall sorghum. However,
in an area with a pronounced seasonal labor profile, returns per manday
during the main work peak in the profile are likely to exert a stronger
influence than returns to total mandays.
106.      Despite these considerations, the extent to which new technology
would influence cropping patterns is likely to prove rather limited during
a five-year development period. Most farmers still grow their subsistence
food requirements and the overall volume of marketed food crops would need
to increase substantially before specialization of production becomes com-
mon.
107.      Expected adoption rates of new technology for each crop have been
estimated by judging the improved return per hectare and per manday, and are
included in the production estimate of Appendices 3 and 4. These estimates
incorporate some expected changes in overall cropping patterns in each
area, and were made by considering the impact of increased labor require-
ments in an area where present holding size is largely dictated by labor
availability. Farm labor profiles have been calculated in Appendix 5 for
the 'average' farm in each project area, under existing technology. The
profiles reveal a peak labor demand during June mainly resulting from weeding
requirements. Since this peak is likely to serve as a continuing constraint
on total area cropped, a reduction in early season crops, which make high de-
mands on June labor for the least return, is expected to occur. Although it
would be to the benefit of project returns, no other shifts in cropping pat-
terns have been included except in Lafia Division, where advanced cowpea
production is expected to occur at the expense of sorghum production.
108.      A summary of expected cropping patterns at the end of the project
development period is included in Table 1. Production summaries based on
the working tables of Appendices 3 and 4, along with estimates of produc-
tion economic benefits, are contained in the individual appraisal reports
for each project.
109.      Crop budgets are in Appendix 1.  An average farm budget has been
calculated and is presented in Table 2. This is based on an average holding
size (2 ha in Ayangba and 2.5 ha in Lafia) and applies the average cropping
pattern 1/ of each area (see Table 1) to this hypothetical farm. 2/
1/   Other crops (for which no benefits have been calculated) had to
excluded.
2/   The same methodology was applied to determine farm labor pro-
files in Appendix 5.

SUPPLEXNT 3
NIGERIA
CENTRAL AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECTS
FARM DEVELOPMENT
A. Production Development
1.        The development projections of Supplement' 2, although substantial,
are largely the products of large project areas and the present marked dif-
ference between existing and potential yields. The proposed production
packages are not unduly sophisticated; farmers appreciate the use and value
of fertilizer, and expected crop yields of even the advanced budgets are
still more than 40% below those of research institutions.
2.        The immediate problem, therefore, is one of logistics.  Present
distribution of inputs is sporadic and insignificant despite demand which
in Lafia has been prepared to pay high prices for fertilizer traded on the
black market. Dissemination of technical information is likewise limited
but in any case has little relevance if the basic inputs for applying an
improved technology cannot be obtained. Present subsidy policy has largely
prevented development of private input trading and the disappointing per-
formance of Government in input procurement and technical dissemination
has led to farmers lacking confidence in Government's interest or ability to
help.
3.        A marked response can therefore by expected from the most basic
improvements in agricultural services; this is particularly so in the Lafia
area where substantial surplus production already exists which reflects a
strong commercial outlook by farmers and a willingness and ability to
respond to market incentives without Government assistance. However,
although the basic problems are simple, their solution is difficult, for
it involves contact with a very large number of small and widely dispersed
producers.
4.        Agricultural Services.  Agricultural production services fall into
two distinct categories which should be administered separately: (a) services
involving provision of farm inputs that should in the long-term be controlled
by non-Government agencies; these are essentially trading services; (b) tech-
nical (extension) and research functions that are primarily the responsibility
of Government and which, for effective use, demand a long-term Government
commitment. Both are linked, however, in that effective utilization of the
one requires the presence of the other while both are faced with the same
diseconomies of scale in dealing with small producers.



SUPPLEMENT 3
Page 2
5.        All agricultural services must also achieve a high level of
reliability and the farming community's confidence. Traditional farming
systems are the result of farmers' inherent knowledge of the environment
and their assessment of risk and uncertainty. New technology usually
increases production risks, for investment overheads have to be carried
and enterprises gradually become more dependent on other services in the
economy. Complete confidence in the latter must be achieved before farmers
will move along an irreversible path to new technology.
6.        To achieve this objective a network of multifunctional Farmers
Service Centers (FSC) would be created at which basic project field services
would be clustered and which would become reliable sources of technical
advice and trade inputs for farmers. FSC have been located 1/ primarily
to allow economies of scale in the input delivery system and each FSC serves
a hinterland of up to 15 km radius; a total of 20 are included for Lafia
and 30 for Ayangba. Proposals for commercial services development are dealt
with as a separate subject in the respective appraisal reports. FSC would
also form the field headquarters of the general extension service which would
provide the main communication link between project management and farmers.
7.        General Extension.  A general extension service would be provided
at the ratio of one Agricultural Assistant/Instructor to 500 farm families.
An Assistant Agricultural Superintendent would be in charge of each FSC
responsible to an Agricultural Superintendent in charge of 5 FSCs. Total
staff required (for further details see also Table 1) at full development
would be as follows:
-----Number---
Title                  Grade        Ayangba          Lafia
Senior Agricultural Officer        SAO            1              1
Agricultural Officer               AO             3               1
Agricultural Superintendent        AS             6               4
Assistant Agricultural
Superintendent                 AAS            30              20
Agricultural Assistant             AA            60             40
Agricultural Instructor            AI           240             80
8.        The initial task of the extension staff would be to compile an
inventory of villages and village farming populations 2/ and to define
geographical areas of responsibility; the traditional authority should be
fully involved in the program and their assistance and support should be
assured by explanation and discussion with staff at all levels. A dissemi-
nation network should then be created through village committees organized
1/   See project maps in the respective appraisal reports.
2/   This inventory will also be required for evaluation purposes.  Therefore
the Chief Evaluation Officer and the Head of the Crop Development Divi-
sion would work closely together so as to ensure that one inventory will
provide base data useful for dual purposes.



SUPPLEMENT 3
Page 3
with the assistance of village heads and through other assemblies such
as schools and religious institutions. The objective at this stage would
be to widely inform the rural population of the scope and objectives of
the project and to provide a fast and effective two-way channel of com-
munication between farmers and extension service.
9.        Group Formation.  During the initial stages of the project,
contact with farmers at the individual level will be necessary both for
technical dissemination and commercial supply. However, high operating
efficiency of both these services will be possible only with economies of
scale; an effective group structure among small farmers is therefore
essential.
10.       This need is recognized in Nigeria and has formed the basis of
Government's policy to create a network of rural cooperatives. However,
this program has had very limited success, largely because structures have
been imposed on the community and have frequently been too sophisticated
for the basic needs. of members while lacking the necessary business exper-
tise to undertake broader commercial operations. Successful group formation
of any type can only occur if farmers can easily identify the benefits of
membership.
11.       The successful formation of farmers councils in the Ayangba area
is largely a result of this. The councils are small and cohesive, serving
the simple basic needs of the farming community and carrying the minimum
of overheads; council members have the advantage of easier access to credit
and service provisions and have confidence in their organizers' genuine inter-
est in rural development. For the Ayangba project, the network provides
contact with around 10,000 farmers through 43 zonal committees and 625 indi-
vidual councils.
12.       This type of group structure would need to be expanded in Ayangba
and created in Lafia, and every opportunity should be taken to provide incen-
tives for group formation. Proposals are contained in the Commercial Services
Annex of the respective appraisal reports for providing incentives and special
services for bulked orders of inputs; credit provision and tractor hire
charges (see below) would also contain incentives for scale. General exten-
sion staff would be responsible for disseminating information on these incen-
tives and for explaining to farmers the importance of scale if they are to be
assured of the long-term viability of services. Priority on extension staff
time would also be given to groups and training courses and demonstration
visits for farmers would progressively be directed to groups or group repre-
sentatives.
13.       The projects would not attempt to take over existing groups, such
as the farmers councils in Ayangba, which have been formed independently
from Government. The objectives would be to provide efficient and effective
services to all project farmers and to give special attention to any form of
group structure which is well founded and provides economies of scale. From
these somewhat humble beginnings an important pathway would be laid for
developing advanced production and marketing organization; this subject is
further discussed in Section B of this supplement.



SUPPLEMENT 3
Page 4
14.       Feedback.  Effective extension requires sensitive understanding of
production problems and constraints faced by growers. As the main communica-
tion link between project and farmer, the general extension service has an
important rQle to play in feeding back information to management. Farmers'
reactions to new technology must be observed and reported, and used as the
basis for modifying research and work programs. Close liaison must also be
maintained with traditional leaders and all institutions involved with or in a
position to influence rural development.
15.       Support Services.  To obtain the necessary caliber of staff, an
intensive program of inservice training will be necessary; this would
be the responsibility of a separate Training Division within each project
which would also operate mobile audio-visual training units for the benefit
of farmers. Details of content and outturn of existing training institutions
in Nigeria, along with proposals for staff and farmer training, are contained
in Supplement 8.
B. Farm Managemen,t
16.       Crop production proposals contained in Supplement 2 are based on
average anticipated response rates to improved technology. During imolementa-
tion, however, it is expected that an elite of farmers and progressive small-
holder groups would emerge who would be suitable for application of more
advanced technology. Such groups would require specialist services outside of
the prime work routine of the general extension service, and a special Far=
Aavisory Division has been included in the project for this purpose. Advanced
farm development has been shown to have an important role to play in the three
Northern Agricultural Development Projects, 1/ both in spearheading technological
development beyond the level of simple husbandry improvement and in attracting
private capital into agriculture.
17.       In addition, there is growing concern within Nigeria at the drift of
labor from the land. Young men are becoming attracted away from agriculture's
drudgery to the expectation of an easier urban life, and the movement is
graining momentum against the background of Universal Primary Education (UPE).
Recent increases in the official daily minimum wage rate are an additional
factor limiting the availability of rural labor. The minimum wage rate was
less than R 1.00; it was increased to ' 1.75 following Udoji salary review
and is now established at 'N 2.10. To some extent the trend would be arrested
by the projects' crop development proposals which would substantially improve
returns per manday under existing methods of production; however, technology
to raise physical returns to labor will become increasingly important in
Nigeria; possibilities exist both through mechanizing and modifying the
existing cultivation systems.
1/   Funtua Agricultural Development Project (Loan 1092-UNI, 1975), Gusau
Agricultural Development Project (Loan 1099-UtI, 1975) and Gombe
Agricultural Development Project (Loan 1164-UNI, 1975).



SUPPLEMENT 3
Page 5
18.       Tractor Mechanization.  Labor productivity of small family farms
is not easily improved by tractor mechanization, this being witnessed by
existing Government tractor hire units which provide a highly subsidized
service achieving low output per tractor and covering an insignificant
portion of total cropped area. Output per tractor in the four tractor
hire units visited during appraisal ranged from a low of 12 ha per year
to a high of around 40 ha; although absorbing a large portion of extension
service time and operating costs, they are presently cultivating less than
0.005% of total cropped area.
19.       Demand from farmers for tractor services is presently very high.
To qualify for the service a minimum request for 2' ha cultivation is necessary
though small farmers can group to achieve this area, since it is not a condi-
tion that the area form one continguous block. A high proportion of time is
spent traveling to isolated holdings, and stumps and other hazards impair
field performance. In the Ayangba area, the Benue State Government is
attempting to ease these problems as part of a land development scheme in
which large blocks are being cleared manually at Government expense, the
contracts often being awarded to the farmers themselves. Apart from the
heavy demands on public funds, little consideration has been given to
problems of soil conservation or to the farming system which should be
applied.
20.       No clear policy exists on the contribution mechanization can make
gtowards raising overall productivity. At present, tractor hire services are
directed almost entirely towards land preparation whereas crop weeding
represents the main peak in the labor profile and no mechanical service
exists for this function. Timely planting and efficient weed control which
could be assisted by mechanical aids would provide real benefits. However,
in practice the existing service is so unreliable that potential benefits
are negated by delay in providing machinery.
21.       The scope for private ownership of tractors, which is essential
in the long term, is less than in the three Northern Agricultural Development
Projects, for the areas do not contain a significant number of large farmers
who would be able to support the heavy overhead and operating costs. Tractor
hire services therefore have a part to play in these areas but development
must be cautious and within the framework of clearly defined objectives.
22.       The projects would take over the existing tractor hire services (for
details see Table 2) in Ayangba and Lafia but expansion would be limited and
attention directed toward substantially improving tractor output with a long
term objective of transference to private ownership. Tractor output would be
increased both by improving servicing and operating efficiency and by provid-
ing a broader spectrum of operations throughout the farming year. All tractor
work would be primarily restricted to areas where sufficient land is available
in large and continugous units to permit viable operation. Initially the most
suitable locations are likely to be in the rice producing areas along fringes
of the major rivers' flood plains; these areas would be handed over entirely
to private contractors as and when they become available.



SUPPLEMENT 3
Page 6
23.       The tractor units would be deployed at Farmers Service Center
level to reduce transport times to contract points, and the present hire
charges on-an acreage basis would be replaced by hourly rates (for details see
Table 2); this would overcome the problem of area measurement when calculating
hire payments and would enable strict financial and operational control by
fitting service recorders for each tractor. Hourly hire rates, which would
include transport to and from service centers, would automatically provide a
strong incentive to farmers and farmer groups to (a) increase the area culti-
vated per contract so that the ratio of operational to nonoperational time
is improved, and (b) increase field size and remove obstructions so that
operational field efficiency is improved.
24.       Tractor hire services would be provided against cash payment only.
The projects would purchase additional tractors to provide the equivalent of
two tractor units per Farmers Service Center. Funds have been included for
trailers for transport services during harvesting and for a range of planting
and cultivation equipment, but purchase of the latter would be subject to
experience and recommendations emanating from the three Northern Agricultural
Development Projects. Regular servicing of tractors and machinery would be
carried out by a mobile maintenance unit.
25.       Credit would be provided by the project to assist farmers in land
clearing for tractor cultivation. No specific land clearing equipment is
included in project costs, but surplus capacity exists within the Engineering
Department of each project for subsoiling and ripping of roots and obstruc-
tions to the order of 800 ha over the development period; this could be pro-
vided at a charge to farmers of ; 30 per hectare, plus a delivery charge of
N 1.00 per km. Credit provision would be subject to a detailed farm plan
showing the incremental area of cultivation resulting from tractor use, a
cropping and rotation plan, and a financial projection of benefits resulting
from the investment. Credit repayments would be made over three years; to be
eligible a farmer must have previously and successfully adopted the technical
crop production packages of the project appropriate to his cropping program.
26.       Credit for land clearing and subsoiling and use of the tractor
hire unit would normally be restricted to farmers or groups that can assemble
a minimum of 10 ha for mechanical cultivation, but judgment would rest with
the project according to careful financial analysis. Likewise farmers or
groups with suitable management and operational capacity who wish to purchase
their own equipment would receive specific technical and budgetary advice
from the projects. Farmer credit for tractors and tractor equipment would
be sought from commercial banks against farm development plans prepared by
the project; this system is proving particularly successful in the Northern
Agricultural Development Projects.
27.       Intermediate Mechanization.  The projects would continuously review
the scope for labor productivity improvement at the intermediate level. In
this respect, improved hand tools could make an important contribution,



SUPPLEMENT 3
Page 7
particularly hand-operated seed planters and fertilizer drills, seed
dressing drums, and threshing, shelling and winnowing equipment. Close
liaison would be maintained with the agricultural engineering units at
IAR and IITA which are already investigating the scope for small equip-
ment on smallholder farmers in West Africa. There is no precedent for
work oxen in either of the project areas and with a general lack of inter-
est and experience in cattle management among crop producers, this is
unlikely to prove an attractive alternative to hand- or tractor-powered
machinery.
28.       Transport.  New technology in crop production is expected to
significantly influence the seasonal labor requirements of project farmers
(see Supplement 2, Appendix 5). Much of the additional labor requirement
resulting from improved technology occurs in the harvesting peak and is
most marked in the case of yam when headloading of the substantial potential
yield increase could prove to be a major bottleneck. It is already common
for farmers to sell yams directly from the field or occasionally to sell
the standing crop well before harvest to a trader who takes responsibility
for both harvesting and haulage. This practice is frequently condemned as
a means of exploitation, but it is significant that with the improved yam
budget of Supplement 2, sale price per ton could be reduced by N 10 with
direct haulage from the field, and by N 20 for a harvest and haulage agree-
ment, without reducing financial return per manday. Considerable scope
exists; therefore, for onfarm transport, which could have an important
impact on the economics of tractor, oxen and small equipment use. Like-
wise, there is considerable potential for groups to bulk their produce
and crop input requirements into full lorry loads and to make transport
arrangements with private haulers.
29.       Land Management.  The advanced technology of components described
above can rarely be introduced in rotation since they frequently have
repercussions on the entire farming system. For example, expensive clearing
for mechanization must be rewarded by an extended period of cultivation and
has far-reaching repercussions on the whole bush/fallow cycle. Likewise,
to achieve economies of scale in machinery use, block farming operations
would inevitably embrace a greater diversity of soil types than would occur
with selective bush clearance of small plots, while exposure to the elements
and loss of the binding effect of roots would increase the risk of soil
erosion. Expertise in land management planning would therefore be an important
service for the progressive farmer.
30.       The Land Use and Farm Advisory Division would maintain a library
of air photographs and would have survey and mapping capability. Air photo-
graphy of the areas has already been carried out and prints should be made
available to the projects. In addition the Land Resources Division of the
UK Ministry of Overseas Development has a project, based in Jos, which
covers a broad investigation into all land resources in the central part
of Nigeria (see map attached to this supplement). Although not completed,



SUPPLEMENT 3
Page 8
valuable information has already been collected 1/ and various reports
published. The projects would note duplicate efforts and close cooperation
would be maintained with ODM's Land Resources Division in Jos.
31.       Land classification of block farming areas would be undertaken
by photo-interpretation and ground survey, and marked onto overlay photo
maps of 1:5,000 scale. Cropping proposals, based on soil capability, would
reflect latest research findings and would draw heavily on experience gained
in mechanized and large farm operations elsewhere in Nigeria. Adoption
of soil conservation recommendations would be a precondition of receiving
tractor hire services from the project.
32.       Modified Tillage.  Perhaps the most pressing need for farming in
the area is for a more stabilized farming system which reduces or removes the
need for bush/fallowing, and a more effective system of weed control, parti-
cularly for spear grass (Imperata cylindrica) which is a problem in both
project areas. These problems will particularly confront mechanized farming
operations.
33.       Research attention is now being given to ways of reducing conven-
tional tillage through the use of herbicides and fast-growing cover crops to
smother weed growth. Under West African conditions, there are considerable
theoretical advantages of minimum tillage both to reduce tractor dependence
and to avoid disturbing the often fragile soils more than absolutely necessary.
Research at IITA has also shown that minimum tillage can effectively lower
soil temperatures during the early season when this is at or near the threshold
for seedling survival of crops such as maize.
34.       However, the practical potential of minimum tillage has still to be
verified both in terms of cost and effectiveness, and in the context of
farming systems based on ridging and mounding. The projects would closely
monitor these developments and incorporate field trials into the applied
research program as appropriate.
1/   The maps attached to Annex 1 (project area) of the respective appraisal
reports are based upon unpublished data collected by the Land Resources
Division in Jos.
