SUPPLEMENT 4
NIGERIA
CENTRAL AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECTS
LIVESTOCK DEVELOPMENT
1.        The two project areas have a sedentary cattle population of some
70,000 head out of a national total of some 11 million head. In addition,
there are some 76,000 goats, 56,000 sheep and 16,000 pigs in the area, to-
gether with large numbers of native poultry.
2.        There is no integration of crop and livestock farming in the proj-
ect areas; cattle ownership is almost entirely in the hands of the Fulani
pastoralists, and small stock of the agriculturists survive as scavengers
around the homestead and adjacent lands.
3.        There has been a significant increase in the resident cattle popu-
lation of the Plateau State over the last decade. This trend is likely
to accelerate as the Sahel and Sudan grazing areas continue to deteriorate
or are alienated by agriculturists. The role of the Lafia area in supplying
year-round grazing will become increasingly important. Trade stock passing
through the Ayangba area each year has been reduced because of the easier
routing now available by road and rail. This trend is expected to continue.
Thus as far as cattle is concerned, there is a large difference between the
project areas. In Lafia there is an Increasing demand for resident cattle and
a good potential for grazing reserves. This does not exist to the same degree
in Ayangba, and furthermore, the number of resident cattle is dimishing.
There are important trade routes which favor the Lafia project area, but
whatever trade routes existed in the Ayangba area (Makurdi-Idah), are now much
diminished in usage.
4.        Existing services of the Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Re-
sources would be incorporated in the project. Thus under both projects
the veterinary services in the project areas which are presently not very
satisfactory would be improved. In addition, both projects would also under-
take to improve the small stock in the areas, mainly poultry; but some work on
goats and pigs would also be undertaken. The aim would be to have small stock
activities largely pay for themselves, either through a direct charge to
farmers or through sale by the project of produce obtained from these activ-
ities but not required for distribution to small-holders.
5.        Detailed proposals, including background information on cattle in
general, for the Lafia project are in Appendix 1; detailed proposals for the
Ayangba project are in Annex 3 attached to the appraisal report.



SUPPLEMENT 4
Appendix 1
NIGERIA
CENTRAL AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPNENT PROJECTS
LIVESTOCK DEVELOPMENT IN LAFIA
A. General
1.        The project area has a sedentary livestock population estimated
at 60,000 cattle 37,000 goats, 36,000 sheep and 12,00 pigs. Migratory
Fulani herds, chiefly from Bauchi, Kaduna and Plateau States, enter the
division for dry season grazing. This annual influx numbers about 60,000
head but a proportion are en route to the Benue, Dep and Mada river flood
plains outside the division. Furthermore, some wet season resident herds
also move south for "fadama" grazing so that dry season cattle numbers are
increased by approximately 30,000 head.
2.        There has been a significant increase in the resident cattle
population over the last decade. This trend is likely to accelerate as
the Sahel and Sudan grazing areas continue to deteriorate or are alienated
by agriculturists.  The role of the division in supplying year-round
grazing will become increasingly important.
3.        Despite a high degree of motivation and competence displayed by
senior local staff, existing extension and disease control efforts are insuf-
ficient due to severe material, manpower and organizational deficiencies.
4.        Increases in agricultural production and population incomes have
resulted in a rising demand for livestock products. The development of a
service abattoir at Keffi in the short-term and the proposed national capital
near Abuja in the long-term will exacerbate this demand.
5.        Despite many restrictions on expanding livestock production, the
fundamental physical resources and technology are available. With an ex-
panded extension input this considerable potential could be exploited.
B. Cattle
Background
6.        Cattle population can only be estimated from Jangali (cattle tax),
vaccination and slaughter figures. Collection of Jangali has now been
rescinded but figures for the division 1970-74 show an average collection of
22,600 head. Jangali collection is recognized to be 30-80% effective. It
was collected from June to September and should represent wet-season-only



SUPPLEIIENT 4
Appendix 1
Page 2
resident cattle population. At 50% collection the total herd would be approx-
imately 45,000 but figures for the inoculation program in (October-March) 1973
indicate 47,000 head. Assuming an 80% turnout for inoculation, the total herd
would be approximately 59,000 head. Consideration of these figures and wet
season slaughter records at about 500/month (actual probably around 650/month)
indicate resident population to be approximately 60,000 head but increasing
annually.
7.        District livestock population figures are only indicative but show a
predominence of cattle in the Awe (45%), Obi (20%), and Assaikio Districts (15%)
while Keana and Lafia town each carry about 10% of total head. Doma district
is virtually empty of cattle in June, reflecting the recognized high tsetse
challenge in this area. Although districts vary in size and proportion of
land under agriculture, these figures illustrate the current importance of
Awe, Obi, Assaikio and Keana for wet season grazing, the likely tsetse distri-
bution, and their correlation with intended grazing reserves.
8.        The bulk of Nigeria's cattle population (variously estimated at
about 10 million head) has traditionally been located in less than 10% of the
total area, i.e., north of the 120 parallel. This concentration is the result
of historical, cultural and social factors, but the distribution of tsetse fly
has had profound influence in maintaining this imbalance. Traditionally, a
large proportion of the herd follow an annual north-south migration to seek
adequate grazing and water which become progressively deficient in the north
and available in the south following the dry season retreat of the tsetse.
However, the increasing alienation of northern land from grazing by expanding
agriculture, the control of major epidemic diseases such as rinderpest which
previously decimated the herds from time to time, and the recent protracted
Sahelian drought have led to serious overstocking and deterioration of
northern rangeland. The Government has adopted a variety of major programs
aimed at encouraging graziers to become more sedentary, particularly in the
more southerly areas where a longer growing season and greater pasture produc-
tion enable a higher year-round carrying capacity. These programs include the
national Tsetse Clearance Program, which is aimed at eradicating tsetse from
100,000 square miles in the Sudan and Northern Guinea Zones. The land freed is
being settled, partly by graziers under the provisions of the 1965 Grazing
Reserves Act which permits Government to set aside land for the exclusive use
of nomadic graziers. In an effort to stimulate this activity, the Government
has recently initiated an active program of relocation of the national herd
with a budget to 1980 of N 24 million.
9.        Meanwhile, the increased spread and settlement of southern cul-
tivators northwards into the Guinea Zone has resulted in increased bush
clearing, hunting and decline of native tsetse hosts, resulting in a decline
in the tsetse challenge to domestic livestock. A significant decrease in
the tsetse challenge is evident in the Lafia Division as large herds now
remain throughout the year, undertaking only local migration, often within
the division.



SUPPLEMENT 4
Appendix 1
Page 3
10.       The traditional livestock owners are nomadic Fulani who possess
herds of classical Zebu type which are well adapted to nomadic conditions,
exhibiting good trekking abilities, rapid compensatory growth and slow
sexual maturity. A high degree of lactation and nutrition anoestrus is
also probably typical, which is in keeping with nomadism and a seasonally
harsh environment, and may account for low calving percentages.
11.       Despite varying degrees of settlement or residence within the
Lafia Division, the basic cattle management system remains essentially
traditional and aims at exploiting the environment to the greatest short-
term advantage. Cattle movements are, therefore, conditioned primarily by
immediate range carrying capacity (and quality) and the availability of
suitable surface water, but the proximity of known or suspected diseased
cattle in trade stock will usually cause sudden abandonment of an otherwise
suitable location. Supplementary feeding is limited to salt and various
local mineral preparations. Crop residues are grazed after harvest and
pastoralists' cattle are generally welcomed by farmers for their manurial
value though occasionally farmers charge a fee for stubble grazing. There
is a tradition of tribal rivalry between Fulani and Tiv which is resulting
in increasing claims by cultivators against passing herdsmen. These claims
are frequently exaggerated and occasionally fraudulent. The tendency of
immigrant Tiv cultivators to settle in isolated scattered hamlets in other-
wise unfarmed areas further aggravates the problem.
12.       The quality and productivity of pastures in the Lafia Division are
generally high by northern standards, particularly in areas that have not been
farmed or overgrazed and burnt. However, the seasonality of grass growth
means that most pastures are undergrazed in the wet season resulting in rapid
maturation and deterioration to tall, fibrous growth by mid-August. It is
common practice to burn these areas as soon as possible in the dry season to
facilitate access and to induce a flush of growth on residual soil moisture.
This is achieved at the expense of plant reserves and encourages generally
inferior, fire-resistent species. Frequent 'cold' fires also tend to promote
regrowth and coppicing and lead to dense, short-scrub formation carrying
little grass, as is exemplified by the traditional dry season grazing area
south and east of Wuse. During the dry season, only crop residues and fadama
lands offer reasonable quality grazing, though these areas also exhibit
evidence of overgrazing. Current dry season weight gains are low to negative,
especially for cows with calves, but the shortness of this period and the
generally high and reliable annual rainfall facilitate attempts to increase
dry season pasture production and overall carrying capacity.
13.       Traditional cattle owners are frequently criticized because they
lack a commercial outlook. Herd size is undoubtedly important and the
Fulani's simple material demands can be met by relatively few cattle sales,
a situation which is aggravated by current rapid inflation in cattle prices.
Additional income is also gained by selling milk and milk products. M{ilk



SUPPLEMENT 4
Appendix I
Page 4
vending is a major activity of the women and the major source of day-to-day
income for purchasing grains, cloth, etc. Herd productivity is generally
low. National calving rates are estimated at 40-50%, mortality is 10-20%
for calves and 4-10% for adults, and growth rate to slaughter maturity is
slow (4-5 years). Low turnoff is, therefore, a corollary of such low produc-
tion parameters, though there is still a strong tendency to increase herd size
against some future catastrophe. The relatively large herd size evident at
Lafia is indicative of improved production, especially higher calving rates,
lower mortality and a faster growth to maturity.
14.       Disease control measures by Government have been generally accepted
by Fulani but have not been accompanied by programs to improve animal nutri-
tion. Within the limitations of the existing system, particularly the lack of
rights of usufruct for graziers, the Fulani have developed a delicate manage-
rial balance with the environment; a detailed knowledge of grazing and water
conditions over a wide area, of cattle handling, disease avoidence and recog-
nition; and a skill at assessing their cattle's needs and the pasture quality.
15.       The considerable numbers of settled or semi-settled Fulani in
northern states and their generally positive response to vaccination and
treatment programs indicate that the traditional Fulani wanderlust is a
result of his concern for his cattle and their feed and water requirements.
The opportunities for improved cattle production in developing new management
systems under which an increasing degree of technical inputs can be applied
efficiently and practically are based on the Fulani and their cattle.
Diseases
16.       Tr7panosomiasis.  This disease has previously been a major restraint
on the development of a cattle industry in the division. Both G. Hlorsitans
and G. Longipalpis species of tsetse were evident in initial surveys in the
1940s. However, there has been a definite decline in the tsetse challenge
since that time so that now G. Morsitans is apparently no longer present
and G. Longipalpis concentrations are chiefly in the west, based on the Doma
Forest Reserve. The actual extent of tsetse decline and current status
cannot be accurately determined without proper surveys of the entire divi-
sion to determine fly population distribution and degree of infection.
Survey of the Doma forest early in 1976 indicated only G. Longipalpis present.
Fixed wing spraying of the reserve appears to have been only 80% effective.
17.       An estimate of the degree of challenge can be gained from the
number and infection rate of cattle in the area, particularly those resident
in the wet season. The high challenge in the Doma area is well recognized
by the Fulani who completely vacate the area by mid-June. The request for
tryps treatment (at N 0.1/head with Berenil) is not high (estimated at 250-300/
head/month). The inadequate veterinary coverage, the frequent shortage of
drugs, the operation of confidence men posing as veterinary staff and peddling



SUPPLEMENT 4
Appendix 1
Page 5
drug substitutes, and the resultant Fulani disillusionment with treatment, all
distort any real assessment of disease incidence from treatment frequency.
However, the increasing population of resident cattle and their apparently
good production indicates that trypanosomiasis is currently down to tolerable
limits as far as the Fulani, a disease-conscious people, are concerned.
18.       The greater grouping of cattle at high stocking rates that will
be a necessary adjunct to good grazing management on the reserves in the
wet season could lead to increased tsetsc challenge, although the reserves
are located in traditional wet season grazing areas where current challenge
is low. Furthermore, the ready access to diagnostic, treatment and pro-
phylaxis services on reserves should control the problem. If the Tsetse and
Trypanosomiasis Division of FDL detects a high challenge through their sur-
veys, an eradication program could be undertaken. However, the latter is
feasible only if natural barriers to fly re-invasion exist around the area in
which the eradication will take place.
19.       Rinderpest was formerly the most important epizootic disease of
cattle. It has been greatly reduced by an intra-African (OAU) cooperative
vaccination program (Government Program 15). Regular annual vaccinations
continue.
20.       Contagious Bovinue Pleuropneumonia.  Following increasing incidence
of the disease, despite a costly slaughter program, the Federal Government
adopted an eradication program based on vaccination (J.P. 28). Under the
scheme (unofficially operating since late 1971 though officially launched
in 1973), states are supplied free of charge with vaccine manufactured at
Vom. The disease is strongly associated with trade and migratory cattle
from Bornu region in the northeast where it is endemic and proving difficult
to eradicate. The greatest likelihood of outbreaks is, therefore, in the
Azara and Wuse regions.
21.       Streptothricosis is common in the area and no prevention or cure
is yet available. The most suitable control method is to eradicate carriers
and prevent skin damage by biting insects or rubbing. Spraying or dipping
for tick control appears to reduce incidence of this disease but is probably
associated with reduced biting fly attack as spraying is frequently carried
to extremes of twice a week. Supplying simple back-rubbers to the pasto-
ralists or encouraging them to construct their own, as well as supplying
suitable chemicals, warrants investigation.
22.       Brucellosis.  The status of this disease is difficult to estimate.
A Federal survey is currently being undertaken to assess frequency before
adopting a Federal campaign. Abortions are known to occur and severe oedaema
of knee joints of bulls is often noted, particularly in trade cattle. In
this wetter climate, with the increasingly dense animal concentrations to-
gether with persistence of open mating systems, the disease could rapidly
increase in frequency and be of economic importance. If no Federal program
is forthcoming a survey of frequency should be carried out within reserves
and if necessary a vaccination program of all weaner females with strain
19 should be adopted as a regular procedure.



SUPPLEMENT 4
Appendix 1
Page 6
23.       Foot and mouth disease seems endemic to the area and outbreaks
occur about twice a year, chiefly in Awe and Azara Districts, particularly
in the dry season, and is again probably associated with migratory cattle.
The local cattle have a high degree of resistence and mortalities are low.
24.       Tuberculosis could become a problem with intensification.  Testing
is carried out by the veterinary division free of charge. Carcass inspection
at slaughter slabs indicates that incidence is low.
25.       Tick-borne diseases appear infrequently.  Only rudimentary treat-
ment is available with clinic hand sprays or in the form of cariscide provided
to Fulani who apply it to their animals with a cloth.
26.       Helminthiasis infection is widespread and aggravated by high humidity,
short dry season, concentrated herding at night camps and close fadama grazing
in the dry season. Incidence of liver fluke is similarly high. The debilitating
effect of these diseases, particularly on young stock, poses a major production
loss.
Trade Routes
27.       The project area lies on one of the major north-south trade routes.
Trade cattle (chiefly bulls) from Bauchi, Bornu, and Gongola States converge
at Wuse and then on via Yelwa entering there from the northeast, crossing
the Dep river at Wuse, then to Azara, Ribi, Adudu, Keana and out south of
the area by Makurdi to the southeastern markets. Some cattle avoid Makurdi
by crossing the Benue river at low points to the west. This route carries
about 2,800 head/month in the dry season and 1,800 head/month in the wet
season.
28.       Another lesser route (about 140/month) branches off this major
route at Yelwa and goes via Shendam to Assaikio, crossing the Dep river,
then onto Lafia and west to Nasarawa joining main northern routes from
Akwanga and southern Niger State at Keffi and leading onto Umaisha, Lokoya
and major urban markets of southwest. This route is expected to become
dominant once the Dep River Bridge is completed and hence avoid the difficult
swim at Wuse. The bulk of the cattle would swing south from Assaikio towards
Obi and Keana through to Makurdi. A third and minor route (about 120/month
in dry season) branches off the major route serving Jos Plateau, Bauchi and
Kaduna States at Akwanga and moves south through Lafia, Agyarako and Kodarko
through to Makurdi.
29.       Control Posts.  Currently ther are no control posts in the Lafia
Division although one is planned for Lafia within the 1976/77 State budget,
which would enable a record to be kept of cattle originating in Lafia Division.



SUPPLEXENT 4
Appendix 1
Page 7
30.       Check points are also absent although a Livestock Assistant has
recently been stationed at the river crossing at Wuse to record cattle
numbers and check permits. Frequent encroachment of cultivators onto or
proximate to trade routes poses a continual challenge to traders' safe
and unhindered passage.
Marketing and Slaughter
31.       Crude slaughter slabs are maintained at every village though meat
inspection can be carried out only at major centers (chiefly Lafia). Although
records of small animal slaughterings are probably very unreliable, cattle
numbers are reasonably accurate and probably represent about 80% of total.
Strong butcher cartels exist, particularly in Lafia where about 50% of total
cattle slaughterings occur. Here 100 butchers operate, with only 20 senior
butchers actually slaughtering an average of 10 head/day. Butchers buy direct
from Fulani (and traders) through a network of employed buyers throughout the
area and they have a facility at Lafia to hold and fatten cattle, so regulat-
ing the number slaughtered and the supply of meat onto the market. Butchers
also give some cattle out with resident Fulani in groups of 5-20 head for
contract herding, payment being made in kind or by mineral and salt supple-
ments. Senior butchers, particularly the master butcher (a hereditary post),
sell a large proportion of their turnover to other butchers at a negotiated
margin. These then allocate each carcass amongst themselves in proportion to
their capacity to pay. Similarly, the senior butcher gives credit in kind to
apprentices who retail sheep and goat meats. A new slaughter facility includ-
ing roof, carcass hoist, cutting-up table and running water is planned for
Lafia within the Third National Development Plan.
32.       Contrary to the commonly-held belief that Fulani are reluct to sell,
the generally low proportion of mature bulls in surveyed herds (9%) indicates
that turnoff is near its potential at current production parameters and static
herds. Furthermore, many trade cattle are semimature males that could be
further grown out. More than twice as many males as females are slaughtered
indicating that a rapid breeder-herd build-up could be occuring, though the
trade cattle figures for Lafia Division that will become available from the
proposed Lafia control post will provide a more reliable assessment.
33.       Cattle and meat prices are considerably lower than around major
centers, even to the north. Price/kilo liveweight is approximately N 0.5-0.6
but varies somewhat with fleshing, e.g.:
(i) four-year-old mature bull in good condition (420 kg), N 220-240
or N 0.52-0.57/kilo liveweight;
(ii) aged cull cow, poor condition (250 kg), N 110-120 or N 0.44-
0.48/kilo.



SUPPLE?MENT 4
Appendix 1
Page 8
Mixed Farming and Draft Oxen
34.       While few draft oxen exist in the area as yet, they are signi-
ficant examples of a considerable potential. A number of farmers exhibit
a genuine interest in owning and rearing cattle, utilizing bush grazing
and crop residues. The practice of village youth laboring with a Fulani
family for a year and being paid in kind, usually a heifer, has led to an
increasing interest and expertise in cattle rearing in certain farming
communities, e.g., Wuse, Awe.
35.       In the more densely populated and farmed areas a potential exists
to encourage short-term fattening operations (e.g., five months). The exist-
ing fattening scheme of the Lafia butchers involves day grazing within four
miles of Lafia on crop residues and grassland and a daily supplement of
branmash at a central night camp close to the town. The operation is run
by Fulani employees.
36.       A declining number of Dwarf Muturu cattle occur in association
with Tiv cultivators, particularly around Obi.  These cattle are allowed
to scavenge freely, the Tiv having a tradition of little interest or
management skill with livestock. Because of low productivity (particularly
calving rates and calf and weaner growth rates) they hold little potential
for increased exploitation in a mixed farming system. Their only advantage
is a degree of trypanosomiasis tolerance but the apparently low challenge,
particularly in farming areas, and the existence of adequate prophylaxis and
treatment reduces the importance of this characteristic. In Benue State,
there is a breeding ranch for N'Dama cattle at Raav which is outside the
Ayangba project area. There is a demand for N'Dama in the more tsetse-
infested areas to the south and west but this breed is inferior to the
White Fulani for beef and milk production relevant to the Lafia area.
37.       The requirement for draft oxen is currently unknown, two units
only being in use at Awe where interest in acquiring further plows and
oxen is high and probably associated with heavy fadama soils used in rice
cultivation. Experience in the Northern Agricultural Development Projects
and in the Chad Basin indicates that the demand for plows and a pair of
oxen is almost unlimited. It offers the most practical means of introducing
livestock to agriculturists; but see also Supplement 3 on farm mechaniza-
tion.
Development Potential
38.       Following the largely effective control of epizootic cattle diseases
in Nigeria, the emphasis must turn increasingly towards expanding production.
Production can be improved by increasing the turnoff numbers (i.e., increasing
calving and weaning rates) and the turnoff weight (i.e., rates of gain to
high liveweight at slaughter).



SUPPLEMEENT 4
Appendix 1
Page 9
39.       The deterioration of traditional tsetse-free northern rangeland
and its use for purposes other than cattle raising highlight the relative
potential of more southerly Guinea Savanna areas as locations where this
production improvement is most possible.
40.       The extension of tsetse eradication into this ecological zone,
the apparent natural reduction in tsetse challenge and the existence of
suitable treatment and prophylaxis removes the major limiting factor to
livestock development in the middle region.
41.       The long growing season of at least eight months, the relatively
high and reliable rainfall and the generally high status of existing
rangeland, particularly in areas selected for establishing grazing re-
serves, indicates that reasonably high animal performance should be
possible.
42.       Areas selected are currently relatively free of cultivators and
would pose few compensation and resettlement difficulties. However, the
farming community is expanding rapidly and the opportunity to establish
suitable reserves will diminish equally rapidly.
43.       Areas of deteriorated rangeland, due to overgrazing and frequent
light burns with resultant invasion of unsuitable grass species and accent-
uated bush coppicing, are evident, particularly in the extreme east. However,
this area is unlikely to be in demand for agriculture due to its soil type and
its relative isolation and the fact that it has long been a major dry-season
grazing area. Enclosure and management offer an opportunity to arrest this
deterioration and eventually to improve the reserve.
44.       Grazing Reserves.  Increased veterinary coverage, particularly
for internal and external parasite control, reproductory disease and try-
panosomiasis, would undoubtedly have a major impact on herd productivity.
In addition, pasture management in the form of heavy wet-season stocking
to maintain pastures in a quality state, fire control to prevent fodder
wastage and range deterioration, and a controlled plan of strategic burning
for regrowth control together with light grazing after fresh burns, would
have a major impact on the nutritional value of the reserve and on herd
productivity.
45.       However, these measures are not compatible with nomadism.  Develop-
ment must, therefore, aim at settled herds in the long-term and eventually
even the possession of some form of leasehold land title to encourage
pastoralist involvement and responsibility for the status of the reserve and
promote the evolution of commercial attitudes towards individual ownership,
turnoff and profitability.



SUPPLEMENT 4
Appendix I
Page 10
46.       In the short-term, significant production improvements can be
made by small innovations and development. Delineation of grazing reserves
so that pastoralists have security of grazing rights and to prevent ingress
of cultivation is a prerequisite. Offering an attractive package of good
grazing and water supplies, veterinary services, marketing outlets and
simple human requisites such as a clinic is likely to encourage Fulani to
register their entry into the reserve.
47.       Although three of the four proposed reserves have dry season fadama
grazing within their boundaries, the full contingent of wet season stock could
not be carried through the dry season. Dry season r'angeland carrying capacity
must also be improved but initially migration out of the reserves (and often
out of Lafia Division) must be allowed in the dry season. Conditions must be
sufficiently attractive to induce a large-scale return to the reserve in the
wet season for reasons of pasture management and disease surveillance.
48.       There is little doubt that strong social and economic reasons exist
for establishing grazing reserves or that the practical technology and the
physical resources exist to support their function. However, in the final
analysis, the successful development of a grazing reserve system must depend
on the strength, practicality and sensitivity of the extension effort.
49.       Trade Routes.  Any attempt to monitor and improve cattle production
within the area must include veterinary involvement and responsibility for the
trade routes through the project area. The improvement of the trade route
system to enable surveillance and control of disease, reduce wastage and
offtake and to rationalize marketing Is an integral segment of any overall
livestock development program.
50.       Although improvements within the Lafia Division are relatively
insignificant nationally, the area does contain major national routes. Any
progress towards exposing the need for delineated, serviced routes and
publicizing their function is extremely valuable.
51.       The survey and delineation of routes can be rationalized so as not
to conflict with the development of grazing reserves or other land use.
C. Small Stock
52.       Both West African Dwarf sheep and goat breeds are dominant in the
project areas though some tall sheep are associated with migratory Fulani
pastoralists.
53.       Unlike cattle, the official figures for sheep and goat population
show a significant decline over the period 1970-73. Sheep population
apparently declined by more than 80%, and goat population by more than 16%
over this period. This decline may be due to rapidly increasing human



SUPPLEMENT 4
Appendix I
Page 11
population, increasing incomes and sharply rising beef prices resulting in
heavy pressure being thrown on the small animal population for meat supply
(pig population has apparently remained static, however). Sheep meat
prices are now high at N 2.00-2.50 per kilo and may reflect this market
situation. There is also a definite consumer preference for sheep meat
over goat meat though goat meat prices are only slightly lower.
54.       The distribution of sheep and goats is very uneven in Lafia Division:
70% of sheep and 60% of goats occur in the Obi District and only 10% of each
in Lafia and Assaikio Districts, the next largest concentration. Official
slaughter figures for small animals are also very inaccurate but indicate Obi
must be a major source for other parts of the division, particularly Lafia.
55.       Current management practices are virtually nil, sheep and goats
scavenging freely. Although helminthiasis and especially liver fluke are
constant delibitating factors, the major disease is a serious infectious
and contagious disease of unknown origin called "kata". The disease exhi-
bits symptoms of rinderpest though animals usually die of pneumonia. It
has a high morbidity and mortality if animals are moved from another area
or herded together in large flocks. Previous attempts to establish intensive
goat farms have met with massive mortalities. No effective treatment is
known. Enteritis can also cause serious losses, particularly in young
stock at the onset of the rains.
56.       The pig population is relatively stationary at around 12,000
head. The majority are found in Obi district (80%) and Awe district (12%).
They are usually associated with Christian and pagan cultivators. Official
slaughter figures (only 600/year for Lafia) indicate most slaughterings
occur outside official scrutiny and probably approximate 5,000/year. At
this level, they contribute significantly to meat supply and relieve
pressure on the sheep and goat population. Usual pig meat price is about
N 1.00/kilo.
57.       The local pigs are small, active, carry little fat and survive
entirely by scavenging. Their litter size is normally low (4-6) but they
are reported to be well adapted and disease resistant.
58.       Local poultry, although numerous and hardy, are small and lay only
40-60 small eggs/year. They cross well with exotic breeds and the results
of a small but effective State-run cockerel exchange program, based on day-
old chicks reared to five months and issued to farmers, are generally good.
Development Potential
59.       Until "kata" disease is controlled there is little potential for
intensifying goat or sheep production and management inputs on a large
scale. However, a regular and strategic treatment program for helminthiasis,
particularly in lambs and pregnant or nursing ewes, would increase growth
rates and survival.



SUPPLEMENT 4
Appendix 1
Page 12
60.       A program for the construction of containment stables for 2-6
animals woul-d assist in disease control and in the conduct of improved
management practices. These would be simple roofed structures, built
of bamboo above ground, with slatted floors to control disease and to
facilitate manure collection. Once contained in this fashion, improved
management inputs can be used in the form of disease control, balanced
feeding, culling and selective mating. Such a system could equally be
applied to goats. However, the additional labor requirements and the
current low value of manure (due to high fertilizer subsidy) may limit
acceptance.
61.       Although Large White and Landrace breeds of pigs are available for
crossbreeding from State farms at Jos and Vom, and the State provides loans
to farmers to start pig keeping, there is very little farmer demand in the
project areas. Some individual attempts to utilize improved breeds have
met with failure due to disease. The level of management necessary to gain
the advantages of improved genotype is inapplicable at this stage and the
local breed is best suited to local conditions.
62.       The improved cockerel exchange program has met with very wide
acceptance and could be greatly expanded, the major bottleneck at present
being the centers' capacity to hold the pullet section as laying birds.
Although purchasing sexed chicks (cockerels only) would avoid this problem,
an exchange program that includes pullets will have a greater effect on
production. The efficacy of the program could be further improved by
identifying and possibly reducing the cause of the 20-25% mortality in
exchanged birds.  A basic extension effort to encourage greater use of
supplementary feed to poultry, particularly rice bran, and a supply of
suitable watering facilities is fundamental to increased production.
D. Development Proposals
Grazing Reserves
63.       The objectives of establishing grazing reserves are:
(i)  to secure land strictly for use by pastoralists and their
herds by indirectly controlling the settlement and expansion
patterns of immigrant and established cultivators;
(ii)  to create a system in which modern production methods and
concepts could be effectively introduced to the traditional
sector including a basis for the eventual development of
group or individual title to grazing lands and responsibility
for their maintenance.



SUPPLEMENT 4
Appendix 1
Page 13
64.       Attainment of these objectives would involve:
(i) surveying and delineating grazing reserve boudaries so that
they could eventually be gazetted;
(ii)  delineating internal grazing management units or blocks based
on the land system and management factors and each capable of
carrying a similar number of cattle;
(iii) providing grazing and treatment rights to those pastoralists
who elect to join the reserve. This would include a system
of registration and enumeration of each herd and would carry
an implied willingness to accept management directives from
reserve staff;
(iv)  providing adequate dry season water and undertaking extensive
improvement to rangeland productivity, particularly dry season
pasture quality;
(v) providing comprehensive veterinary coverage, suitable handling
facilities, necesssary nutritional supplements and herd manage-
ment advice;
(vi)  maintaining detailed records of herd size, composition, and pro-
duction, and developing a measure of rangeland performance on
which to base future management policies and further reserve
development;
(vii) assisting in the development of suitable market outlets for
both animal and milk products;
(viii)  enabling and encouraging pastoralist participation in usual
social services, e.g, health care and education; and
(ix)  encouraging the identification or association of each family
or group with a particular grazing unit to foster the
development of responsibility for range quality, facilities,
fire, etc.
65.       Four suitable areas for grazing reserves have been identified.  All
include light or nil cultivation so that minimum disruption and compensation
to cultivators will be necessary. Tentative boundaries discussed with the
traditional authorities are shown on the map attached to the appraisal report.
The approximate area in each reserve and the project year development begins
is as follows:



SUPPLEMENT 4
Appendix I
Page 14
(1)  Keana Reserve              29,000 hectares           Year 1
(ii)  Assaikio Reserve           24,300 hectares           Year 2
(iii)  Awe Reserve                47,000 hectares           Year 3
(iv)  Wuse Reserve               36,400 hectares           Year 3
66.       The Keana, Assaikio and Awe reserves are already major grazing
areas for resident cattle while the Wuse reserve is part of a heavily-grazed,
traditional dry season area for migratory cattle and has deteriorated due to
over-grazing and frequent firing.
67.       Each reserve would have resident management and development staff
housed at a base unit or headquarters where cattle handling facilities,
consisting of yards, crush, plunge-dip, holding paddocks, scales, and loading
ramp, together with store room, workshop, clinic and mobile UPE 1/ base, would
also be located.
68.       This base unit would be serviced by an all-weather road and would
serve as a nucleus for management, venue for sales and markets and terminus
for transport to and from villages outside the reserve.
69.       The Grazing Unit (GU) would be the basic unit within the reserve and
would be an area capable of carrying about 2,000 head in the wet season.
Improvement staffing and services would be allocated according to the needs of
the Grazing Unit and would include:
Water facilities - one 6-10,000 cubic meter earth dam for each GU
Stock handling   - one set of yards with crush, plunge-dip and
ramp per three GU's
Staff            - Grazing Control Officer (GL 06) per GU assisted
by one laborer
- Reserve Ranger (GL 07) per three GU's
- Range Management Officer (GL 13) per grazing
reserve.
70.       The Grazing Control Officer (GCO) would maintain direct contact with
pastoralists, would be the communications channel between them and manage-
ment and vice versa, and would actively participate in all husbandry and
management procedures initiated by the reserve management. Reserve Rangers
would supervise three GCO's each and would be responsible for implementing all
range management, husbandry and veterinary activities on the 6,000 head
involved. The Range Management Officer would be charged with overall super-
vision and coordination of entire reserve management, implementing develop-
ment plans, initiating all husbandry procedures, marketing outlets, etc.
1/   Universal Primary Education.



SUPPLEMENT 4
Appendix I
Page 15
71.       St,rict registration of cattle entering and leaving each GU would
be essential and would comprise a record of herd size and numbers in each
class, and production data. An annual vaccination program would be imple-
mented together with trypanosomiasis treatment or prophylaxis depending on
surveyed tsetse challenge and experience. A program of strategic dipping for
tick control and treatment for helminthiasis would be undertaken.
72.       Grazing conditions, veterinary coverage and sympathetic extension
would be of such an order as to positively encourage graziers to join the
reserve in sufficient numbers in the wet season to enable heavy wet season
grazing for maintenance of pasture quality throughout the wet season. On
current estimates about 30% of wet season stock must vacate the reserve in the
dry season. The numbers and the individuals to leave would have to be clari-
fied well in advance by discussion between GCO and pastoralists. Keana has
particularly acute dry season problems because of the absence of any fadama
grazing, but it is proximate to large amounts of stubble and bush/fallow
grazing to north and east.
73.       Grassland development would involve better management of existing
pastures; strip sowing and the introduction of improved grass species are not
contemplated at this stage.
74.       Marketing developments would aim at increasing returns to pastor-
alists, increasing market knowledge and rationalizing turnoff to culling
policy, grazing and seasonal conditions. Regular sales could be held at the
base unit where both butchers and graziers could become familiar with sale by
weight and sale stock could be trucked out.
75.       The traditional sale of milk and milk products would be made
possible by encouraging and organizing the establishment of regular com-
mercial transport services to and from major adjacent villages and markets.
76.       Offtake will increase principally in response to increased calving,
weaning and growth rates and reduction of deaths due to disease. By limiting
specific graziers to specific areas with a mutually agreed maximum carrying
capacity, a degree of pressure would be brought to bear to stimulate turnoff.
Similarly, by an extension effort through the GCO, encouragement would be
given to sell all diseased or non-breeding animals.
77.       Once the benefits of the reserve are seen to be clearly recognized
by the Fulani, a mutually negotiated and agreed per head fee could be levied
to help pay for services and inputs supplied by the reserve. This fee could
best be paid in kind at an agreed price per kilogram liveweight and be payable
at the end of the wet season.



SUPPLEMNT 4
Appendix 1
Page 16
78.       Cost estimates are in Table 1, herd projections in Table 2, model
herd composition in Table 3, valuations in Table 4, and cost/benefits of
grazing reserves in Table 5.
Trade Routes
79.       The objective of establishing trade routes is to develop permanent
facilities that will enable full control of trade cattle movement, disease
surveillance and treatment and reduced production loss en route by: (i)
providing suitable cattle handling facilities at check points on all entry
points of routes into the project area, and adequate, staff and equipment for
thorough disease surveillance, treatment, dipping and recording, (ii)
providing clearly delineated routes free of farmer encroachment or harass-
ment, and (iii) providing at regular intervals night camps with containment
and water facilities and good grazing.
80.       The routes would be developed in successive years beginning with
the Wuse-Abudu-Keana route in Year 1. Both Assaikio-Obi-Keana and Lafia-
Kodarko would be developed in the second year.
81.       Routes would be surveyed preliminary to initiating formal gazetting
procedures and delineated with chained and raked line about 30 meters wide.
The perimeter of night camps (about 100 hectares) would also be cleared as
would water and camp sites.
82.       Night camps would be perimeter fenced, and would contain dams or
wells with- pump, tank and troughing. A hut would house the resident pump
attendant who would double as supervisor and guard.
83.       Check points would be developed after but adjacent to the river
crossings at Wuse, Assaikio and a control post at Lafia. They would involve
yards, crush, plunge-dip and ramp, and would be associated with the usual
night stop facility which would be further subdivided to enable separation
of mobs and short-term isolation. Staff other than pump attendants and guards
would be based at nearby Farmers Service Centers at Assaikio, Azara and Lafia,
and would consist of a GCO and two laborers.
84.       Cost estimates are in Table 6.
Poultry Upgrading
85.       The objective would be to attempt a high degree of saturation
with improved cockerels and pullets throughout the project area so as to in-
crease meat and egg production.
86.       The project would establish a Poultry Rearing Unit based in Lafia
town.  Day-old, unsexed, vaccinated chicks would be purchased from the NLMA
hatchery at Jos or from one of a number of commercial hatcheries in the south.



SUPPLEMENT 4
Appendix 1
Page 17
Chicks would be raised to five months before exchange for village poultry, al-
lowing a period for adaption before sexual maturity. Rearing capacity would
be 10,000 chicks per year, enabling an output of 9,000 birds/year for exchange
after allowing time for cleaning and disinfection between batches and a 10%
mortality.
87.       Pullet and cockerel distribution would be on an exchange basis
though a 20% bonus would be offered to villages that agreed to a complete
exchange of either sex of local poultry. The high demand likely to be
enjoyed by the program enables certain conditions of improved management
(e.g., water supply and supplementary feeding) to be a condition on those
entering the scheme. All stock would undergo a minimum of two weeks limited
free range prior to release to enhance adaptation. All birds received by
the project during the exchange operations would be sold at prevailing market
prices possibly after a short fattening period on limited free range, during
which they would run with the next batch of exchange birds. No egg produc-
tion per se I/ would be undertaken but the unit would concentrate its efforts
on exchange and extension to increase survival and production of exchanged
birds.
88.       Cost estimates are in Table 7.
Smallholder Fattening of Draft Oxen
89.       The objective would be to encourage and assist agriculturists
wishing to procure cattle for draft and/or fattening purposes so as to
enable an integrated and balanced approach to cattle development, and would:
(i) provide a credit and extension package for agriculturists
to procure and run cattle;
(ii) provide an outlet for store, cull or "fee" cattle turnoff
from grazing reserves;
(iii) introduce agriculturists to the benefits of a mixed farming
system;
(iv) utilize the large body of crop residues, bush/fallow and by-
products associated with the agricultural system; and
(v) improve production by helping to ensure slaughter cattle
are as near to optimum weight as possible.
90.       The program would be directed to progressive farmers and preferably
to those with access to some cattle experience who would agree to adopt certain
1/   Some egg production might be undertaken to provide additional revenue,
which, together with sales of old birds, would cover the costs of the
poultry units.



SUPPLEMENT 4
Appendix 1
Page 18
management practices. A high level of supervision would be required at the
outset including practical demonstration of the use of draft oxen. The latter
are likely to find greatest initial application on the heavy soils in rice
growing areas, e.g., Awe.
91.       Cattle would be issued in groups of 2-5 depending on cattle supply
and the individual's credit worthiness. Farmers must have suitable shaded
stalls and would be encouraged to organize themselves into small groups with
up to 25 head for day grazing under supervision whenever suitable grazing is
available. Credit would be provided for cattle, veterinary expenses and
mineral supplements and would be included with insurance backup, marketing
assistance and a specific sale contract in an all-inclusive package.
92.       Cattle would be stall fed at night and whenever quality grazing was
scarce. Use would be made of crop reserves, mill by-products (particularly
rice bran) and small plot plantings of fertilized pennisetum. The establish-
ment of perennial stylosonthes in first year bush/fallows, to provide better
quality grazing or cut fodder and to reduce "imperata" invasion, could become
a condition of credit, with seed provided in the package. The length of
the fattening period has been estimated at ten months but in practice could
vary with the season of purchase, though it is unlikely to exceed this figure.
Draft oxen could be funded on an annual or biennial basis to ensure one
trained animal was always available.
93.       Butcher Fattening.  A general extension effort would be aimed at
expanding and improving the existing butcher fattening and holding program
so as to maximize slaughter weights and to increase butchers' capacity to
absorb seasonal market oversupply without seriously affecting price.
94.       A partial budget for smallholder fattening is given in Table 6.
Livestock Extension Unit
95.       In addition to the above all existing services of the Livestock
Division within the area would become the responsibiliy of the LEU, including
meat and hide inspection and handling, and compulsory inoculation programs.
The latter would be undertaken by a mobile team of verterinary staff that
would use strategically located FSCs as field bases. These would provide
accommodation, drug storage and refrigeration facilities.
96.       The LEU would provide specialist advice to any farmer wishing to
undertake commercial livestock production. With a long-term objective of
encouraging mixed farming, it is intended that responsibility for routine
management advice be gradually assumed by the General Extension Services and
supported by LEU advisers. To this end the LEU would include basic husbandry
training for extension staff in its training program. Livestock training
would initially be aimed at turning out practically-oriented Grazing Control
Officers and Livestock Assistants for reserves, routes and general extension.
97.       Capital and recurrent costs for LEU are given in Table 9 and a
summary of total costs of the Livestock Development Section is given in
the respective appraisal report.

SUPPLEMENT 5
NIGERIA
CENTRAL AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECTS
FORESTRY
A. The Sector
1.        An assessment of the Forestry Sector is contained in the following
Bank documents:
-    Agricultural Sector Survey, Nigeria, Report
PA-115(a), dated January 26, 1973 (see Volume
II, Annex 5);
-    West Africa Forestry Sector Study, Report
1114-WA (MR), dated March 30, 1976; and,
-    Industrial Development Prospects in Nigeria,
Report 310 - UNI, dated January 4, 1974
(see Volume II).
This section summarizes the main features and some important aspects of the
sector, which are dealt with in more detail in the earlier reports, and up-
dates information regarding recent changes in forestry development emphasis
and forest management.
2.        Forest Resource.  Up to the present time, virtually all timber
used in the country has been harvested from the natural forests and savanna
woodlands. These have undergone progressive depletion over time by incursions
for agriculture, grazing and plantation crops, fellings for local requirements
for buildings and fuelwood and commercial exploitation of valuable timber
species. Review of the Bank reports and current UNDP/FAO studies indicates
that there is a progressive decline in the rate of natural regeneration
of the forests and savannas in relation to the increasing demand function
for forest products and a progressively declining area under tree cover, which
is resulting in an absolute decline in the growing stock. In aggregate
terms, this situation holds for all forest products. With firewood, for
example, while there appears to be an abundance of scrub savanna to meet
local requirements in many areas, there are severe shortages in the bigger
townships and many thousands of tons of fuelwood are being transported an-
nually over distances exceeding 160 km (100 miles) radius around, e.g.,
Kaduna, Jos and Makurdi, which is progressively depleting the surrounding
resource base. The position is similar elsewhere in the country. The general
shortage of straight durable poles is a contraint on the quality of local
housing and buildings and development of transmission lines for electrification



SUPPLEMiENT 5
Page 2
and telephones. Commercial exploitation of the forests is progressively
depleting the stock of merchantable species and recent estimates by a joint
Federal Department of Forestry (FDF)/FAO team indicate that a vigorous pro-
gram of man-made plantations will be needed to meet future domestic demand
beyond the 1980s.
3.        The Federal Military Government (FMG) has placed an embargo on
the export of all logs and manufactured forest products in order to con-
serve existing resources, and through FDF is expanding State Forestry
Department activities in forest plantations development.
4.        The quality and extent of the existing forests is related to the
natural vegetation zones and the degree of exploitation which has taken
place. A simplistic portrayal of the different types of Nigerian forestry
resources is given in the map attached to this supplement, which was pre-
pared by the Savanna Forestry Research Station (SFRS) and which classifies
the country into various zones of broadly similar characteristics as fol-
lows:
Sudan Savanna   NW, N, and NE as far south          Natural vegetation profoundly

5.        Forest Estate.  Within the various zones, forest reserves were es-
tablished earlier in the century with the objective of bringing selected
areas under systematic management for protection and production. These re-
serves which represent about 10% of the total land area contain the major
remaining forest resource. Many of the reserves, particularly those outside
the rain forests, are protection forests in steep and shallow soil areas,
with little or no productive function. Present data on the stocking den-
sities of the reserves are sparse and inadequate and an ongoing FDF/UNDP/FAO
project 1/ is assembling inventory data covering the major resources in the
Derived savanna and rain forest zones and also producing vegetation coverage
data for the whole country. Forest reserve areas are summarized in Table 1.
6.        In previous years, a significant proportion of commercial timber
was harvested from forests and woodlands outside the forest reserves. This
resource has now been mainly exploited and is now contributing only some 20%
to total annual fellings. Supply of commercial timber from this source is
expected to be exhausted within the next decade or so. However, these con-
tinue to be the main supply areas for fuelwood, which is by far the largest
product of the forests.
1/   High Forest Development Project NIR/71/546.



SUPPLEMENT 5
Page 4
7.        Forest Plantations.  The major development has taken place in the
forest reserves of the high forests; data for plantation development in the
savanna areas are not available except for Plateau and Benue States (see
para 28 and subsequent paras), but the latter are not extensive. Records
for the high forest areas indicate that up to 1974, some 80,000 ha had been
cleared for planting, about 55,000 ha of which was cleared during 1966 to
1974 and most of the remainder during 1951 to 1965. The actual area success-
fully planted is not known and is likely to be far less. FDF have carried
out a recent aerial survey of a selected area of pulpwood plantations for the
proposed Iwopin pulpmill which indicated that only about 50% of the recorded
area was actually planted and that poor stocking occurred in the planted area.
Plantations before 1950 (about 3,000 ha recorded) are mainly composed of in-
digenous hardwood including mahogany (Khaya species) and obeche (Triplochiton).
Between 1951 and 1965, teak (Tectona grandis) and Gmelina aborea were intro-
duced on a larger scale and these species account for over 60% of the area
planted during 1966 to 1974. Teak is favored because it is expected to have
a shorter rotation (some 60 years) than the traditional hardwood (e.g., ma-
hogany, 80 years). A problem of butt rot (Rigidoporus iignosus) has occurred
and is presently being closely monitored by the Federal Institute of Forest
Research (FIFR). This is estimated to be affecting some 5% of the trees
planted. A curative treatment is possible if infection can be identified in
the early stages, otherwise the disease is fatal to the crop. Gmelina is
being extensively planted for pulpwood. Plantation data are summarized in
Appendix 1.
8.        The Savanna Forestry Research Station (SFRS) at Samaru was estab-
lished under a joint Federal Government/UNDP/FAO project in the 1960s to
consolidate earlier pioneer work and develop techniques for plantation de-
velopment in the northern States. FAO involvement terminated earlier this
year and the station is continuing under the direct management of FIFR. The
project has been successful in developing introductory trials of fast grow-
ing species and establishment techniques at the nearby plantations at Afaka,
and these have a potential for application over a wide area of the savanna
zones. FIFR operate another research station at Kano and are undertaking
the establishment of substations to Samaru in the NE, Middle Belt and
Mambilla Plateau in the SE, to set up introductory trials along similar
lines.
9.        Production, Consumption and Projected Demand.  The FAO Year Book3
of Forest Products 1975 records total roundwood production as 45 million m
in 1962 rising to 60 milliSn m in 1973, of which firewood is recorded as 43
million m and 57 million m respectively. There is some doubt as to the ac-
curacy of the data.
10.       A 1970 study 1/ carried out by the UNDP/FA0 Savanna Forestry Re-
search Station project, estimated local annual production for the "Savanna
Areas," composed of the Sudan, N. and S. Guinea, Jos and Derived Savanna
zones, with a population of about 35 million, as follows:
1/   FAO:SF/NIR 16 Technical Report 1.  Wood Requirements in the Savanna
Region. S. Thulin.

Fuelwood                   22 million m3
