these products in
"Savanna Zone."
11.       The ongoings FDF/UNDP/FAO High Forest Development Project has pro-
duced an interim report 1/ of industrial roundwood production in 1974 and
projected demand for forest based products up to 1990. These data are sum-
marized in Table 2.  The forecasts of future domestic demand are acknowledged
to be optimistic and are related to the relatively high growth in GDP which
has taken place over the past four years and is forecasted to continue over
the next plan period and beyond.
12.       Forest Industries.  With the exception of two or three larger saw-
mills, (e.g., the Northern Nigerian Timber Corporation Sawmill at Ochaja,
Benue State) and small township sawmills in the savanna zones, which resaw
to dimension sizes timber balks produced by local pit sawyers, the major
forest-based industries are concentrated in the high forest zone. Indus-
tries include some 400 sawmills, 4 plymills, a small blockboard plant, pre-
fabricated housing and several match factories. There is one paper mill at
Jebba, with a production of 14,000 tons/year, presently operating on imported
pulp and recycled waste paper. Minor industries of the Sudan and Guinea
zones include gum arabic production from Acacia senegal, tannins produced
from Acacia milotica and shea butter produced from Butyrospermum paradoxum.
13.       The sawmilling industry is predominantly composed of small-scale
units operating with one or two CD5 type band-saw headrigs with overall aver-
age production of 1900 m3 sawn-timber at 250 x 8 hour shifts/year and average
employment of 17.3 men/mill. Total production in 1974 was estimated at
847,000 m3(sawn). In addition to this volume, it is estimated pit sawyers
produced about 85,000 m3/year, mainly in the Guinea Savanna zones and S.E.
State. The distribution of sawmilling production is summarized in Table 3.
14.       The largest plymill, with a capacity of 33,000 m3/year, and the
blockboard plant, are operated by the African Timber and Plywood Co. (AT & P)
at Sapele and integrated with one of the larger sawmills in the country.
There are two plymills with capacities of 11,000 m3/year, one at Piedmonte
near Benin with a private Italian company participation, and the other at
Epe, Lagos State, operated by the Swiss-Nigerian Plywood Co. The fourth mill
is situated at Calabar, Cross River State, with a capacity of 1,000 m3/year
1/   Working Document NIR/71/546.  November 1975.  0. Baykal.



SUPPLENENT 5
Page 6
and producing only veneers; this is reported to be now wholly State-owned
with the former US partners terminating their interests. There is cur-
rently no production of particle board or fiber board although imports of
the latter are around 12,000 tons/year.
15.       Expansion plans for the mechanical wood-using industries include:
-    increasing the capacities of the Piedmonte and
Calabar plymills by a further 10,000 m3/year each;
-    constructing a particle board mill of 32,000 tons/year
capacity at the AT & P Sapele complex;
-    constructing a further particle board mill in Lagos
by private sector;
-    constructing two integrated industries including
sawmill/plymill/particle board, one in Ondo State
and the other in Calabar, Cross River State, by
joint venture between Rumanian/State Governments.
16.       Pulp and Paper.  Recent estimates of consumption carried out by
the ongoing FDF/UNDP/FAO project, 1/ summarized in Table 4, indicate total
consumption of 90,000 tons in 1972, 106,000 tons in 1973 and 122,000 tons
in 1974, based on production at the Jebba mill of 14,000 tons/year and im-
portation of the balance. The estimate for 1972 is some 30,000 tons lower
than the estimate given in Bank Report 310-UNI and 23,000 tons lower than
a Jaakko Poyry (JP) study, commissioned by the Federal Ministry of Indus-
tries, which formed a preliminary basis for development plans for the sec-
tor.
17.       The JP study estimates demand will increase to 484,000 tons by
1985, which is considered by FDF to be a conservative estimate. Proposals
for the construction of two new mills, a 120,000 ton/year cultural paper
and market pulp mill at Iwopin in the former Western State and 60,000 ton/year
newsprint mill at Calabar in Cross River State and expansion of the Jebba
mill in Kwara State to a capacity of 60,000 to 100,000 tons/year have been
under active planning from 1974, with follow-up studies being carried out by a
number of international consultants sponsored by the Federal Ministry of
Industries, with the objective of achieving construction and start-up of
the industries between 1978 and 1980.
18.       A substantive dichotomy has developed between FDF and the Federal
Department of Industry over the timing of the Iwopin and Calabar mills
because of the limited area of pulpwood plantations so far established.
FDF has also indicated its reservations about the Jebba mill expansion
1/   NIR/71/546, November 1975.



SUPPLEMENT 5
Page 7
because of technical problems expected in establishing fast-growing pulp-
wood plantations in the area compared with the more favorable growing con-
ditions in alternative areas. There is a further apparent difference in
approach regarding the utilization of the indigenous forest as an interim
supply of pulpwood, and the availability of pulpwood from these sources.
Utilization of indigenous hardwoods is proposed for the Jebba expansion,
but appears not to be under consideration Sor the Iwopin and Calabar
proposals. FDF consider estimates of 40 m /ha of indigenous species in the
forests selected to supply Jebba a gross over-estimate, whereas volumes
of this order of magnitude or more would be available in the forests supply-
ing the Iwopin and Calabar mills.
19.       FDF has indicated additional development proposals which could be
alternatives to the Jebba mill, which include a possible short-fiber mill
in the Ageneboo-Lokoja areas in Kwara/Benue States based on Gmelina arborea
plantations to be developed in the forest reserves situated adjacent to the
east and west banks of the Niger river and for which Federal funds are being
allocated (see para 26), and a possible long-fiber mill in Kaduna State to
be based on pine plantations which it is proposed to develop in that area.
Table 5 s=mmarizes the reported pulp and paper development proposals.
20.       Forest Management.  Management of the forest reserves and control
of the exploitation of non-reserved forests is the responsibility of the
State Governments. Each state has a Forest Department (FD) headed by a
Chief Conservator of Forests with field staff, including professional, tech-
nical and policing grades, having territorial charge of assigned areas. In
addition to State FD staff, there are Local Government Council (LGC) staff in
the lower grades, whose main function is to control felling and revenue
collection. A major function of FD has been the protection and control of
natural forest exploitation.
21.       In some States (e.g., Plateau State), the LGC receives all revenues
from sales of forest products. This position is inconsistent in areas where
heavy investment in plantations establishment is being undertaken by the State
and changes in the beneficial ownership pattern have taken place in some
States.
22.       Until 1971, the Federal Department of Forestry Research located at
Ibadan provided technical information to the State FDs and managed forestry
training at subprofessional level at the Ibadan forestry school; the Jos
forestry school was upgraded to a higher technical standard and also became
the responsibility of the department in 1971. In the same year, a separate
Federal Department of Forestry (FDF) was established with the responsibility
for developing a national development program and coordinating forestry de-
velopment in the States. In 1976, the Department of Forestry Research was re-
named the Federal Institute for Forestry Research and made responsible to the
Agricultural Research Council and not directly to the Federal Ministry of
Agriculture and Rural Development as in the past. As such, it is not di-
rectly integrated with FDF, which comes directly under RIARD.



SUPPLEMENT 5
Page 8
23.       FDF is expanding its activities through the development of spe-
cialist divisions at Federal headquarters, consisting of planning, manage-
ment, forest industries, wildlife and conservation, extension and publicity,
and a sawmill training center. FDF offices are being established in State
capitals.  The allocation of Federal funds to the States for forestry de-
velopment is now managed and monitored by the FDF. In 1974/75, allocations
to the States for pulpwood plantation development amounted to US$8.3 million
and US$2.9 million for other plantation development.
24.       Development Plans.  The FDF proposals for the current plan period,
1975 to 1980, anticipate an expenditure of some US$144 million (at 1975
prices), which would be dispersed mainly through State Forestry Departments
(FDs). Detailed features of the activities are given in Appendix 2 and sum-
marized in Table 6.
25.       In addition, FDF have proposed a number of new UNDP/FAO projects to
give support to the activities under FDF 2, FDF 4, FDF 5, and FDF 9, at es-
timated costs of Federal Government US$4 million, U1DP/FAO US$2 million.
(See also Appendix 2 and para 27).
26.       The main emphasis is given to pulpwood plantations development for
the proposed Iwopin, Calabar and Jebba mills, (US$84 million). Other indus-
trial plantations (US$12 million) and local forestry, in the central and
northern states (US$18 million) are also selected for special emphasis. Some
US$20 million expenditure is proposed for developing a detailed data base and
management plans for the national forest resource, and for strengthening par-
ticipation of the FDF in forestry development.
27.       International Participation.  Besides the two UJNDP/FAO projects
discussed in paras 5 and 8 (High Forest Development and Savanna Forest
Research Station), there are two other ongoing joint FDF/UNDP/FAO projects
assisting with forest utilization; the Timber Design and Construction Pro-
ject, NIR/68/012 and the Sawmill Training Center, Benin, TF/NIR-22 (FIN).
In addition, the British ODM is cooperating with FIFR in a project to
study propagation of obeche (Triplochitan sclerox7lon), which was formerly
a major export species. The Irish and Finnish governments are partici-
pating in projects in foresty and game management training at Ibadan Uni-
versity. Canadian aid assisted between 1965 and 1973 with projects in for-
est utilization in the former Western State and forest industry in Oban
forests in Cross River State. FDF is also negotiating with CIDA for a wood
consumption and marketing survey.
B. Plateau and Benue States
28.       Forest Resource.  The forest resource includes gazetted forest
reserves (FRs), community forest areas (CFAs), mining reclamation areas,
and extensive areas of low volume savannas in unallocated and private
lands. Records exist which indicate the areas of FRs and CFAs and rough



SUPPLEMENT 5
Page 9
maps have been prepared to show the location of the former. (See maps
attached to the respective appraisal reports). It is reported that in some
cases, boundary demarcation of the FRs has not been completed on the ground,
and some encroachment has taken place through cultivation by farmers; the
actual areas are therefore likely to be less than those recorded. No detailed
information is available regarding the stocking density and types of trees
both in the FRs and savannas, and assessment is largely subjective. The
UNDP/FAO High Forest Development Project will be providing forest inventory
data for some of the FRs in Benue State and a report on the forests of Jos and
Jemaa Plateaux and Benue Valley is currently under preparation as part of the
Central Nigeria Land Resources Project, aided by the British ODM. Both
reports are expected to be available by the end of 1976. A. list of the main
tree species and their uses is included in Appendix 3.
29.       Plateau State.  Table 7 contains a tabulation of the forest re-
sources and base data. The recorded area of FRs, CFAs, and mine reclama-
tion areas is 3,727 km2, 103 km2 and 12 km2 respectively and comprise about 8%
of the land area. There is little tree cover on the Jos Plateau and the FRs
which exist are mainly in the hilly and rocky areas bounding the plateau and
serve as protection. In response to severe shortages of fuelwood and timber,
the development of CFAs was initiated in the Jos division some 30 years ago,
and they are mainly concentrated in this area (see para 31). The mining
reclamation areas are also located on the plateau and the local timber short-
age is a main incentive to undertaking the rehabilitation of areas disturbed
through mining operations. In the other divisions of the State, the FRs
contain scattered stands of commercial species, but these are reported to be
insufficient in quantity to sustain any sizeable forest industry. The Doma
FR, about 864 km2 in the Lafia project area, is by far the largest reserve.
This is composed of a variety of soil and vegetation types, with about 25%
ironstone outcrops and hills, a predominance of savanna woodlands and grass-
lands, and very little closed-canopy forests.
30.       Management of the FRs and CFAs is the responsibility of the State
FD of MANR. Ownership of the forests vests with the Local Government Councils
(LGC). The FD, headed by a Chief Conservator of Forests (CCF) with supporting
field staff in each of the administrative divisions, recommends protection and
exploitation practices and tariffs to be charged for forest produce, which
upon agreement by the LGCs are then codified in forest rules. The last
revision of tariffs was in 1963 and it is due for updating. Besides the CFAs
the FD have also initiated small reforestation programs in FRs amounting to
about 1,100 ha to date, mainly of eucalyptus, gmelina and teak, and providing
the finance and field staff for their implementation. Consideration is now
being given to changing the existing pattern of revenue payments to LGCs so
that revenues from plantation produce will accrue to the State to offset
against expenditures.
31.       Community Forest Areas (CFAs).  These date from the 1930s and were
initiated in the Jos Plateau with the objective of supplying local require-
ments of fuelwood which was even then in short supply. Initially, villagers



SUPPLEMENT 5
Page 10
agreed to setting aside areas for fuelwood production, and under the guidance
of FD staff, undertook responsibility for providing their own labor for estab-
lishment and maintenance and effectively protecting the tree blocks. By 1945,
some 1,200 ha had been planted in Jos division, but with limited technical
success because of unsuitable species, poor quality planting stock and inade-
quate supervision. A central nursery was established at Bukuru in 1960 to
supply improved planting stock, of many Eucalyptus species, and in the
ensuing years much of the earlier planting was replaced and the areas of
CFAs increased to about 3,200 ha.
32.       The scheme was extended throughout the former Benue-Plateau State
and CFAs were established in most divisions.  By the 1960s, some 134 CFAs
covering about 103 km2 had been created in what is now Plateau State, mainly
in the Jos and Pankshin divisions, and 138 CFAs covering 31 km2 had been set
aside in what is now Benue State, mainly in the Gboko and Katsina-Ala divi-
sions (see Tables 7 and 8). Only a portion of the areas set aside have
been planted.   No CFAs were established in the Ayangba project area (Ankpa,
Dekina and Idah divisions) and only token planting was carried out in the
Lafia project area. No new CFAs have been created sinced about 1965.
33.       Local support for the scheme, however, diminished, with a reduction
in the amount of voluntary labor, and gradually the FD and LAs increased their
commitment, in many instances paying cash wages to the villagers employed on
the operations and introducing a limited amount of mechanical cultivation.
34.       Problems have arisen with respect to management of the tree blocks
on a sustained cutting cycle, with cases of local people disregarding the
management plans prepared by FD staff; uncontrolled fellings have taken place
with the result that gaps in the canopy caused by felling have allowed weed
growth in the tree blocks that has severely downgraded both the production
and quality of the crop. Harvesting arrangements contribute to the lack of
support since the common practice is for the crops to be sold standing to a
contractor who is then free to resell the produce in markets and at prices of
his choice. Revenue from sales are paid to the LGC and used for community de-
velopment projects such as bridges, water supplies and roads; there is no di-
rect benefit payment to the participating villagers.
35.       New planting in CFAs substantially decline in the laCte16  s anid
there has been a change in attitude by FD staff towards the concept. In the
Jos division, emphasis has moved to establishing pole/fuelwood plantations
in FRs; at one scheme at Heipong, some 40 km from Jos, local farmers have
been paid compensation to free land for plantation development to be carried
out by ED. At the same time, tree seedlings are available for sale to lo-
cal farmers (at N 0.05/seedling) wishing to plant on their farms. In Lafia
division, no planting in CFAs has taken place for over ten years. Govern-
ment is now considering gazetting these CFAs as FRs.
36.       FD is presently experiencing a severe shortage of staff, aggra-
vated by the recent State boundary changes which resulted in several senior
staff transferring to other States. Besides the CCF, there are only two



SUPPLEMENT 5
Page 11
senior field staff (Divisional Forest Officers), against a complement of four,
and insufficient experienced trained technical staff; the position of the
latter is expected to improve in the next two years as trainees complete
their courses at the Jos Forestry School. In addition to FD staff, LGCs
employ junior forestry staff primarily for protection of the FRs, control of
felling in the non-FR areas and revenue collection. FD staff supervise the
work of LGC staff, but have no direct employer/employee relationship.
37.       All forest exploitation of timber is carried out by minor contrac-
tors, using pitsaws and power saws, who are licensed by the LGCs and market
their timber either directly to the end buyer, or more commonly, as baulks
to intermediary traders. Small circular resaw benches are operated by pri-
vate traders in some townships which breakdown the balks to dimension sizes.
A large proportion of the wider size planks (e.g., 12" x 1") are purchased
from the south, and also other dimension stock. Felling is restricted to
non-FR areas, with the objective of retaining the limited stocks of merchan-
table trees in the FRs for future controlled exploitation. Illegal removals
are reported to be significant. Split poles from the borassus palm (Borassus
aethiopum) are used extensively, particularly for roofing supports for
thatched roofs of houses. These have an advantage of termite resistant
heartwood and have been in plentiful supply in the past. The palm is slow
to mature, with an estimated rotation of 60 to 70 years, and is wasteful
in conversion, with about 30% of the split poles becoming mishapen and unsuit-
able for construction. Fast-growing pole crops of Eucalyptus spp. with
simple preservative treatment will provide a more satisfactory and lower cost
product.
38.       Firewood is the major forest product and there are well-established
markets in all the towns visited. In Jos, firewood is transported to the
township from distances over 160 km (100 miles). Much of this is carried
as "'unofficial" cargo behind the cabs of trucks and as top-up loads. Along
the roadsides radiating for up to 50 km (30 miles) or so from the towns,
stacks of bundled firewood are offered for sale by villagers. This trade is
steadily depleting the savanna woodlands. Charcoal is a well established
trade in Jos, but so far has not been adopted elsewhere in the State.
39.       Prices for forest products are high; for sawn timber retail prices
range from US$150/m3for the lowest grade whitewoods to US$250/m3for redwoods
(mahoganies); borassus poles range from US$1/12' bent pole to US$2/14'
straight pole; charcoal retails at US$7.5/100 lb bag in Jos; firewood sells
at US$12/m3(solid) at country roadside and between US$30 and US$50/m3in the
townships. Firewood is a significant item in the family budget of town dwel-
lers and at the estimated consumption of around 4 to 5 m3/family/year, is
likely to account for 10% to 20% of family income.
40.       Foresty Development.  A detailed summary of the Plateau State de-
velopment proposals 1976-1980 is contained in Appendix 4. The main activity
proposed is for Gmelina pulpwood plantations in Doma FR. Earlier in the year,
2,000 acres of the FR was mechanically cleared on contract at a cost of



SUPPLEMENT 5
Page 12
US$348/acre, as an initial planting target. The small adjacent nursery com-
menced in 1975 to supply the plants and has sufficient stock for only a few
hundred acres. Planting, using a stumping method, is likely to result in a
high proportion of failures as stumps were being cut back to an overall size
of some 75-100 mm (3" - 4") instead of the prescribed 150-205 mm (6" - 8")
with weed growth beginning to suppress the stumps before shooting had taken
place. The supervisor of the field operations commutes from Lafia town daily;
there is no on-the-job housing provision nor any basic infrastructure. Local
farm casual labor is employed for the field operations. The Doma FR is not
included in the areas selected for pulpwood development in the Federal plan
and it has not been possible for the mission to establish a rationale for the
project. This aspect and some of the technical/ management problems were
discussed with FD and FDF at the invitation of the CCF, and it is understood
the project will be reapproached with the objective of relating the develop-
ment activities to accord with national plans and undertaking a yearly program
which can be effectively achieved within the manpower constraints.
41.       Other activities in the plan include teak plantations in Ayangba FR,
also in the Lafia division, fuelwood plantations, mainly in Jos and Pankshin
divisions, inventories and surveys and a large investment in game reserves
and the Jos wildlife park.
42.       Benue State.  A tabulation of the resource base data is contained
in Table 8. FRs are recorded as covering 1,756 km3and CFAs 31 km3which to-
gether are about 4% of the land area of the State. There are no CFAs in the
Ayangba project area (formerly Kwara State), but these three divisions con-
tain 95% of the FRs. They also differ from the other divisions of the State
in that ownership rests with the State and not the LGCs.
43.       The Northern Nigerian Timber Corporation (NNTC) sawmill at Ochaja
is the only large-scale forestry industry in the State; there is a small saw-
mill at Makurdi, and elsewhere, minor exploitation is carried out by pitsaw-
yers.  NNTC (formerly Coast Timber Co.) has been operating for some 18 years.
Ownership is now 60% State, 35% LGC and 5% private. The concession area in-
cludes the FRs of Alla, Acharane, Ajofu, W. Okura and Okuralyale (some 382
km2), and merchantable trees in the non-forest areas of Ankpa, Dekina and Idah
divisions.  The sawmill has a capacity of`--17        tyear (400,000 ft ) (sin-
gle shift). Annual log production in the past has been approximately 42,000 m3
(1.2 million ft3), of which 23,000 m3 (0.8 million ft3) was marketed as
roundwood, about half of which (composed mainly of obeche = Triplochiton) was
exported. Since the embargo on exports in March, 1976, the mill has increased
sawmill production for the local market, adding an additional vertical band
breakdown saw, and is currently exploring the possibility of producing pre-
fabricated housing units. Preliminary studies are also being undertaken into
the prospects of establishing an integrated particle board mill utilizing
mill residues and species of Antiaris and Ceiba. The mill employs some 300
mill and logging operatives and has its own village in which more than 50%
of employees reside.



SUPPLEaNT 5
Page 13
44.       The staffing position in Benue is more satisfactory than in Plateau
State, with all divisional forestry officer posts being filled. Most of the
senior staff are newly transferred and in the process of taking up their
territorial duties.
45.       The prices of forest products 3are similar to those in Plateau State;
NNTC prices ex-mill range from US$186/m  for whitewood (for which demand is
limited) to US$260/m3 for mahoganies; pole and fuelwood prices are in the
same ranges previously discussed.
46.       Forest Development.  The development plan 1976-1980 is summarized
in Appendix 5. The main emphasis is on timber plantations development, of
which a major part is scheduled for the FRs in the Ayangba project area. It
is possible that part of this program will be included in the FDF pulpwood
plantation development program for the proposed Ageneboo-Lokoja pulpmill.
Next to timber plantations, fuelwood is given emphasis, particularly in the
Makurdi, Gboko and Katsina-Ala divisions. Other activities include forest
surveys and inventories, fire protection, game control and development of re-
serves, and roads and buildings infrastructure.
C. The Project
47.       A description of the forest areas within the project areas is given
in Appendices 6 and 7. The project forestry activities will be directed to-
wards local supply of products to complement the development program, within
the constraints of a realistic work schedule to be managed by the project
authority, land availability and availability of staff and labor. Proposed
activities will include establishment of plantations for poles (a proportion
of which would be expected to be grown to timber dimensions), and fuelwood;
supply of tree seedlings to farmers, including fruit trees; training courses,
to be conducted at the project training centers, including introductory
courses for forestry staff and courses in the use of tools and their mainten-
ance, simple sawmilling technology, and carpentry for local operators; pro-
vision of technical assistance to small local sawmillers to enable them to
obtain credit; initiation of a charcoal industry in conjunction with land
clearing for plantations establishment; support of national activities in
research, introductory trials and forest development planning. Project
activities are summarized in Table 9.
48.       Plantations Establishment.  In both project areas, the present
stocking of the savannas is generally sufficient for local farm supply of
rough poles and fuelwood for the immediate future. For this reason, and
because CFAs are experiencing serious difficulties at the present time, it
is not expected that the projects will undertake the planting of community
tree blocks, although emphasis will be placed on the sale of seedlings to
farmers (see para 55). Around the main townships, however, the land
has been cleared of trees for a radius of 5 km or more, and both poles and
fuelwood are trad5d goods commanding high prices (upwards of N 0.01/lb for
firewood (US$20/m ) and N 1.0/12' pole of borassus). Moreover, cursory visual
assessment of the tree stocking in the rural areas is deSeptive, since the
actual volume of solid wood, estimated at about 4 to 8 m /ha, is far 'Less



SUPPLEMENT 5
Page 14
than would be expected by the vegetative cover, which is mainly composed of
branchlets and foliage. There are strong indications that over most of the
area, the annual off-take of solid wood is significantly greater than the na-
tural regeneration so that the capital stock of woodlands is being depleted
at an increasing rate, which will result in a progressive shortage of poles
and fuelwood unless steps are takeu to supplement the natural supplies. Fur-
thermore, the poor quality of poles from the natural woodlands in terms of
straightness and length limits the type of construction and other farm uses
(e.g., cattle corrals, sheds, fencing) to which they may be put.
49.       The project will establish pole/fuelwood plantations within 5 km
radius of the major towns so as to secure future supplies at reasonable cost
to these main centers. The techniques developed during the project would es-
tablish standards for forestry management and be used for extending this sys-
tem to other areas of growing deficiency in subsequent development phases.
50.       The operations will be carried out by project staff on land set
aside and titled for forestry. In Lafia, existing forest reserves would be
used except for Awe; in Ankpa, Dekina and Idah (and Awe), suitable areas,
capable of sustaining vigorous tree growth and situated within 5 km of the
main towns, will be selected in consultation with the LGCs.
51.       Produce from the project plantations will be sold at economic stum-
page prices so as to provide refinancing of replacement plantations.
52.       The results of the plantations so far developed in the project
areas and the systematized demonstrations of SFRS, Samaru, will provide
the basic technology for the project. Plantation areas will be surveyed and
access tracks/firebreaks constructed a year or two prior to planting. Any
merchantable trees will be exploited by minor contractors being directed to
these areas and fuelwood removed by local farmers and contractors. The main
species will be Eucalyptus camaldulensis, E. teriticornis (poles and fuelwood),
E. cloeziana (poles and timber), Gmelina arborea (poles, timber and fuelwood).
The denser canopy provided by E. cloeziana and Gmelina arborea would be
utilized to minimize weed infestation and fire hazard by planting these
species along the external portions of the plantations.
53.       Seed supply will be provided by FIFR, using selected seed of known
provenance and a liaison maintained with the project with the objective of
designating selected areas as seed collection areas. SFRS, Samaru, will pro-
vide technical back-stopping for soils survey and evaluation of fertilizer and
trace element deficiencies, particularly boron. The expected rotation for
poles and fuelwood is eight years and average growth rate 15 m3/ha/year.
54.       A later development, to be instituted when the plantations become
yielding, will be the preservation treatment for poles. A simple open-tank
(hot-cold) method, possibly obtaining creosote from the proposed coking-
coal mining industry at Obi, is a likely technique for a wide range of pole
products including construction poles for improved house and farm building,
fencing and stockades. A later development would be pressure treatment for
larger-sized poles for power transmission and telephone cables.



SUPPLEMENT 5
Page 15
55.       Tree Seedlings to Farmers.  The nurseries supplying the planta-
tions program would also raise tree seedlings for sale to farmers and amenity
trees for sale to the LGCs for townships and public buildings. Sales to
farmers would include fruit trees of citrus, mango, coconut, guava, and
cashew. This service has already been established, particularly for citrus,
in parts of the project areas, and the objective will be to expand supply
to meet the demand and at the same time introduce other varieties, e.g.,
paw paw, pineapple, avocado pear.  Initially, supplies will be for local
fruit consumption; a later development could be the expansion of a fruit
growing industry.
56.       Fruit Tree Production.  There are two existing nurseries near Lafia
town; one of almost two acres operated by the LGC was constructed some
12 years ago and has developed a small citrus orchard for producing budding
material; there are proposals for expanding the size of the nursery to five
acres by acquiring adjacent land. The other is a five-acre nursery operated
by MANR which commenced operations in 1972. This is currencly being extended
by a further five acres, finalization of which is contingent on the payment
of compensation to affected farmers. The MANR nursery also handles some
seed multiplication for agricultural crops. Both nurseries have inadequate
irrigation facilities.
57.       Both nurseries produce similar varieties of fruit trees for sale
to farmers. Citrus, mainly orange, is by far the main species, with some
mango, coconut, cola nut, guava and cashew; the MANR nursery is currently
building up stocks of pineapple. Varieties of orange produced include Wash-
ington Naval, Tangello (locally developed) and "King." Root stock is raised
from seed obtained from local farmers (Obi district) and budding material from
the LGC nursery and local farmers; the Government farm at Yandev used to be a
supply source. Sales of orange trees in the current season are limited
by the available supply and are expected to be 5,000 from the LGC nursery
and 4,100 from the MANR nursery. MANR reports more than 17,000 request-orders
from farmers. Sales of other fruit trees range from a few hundred up to
1,000 and are limited by supply. The current prices charged by the nur-
series are:
LGC Nursery                    MANR Nursery
Citrus            N 0.20/tree                    N 0.15/tree
Coconut           N 0.30/tree                    N 0.30/tree
Mango             N 0.30/tree                    N 0.30/tree
Local opinion indicates farmers would willingly pay higher prices.
58.       The technique of grafting citrus has been satisfactorily de-
veloped although the MAENR nursery reports a shortage of budders and both
nurseries are currently without grafting tape and are improvising by using
cut plastic sheeting and raffia binding. Improved varieties of mango in-
clude "Peta," "Julie," and "Alfonso"; the intention is to use local mango



SUPPLEMENT 5
Page 16
as root stock and graft on these, although there have been difficulties in
obtaining budding material and developing a satisfactory technique. Improved
oil palm have been raised in the past and are considered to be an important
crop for local consumption. Pineapple and paw paw are presently considered
to be luxury crops which are not yet common in the diets of local people, but
these, together with avocado pear, are regarded as promising introductions,
initially for home consumption and with the possibility of expanding for
market production in the future.
59.       In Ayangba, there is local demand for cocoa, coffee and oil palm,
in addition to the species discussed, and these will be included in nursery
production. It is estimated farmers would absorb 50,000 to 60,000 fruit
trees/year in the Lafia project and 100,000/year in Ayangba.
60.       Farmers will also be offered seedlings of plantation trees (Eucal-
yPtus and Gmelina spp.) for establishing farm woodlots for pole/fuelwood
production and stakes. The lack of suitable material for staking the yam
crops is a prime reason why this practice is not common. Suitable stakes can
be produced on an annual coppice system using Eucalyptus spp. once the tree
rooting system has been satisfactorily established. A further 50,000 to
60,000 seelings of these species will be produced for sale.
61.       A separate nursery will be established for each of the plantations
to be developed (four to five in Lafia and one each in Ankpa, Dekina and Idah);
these will be amalgamated with the existing agricultural tree nurseries (e.g.,
Lafia).
62.       Charcoal Industry.  At present, the use of charcoal in the project
areas is confined to blacksmiths and laundry men (charcoal irons), with
occasional use by vendors of roasted maize cobs. However, in Jos, there is
a sizeable domestic demand for charcoal and a well-developed charcoal market,
with prices of N 4/45 kg bag upwards. Charcoal has a significant advantage-
over fuelwood in its convenience as a domestic fuel, because it is more
easily handled and stored and contains about double the heat value of well
dried wood. There are good prospects that it would be adopted as fuel in
the larger twowns. From the supply side, the transport costs of charcoal are
less than half those of firewood, and when charcoal is produced as part of a
land clearing operation, it can significantly lower the cost of clearing,
besides utilizing timber which would otherwise be burned to waste.
63.       The project would initiate charcoal production in both project
areas, using both traditional (Jos) earth-kilns and portable steel-kiln
methods, to establish the industry in these areas. Provision is included
for the services of a charcoal specialist consultant to assist in setting
up this component. A technical note prepared by the ongoing FDF/UNDP/FAO
project 1/ on developing portable charcoal kilns is attached as Appendix 8.
1/   NIR/21/546



SUPPLE1IENT 5
Page 17
64.       Training Courses.  The present product standards, and conversion
rates from roundwood to sawnwood, can be significantly improved. Discussions
with local small-scale sawmill operators and carpenters indicate they will
be anxious to attend short courses in tool maintenance, simple sawmill tech-
nology and carpentry.
65.       The FDF sawmill training center at Benin will include a mobile unit
which would be available for periodic short-course instruction in aspects of
sawmilling and tools maintenance. Specialist instructors in carpentry are
available for courses in carpentry from the Trade Schools, and also FDF Work-
shops, Ibadan.
66.       The project would arrange for short residential courses (2-3 weeks)
to be conducted at the project Training Centers in cooperation with the FDF
and Trade Schools. These are expected to be held once yearly, with some
20-25 small-scale operators per course.
67.       Initiation courses would also be given to forestry staff employed
by the project in nursery practice, and extension advice would be given to
farmers on plantation establishment and maintenance.
68.       Credit to Small Forest Industries.  In Lafia township, there is a
well-established small-scale forest industries section comprising four simple
circular saw benches, each under separate ownership, housed in one open shed
with adjacent open sheds in which some 80 independent self-employed carpenters
produce joinery and furniture products. Similar small-scale carpentry shops
occur in most towns in the project areas. Significant improvements in re-
covery rates, productivity and product quality are possible with the introduc-
tion of simple improved technologies (see para 64). The Small Industries
Credit Scheme operated by the Ministry for Industries is expected to provide
credit finance to bonafide applicants wishing to upgrade sawmilling and car-
pentry workshops. The project would assist in processing applications.
69.       National Forestry Program.  The project would assist in the develop-
ment of the national forestry program by supervising the extension of intro-
ductory trials of tree species in the project areas and assisting with com-
plementary planning of national forestry projects.
70.       Management.  The forestry activities will be under the responsibility
of a forestry specialist and team of technically trained forestry staff se-
conded from the State forestry services to the project. Because of the present
severe staff constraint, it is expected the forestry specialist will be in-
ternationally recruited; secondment of sufficient numbers of other staff may
also be difficult and the project would then undertake inservice training
of suitable candidates in collaboration with the State FDs.
71.       Costs.  Costs are summarized in the appraisal reports.  For Lafia,
total costs 1/ are estimated at N 1.69 million. Detailed cost calculations are
in Appendix 9. For Ayangba, total costs are estimated at N 1.17 million. De-
tailed cost calculations are in Appendix 10.
1/   Without physical and price contingencies.



SUPPLEMENT 5
Page 18
72.       Benefits.  The rate of return on the investment in pole and fuelwood
plantations would be 10.4%. Project costs, adjusted for postproject expendi-
tures to complete the growing and harvesting of the plantations over the three
eight-year rotations expected, have been used to calculate the profitability.
Revenues have been based on stumpage rates, which are related to the cost of
growing and are about 50% of what the market would bear, since as discussed in
para 30, present forest tariffs are outdated and have no relation to current
market prices. Details of the calculations are in Appendix 11.
73.       Cost Recovery.  Analysis of project costs indicates that the average
cost to the project of raising tree seedlings would be N 0.13/seedling. De-
tails are in Appendix 12. This compares with current prices charged to farm-
ers of N 0.05/seedling for forest trees and N 0.15-0.30/seedling for fruit
trees (citrus and mango). Full cost recovery on sales of seedlings to farmers
can therefore be achieved with only marginal average increases in the present
charges.  (See para 57.)
74.       Project Implementation.  For successful project implementation the
following aspects will require detailed attention:
(a) Land for nurseries should be set aside.
(b) Acquisition of land for pole/fuelwood plantations in Awe
(Lafia), Ankpa, Dekina and Idah (Ayangba) is necessary,
while other pole/fuelwood plantations would be estab-
lished in already gazetted areas.
(c) A nucleus of trained forestry staff should be seconded to
the project.
(d) Forest tariffs are outdated and should be brought in line
with today's costs of growing forest products.
(e) Tree seedlings should be sold to farmers at full cost,
and prices should be raised to that level.

CENTIL AGIRULTURLL DEVECOFMT PROJETS
Main Uses of Economic Trees in the Proiect Areas
Of Special Value
Butyrospermim parkii (Shea Butter) *       Fruits make Shea Butter
(Local use & export);
Timber for mortars & fixewood.
ELaeis guineensis (Oil Palm) *             Palm Wine tapped from  base
of inflorescence. Also used
for home made Gin.
Palm Oil (local or export)
Shells of Kernels used as fuel.
Leaves for ropes, basket
making, brooms & scrubbing
brushes.
Stem for rafters if no
Borassus available.
Borassus aethiopum (Fan Palm) *            Split Scantlings;
edible fruit; plumule of seed
is highly nutritious and sold
in markets; leaves for
matting & thatching.
Protected Prlmarily for Timber
Afzelia africana *                         Sawn timber, carving mortars,
firewood.
Prosopis africana *                         he preferred species for
charcoal. Also used for
tool handles & poles (terzite
proof ) for building.
Cakes made from endosperm of
seeds (like Parkia).
Mhaya senegalensis (Dry Zone Mahogany) *   Bark used anti-malaria
Timber both sawn and carved
into mortars.
Khaya grandifolia (African Mahogany)       Timber for fumriture and
constructi on.

Mitragyna ciliata                           Construction timber.
Chlorophora excelsa (Iroko)                 Termite resistant, highly valued
(local and export) timber for
furuiture and construction.
Nauclea didderrichii *                      Makes excellent transmission

SUPPLEMENT 5
Appendix 8
Page 1
NIGERIA
CENTRAL AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECTS
Charcoal Production 1/
Preamble
1.        The studies being conducted under the High Forest Development Pro-
ject already indicate clearly that the forests can be more intensively uti-
lized by converting to timber more species and smaller diameter logs. This
is an essential development objective if the rapidly increasing demand for
wood products is to be satisfied from the dwindling forestry resources.
2.        However, this will take some time to fully implement since it
will be dependent on the introduction of new sawmilling and wood processing
machinery, and the restructuring and integration of the forestry industry.
3.        Meantime some immediate measures are necessary, especially where
natural forest has to be converted to intensive industrial plantations for
the pulp and paper industry. At present, the remaining trees are destroyed by
burning after logging, to clear the site. At the same time we are aware of
the ever increasing problems of domestic fuel supply in terms of both avail-
ability and cost, as exemplified today by both kerosine and cooking gas. The
Federal Department of Forestry proposes therefore to initiate charcoal produc-
tion on a moderately large pilot scale, and to utilize for this the wood at
present being destroyed. It also proposes to integrate this project with the
larger forest village and agro-silvicultural project, together with the
introduction of wood-based craft centers as an additional income-generating
activity for the new villages.
4.        The implementation of the project is described in the following
pages together with the costs involved and resulting benefits.
Charcoal Production Project
5.       The manufacture of charcoal in metal kilns has been proposed as part
of the plantation development scheme, to provide an alternative source of fuel
for local residents. The increasing price of kerosine and cooking gas has
caused numerous difficulties.
6.   Currently the local price is N 2.50 per sack of 88 lbs (40 kg) at the
Ibadan Circle Forest Office for charcoal made by traditional methods in the
Ijaiye Forest Reserve.
1/   Draft of Paper Prepared by FDF/UNDP/FAO Project NIR/31/546



SUPPLENENT 5
Appendix 8
Page 2
7.        A proven kiln is a circular model originally designed in Uganda
and used there with some success. It is cylindrical in shape with two nest-
ing sections approximately 2-3 meters in diameter and 1.7 meters high. It
has a conical cover and a number of conduct pipes to provide the air required
for burning and four stacks for the escape of smoke and steam. The kiln it-
self has a cubic volume of 7.1 cubic meters (250 cubic feet).
8.        The Western Nigerian Technical Company in Ibadan provided an estimate
of N 560 in August 1973 for the fabrication of a kiln of this type. For the
purpose of this report the cost is assumed now to be 100% more, i.e., N 1,120,
but a more accurate estimate will be obtained. As the kiln is manufactured
of sheet steel, its weight is considerable, and in Uganda it was rolled on its
,side for short distances or put on a trailer for longer moving.
9.        FAQ reports on the operation of this type of kiln have been pre-
pared as a result of studies made in Uganda and Malagasy.
10.       These reports state the cycle time from charging, firing and cooling
was 72 hours and that a two-kiln unit was the minimum cycle which could be con-
sidered economic as a unit. This size would employ two men full-time. During
firings in East Africa and elsewhere, the average yield of 35% by weight was
achieved.
11.       Thus assuming:
1.   the volume of wood per charge to be 200 cubic feet
to allow for gaps in the pile during charging;
2.   a cycle time of three days;
3.   a two-unit operation;
4.   a yield of 20% by weight;
5.   a kiln life of three years;
the following results are achieved:
N 2240 or N 62.22
1.   Monthly cost of kilns -  36
2.   Wood cost -
Alternative (a) prepared by daily paid crew
3 men for 8 hours per day for 1 month at N 1.50/manday
N 4.50 per day for 30 days          =       N 135.00.

12.       Yield is estimated as follows:
1.   4,000 cubic feet of solid wood;
2.   Weight per cubic feet, 40 lb;
3.   Total wood burned, 160,000 lb;
4.   Yield, 20% or 34,000 lb;
5.   Weight per sack, 80 lb;
6.   Thus 400 sacks per month.
13.       Sales price in Ibadan is N 2.50 per sack, but price at site excludes
transport cost of, say N 75 per trip and 4 trips needed, i.e., N 300 per month
or N 0.43 per bag.
14.       Thus, the price at the manufacturing site is N 2.07 with a production
cost of N 0.62 or a profit of N 1.45 per bag (N 580.00 per month).
15.       The size of the wood used in the kiln should be fairly regular in
size and billets should be from 2 to 4 feet long and up to 1 foot in diameter.
Wood should be cut 3 or 4 weeks before burning to reduce the moisture con-
tent.



SUPPLEMENT 5
Appendix 8
Page 4
16.       The demonstration areas will be approximately 200 ha in extent.
The indica5ive inventory gives the average volume of 40 cm d.b.h. 1/ and
over, 70 m /ha. Branch wood and small trees wsuld increase this volume con-
siderably.  There thus should be at least j0 m  per hec are of wood utilizable
for charcoal making, or a total of 4,000 3  (140,000 ft ).  Each two unit kiln
utilizes 4,000 ft per month or 48,000 ft in a 12-month operating season.
Thus 6 kilns (3 two-kiln units) could be used in the demonstration area.
17.       Note:  all costs, wages and yields have been estimated and could be
checked by staff. If a unit of 6 kilns were established, it is recommended
that an ACF be put in charge. Also, a proper design should be obtained since
refinements to the kilns have been made, such as a grating for the bottom, etc.,
which have increased yields and efficiency. The foregoing, however, presents
an introduction to the subject which can be used in the preliminary stages of
preparing the demonstration program.
18.       A reference used in preparing the foregoing was a pamphlet entitled
"Latest Techniques in the Treatment of Natural High Forest in South Mango Dis-
trict," prepared by D.E. Earl, published by the Government Printer, Entebbe,
Uganda, in 1968. 2/
Estimates of Expenditure for Charcoal Project
19.       Much of the expenditure on this project has already been included
in the agro-silviculture project because the two must be seen as an inte-
grated whole.
20.       It is assumed that 32 men will work at each center, with a Charcoal
Overseer and Forestry Assistant for supervision and record maintenance.
The cost of staff, labor and housing have already been allowed for. It has
also been assumed that the charcoal will be transported from the field when
the tractor and trailer are not working on the agricultural crop. For this
reason the charcoal burning will have to be done in the dry weather, after the
maize is harvested (approximately October to February). From March to July the
labor will be involved in cutting wood, and in August and September in assist-
ing with the maize harvest.
21.       The following expenditure is therefore further required:
Capital Expenditure (1976/77 only)

NIGERIA
CENTRAL AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECTS
SEED MULTIPLICATION
General
1.        Present seed multiplication activities in the project areas are
divided into three stages of bulking; all three stages involve multiplying
seed received from research stations. The first stage is usually carried out
by the Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources (MANR) on its farm
centers. Depending on the yield obtained and anticipated demand from farmers,
a second multiplication may be carried out on the farm centers or contracted
out to farmers. TWhere demand for seed is high enough, further multiplication,
the third stage, is always carried out by contract farmers.
2.        Contract farmers sign an agreement with MANR.  Farmers are usually
provided with tractor services and farm inputs, including seed, at the normal
cost, and they have to pay for these at harvest plus a 5% interest charge.
They are also obliged to sell all produce from the stipulated acreage to .XAN-R
at agreed prices.
3.        Contracting farmers are usually assisted in planting and fertilizer
application by an Agricultural Instructor (Al) who works under the supervision
of the Divisional Agricultural Officer. Weeding, rogueing, spraying, harvest-
ing and cleaning are carried out by the farmers without assistance but under
continual supervision of the Al. Seed purchased from contract growers is
bulked, treated and stored in air-tight drums. Sales to farmers are made
through farmers' groups but some seed is reserved for emergency demands and
for farmers who do not belong to groups. The price of seed to farmers is the
same as the price at which it is purchased from growers, with Government
absorbing collection, treatment and storage costs.
4.        In Lafia, seed multiplication is carried out at the Lafia Farm
Center and at the two irrigation centers at Dep and Rutu. In Ayangba two farm
centers are involved in seed multiplication. These are at Otobi and Ochaja
and are mainly involved with multiplication of groundnuts and maize seed. An
important center for seed multiplication for the whole of Benue State is the
Yandev Farm Center and Training College. The Yandev Farm Center is mainly
responsible for bulking seed from research stations for distribution for Stage
III type multiplication by farmers. Seed production at Yandev covers maize,
sorghum, rice, benniseed, soyabeans, groundnuts, cowpeas and kenaf. It
also raises seedlings of oil palm, citrus, mango, coconut and cashew. The
Yandev Farm Center is situated five miles north of Aboko in Benue State.
Though some distance from both project areas, it has sufficient land resources
(a total area of 240 hectares, and another adjacent 260 hectares have been
approved for acquisition) and other facilities.



SUPPLEMNT 6
Page 2
Seed Ifultiplication for Project Crops
5.        The main crops for which seed or planting material will be required
are cassava, rice, maize, melons, benniseed, cowpeas and perhaps sorghum,
if acceptable new varieties are identified.
6.        Yams.  Although development projections have been based on tech-
nology that already exists, IITA confidently expects to have new stable lines
of yams for general release during the project development period. The
potential of new technology has been described in Supplement 2. In the event
of new technology becomina available for farmers, propagation of yam seeds
could easily be undertaken by the farmers themselves under supervision of the
project and with assistance from IITA. This could easily be accommodated
under the seed multiplication component of the projects.
7.        Cassava.  The project has to produce improved planting materials
for farmers during most of its life, although farmers will be encouraged to
produce their own. Like yams, the system does not require too large an area,
as it is estimated that a plot of two acres should be enough for the produc-
tion of 50,000 cuttings. The project should concentrate on material for
demonstration rather than try to organize the complete supply of planting
material. Farmers' demand for improved material is likely to rise very
rapidly and an early effort should be made to encourage farmers' groups to
establish nurseries for their own needs. Since it may be difficult initially
to get farmers to harvest cuttings before they are ready to harvest the
tubers, the projects would establish enough nurseries to supply seedlings in
Years I and 2.
8.        Maize and Rice.  It would be unnecessary for the project to estab-
lish a system of multiplication different from the present; attention should
be applied to examining and correcting the practical pitfalls in this system.
One area that needs strengthening is collection, cleaning, dressing and
storage; for example, since the viability of maize seed can be sharply reduced
under warm humid conditions, maize for seed should be grown only in the late
season. This would also allow for efficient drying (sun-drying) to a moisture
content of 12% or lower, and for storage during the dry season. Also, to be
sure that only seed from seed farms is sold to the project, it will be neces-
sary to have a mobile sheller that can be moved into the farm to shell the
harvested seed for immediate collection by the project. In the case of rice
it would be advisable if the project supplied pedal threshers to seed produ-
cers. For all grain crops there is also an urgent need for seed cleaning
facilities, while for general seed production there is a need for a moderate-
sized seed-testing laboratory.
9.        There is, however, a need for the project to produce some foundation
seed of experimental material which project research found promising. In the
case of maize, new and improved varieties are constantly being tried, and an
area should be set aside for the project itself to multiply foundation seed.
Rice would, however, pose some problems because of its demand for specific



SUPPLEMENT 6
Page 3
soil types. Production of this will therefore mainly be by farmers on their
own farms but with the help and under the supervision of project management,
but the project would also have an area available at the project farm. Rice
seed production may also be contracted out to Government projects at Dep or
Rutu.
10.       Benniseed.  Like maize, the project would have to make provision
for producing foundation seed of improved varieties. Further multiplica-
tion can be carried out by farmers themselves. Because of the small area
under the crop at present, there is need to change seed only about once in
five years, and because benniseed is generally self-pollinated, the project
needs to set aside only two hectares for bennised seed production.
11.       Melon.  Initial bulking of seed of improved cultivars, as they
are released from research institutions and tested on project experimental
farms, would have to be undertaken on project seed multiplication farms.
Unlike benniseed in Lafia, melon is not only grown by a large number of
farmers in Ayangba, but its importance is rapidly increasing. Demand for
improved types, therefore, is likely to be high. Since melon is predominant-
ly cross-pollinated, and since seed requirement per hectare is small, it would
be best if project seed.requirements were met from the project farm.
12.       Cowpeas.  Seed will have to be changed every three years and area
requirements would be limited since seed production would also be done at
contract farms. However, it will be necessary to set aside some area on
the project farm for bulking new improved varieties resulting from project
research efforts.
13.       Other Crops.  Of the other crops grown in the areas not specifi-
cally part of the project effort, perhaps the more promising ones in the diets
and farming system are groundnuts and pigeon peas. It would be worth alloca-
ting some area for producing improved varieties, in case farmers show some
interest in them. The project farm would be large enough to accommodate
this.
14.       Tree Crops.  The project will maintain the existing tree crop
nurseries for sale of seedlings to farmers. The nurseries will, however,
be-expanded to produce seedlings of forest trees and other fruit trees pre-
sently not grown in the nurseries. For more details see Supplement 5.
15.       Seed requirements and areas needed for seed multiplication (both on
the project farm and on contract farms) for both projects are detailed in
Appendix 1.
Organization of the Seed Multiplication Program
16.       The emphasis would be on multiplying seed by contract farmers,
and since a system of contracting seed production to farmers already exists in
the areas, the existing systems would be examined and improved under the
project so as to ensure good, clean and viable seed.



SUPPLEMENT 6
Page 4
17.       For such a program, a graduate trained in seed technology will be
needed for each of the projects. Since this type of training requires a
relatively short time (three to six months), arrangements would be made to
send a graduate or fairly senior officer for training in Year 0. This officer
will be responsible for organizing seed production under the project. His
major tasks would be to ensure the purity and viability of seed sold to
farmers.
18.       There will be two main aspects of the project, the field production
aspect and the laboratory processing and storage aspect. The field aspect
will be under the supervision of Agricultural Assistants and Agricultural
Instructors. The processing and storage aspects can be handled by a seed
technologist with the support of experienced storekeepers and laborers.
19.       Excluding cassava and yams, the total acreage of seed grown by
farmers would be built up to about 300 ha for Lafia and 500 ha for Ayangba
(see Appendix 1). One Agricultural Assistant, supported by two Agricultural
Instructors, should be capable of supervising 50 ha. One AA and one AI
will be assigned to the project seed farm. Training for short periods at
existing research centers will also be required. Detailed staffing require-
ments for the two projects on the basis of these assumptions are in Table 1.
Zonal employment of field staff would be used. Inputs for seed multiplica-
tion would be provided and seed purchased from farmers would be assembled in
selected farm service centers before transmission to project seed storage
centers.
20.       Seed requirements for Year 1 will have to be contracted out at
existing national and state institutions with enough facilities to produce
good seed, such as Yandev Farm Center, as it is not possible to completely
organize the seed multiplication program in Year 0.
21.       Since it is important to evacuate seed from farmers' farms as
soon as possible after harvest, it is recommended that simple moveable
threshers be acquired by the project, sent to the seed farms for threshing,
and that the seed then be carted to project stores. It is also recommended
that seed be cleaned, tested for purity, treated against pests and dressed
before storage. Before sale to farmers, seed should also be tested for
viability. A seed laboratory will be needed for these activities, and provi-
sion has been made for this together with the necessary equipment. An equip-
ment list is in Table 2.
