NIGERIA
CENTRAL AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECTS
RESEARCH
General
1.        Research in the project areas comes under the three main research
institutions in the country (see also paras 6 to 10). These institutions are
the Federal Department of Agricultural Research (FDAR), the Institute of
Agricultural Research (IAR) and the International Institute of Tropical
Agriculture (IITA). These institutions design the field experiments, supply
the necessary inputs for these experiments and analyze the results, but
the actual experiments are carried out by staff of State Ministries of Agri-
culture and Natural Resources (MANR).
2.        In each State's MANR, an Agricultural Officer is in charge of re-
search, and normally, one Agricultural Assistant is employed for research
purposes in each division under the AO's supervision. It is this Agricultural
Assistant who actually lays out trial plots, supervises labor and records
observations in log books provided by the research institutions. Trials are
conducted at MANR "farm centers" and there is normally a farm center near each
divisional headquarters.  In the Lafia area there is a farm center near Lafia
Town with a subcenter near Doma, and seven separate trials were being con-
ducted in 1976. In the Ayangba area there is one farm center each in Ankpa,
Dekina, and Idah. Respectively, seven, eight and four separate trials were
conducted in 1976.
3.        A log book is kept for each trial and is sent back to the appro-
priate research institution at the end of the season for analysis of results.
IAR at Samaru regularly (every January/February) convenes a cropping scheme
conference to discuss the results of previous year's trials and to program for
the next season's.
4.        Research presently carried out in the project areas under the
system described above may be grouped as follows:
(a) soil fertility trials - fertilizer and micronutrient studies;
(b) varietal trials;
(c) spacing trials;
(d) intercropping and farming systems trials involving maize,
sorghum, cowpeas, groundnuts, yams, cassava and soyabeans;
(e) insecticide and fungicide trials involving the use of ULV
sprayers;



SUPPLE}ENIT 7
Page 2
(f) sowing date trials;
(g)  disease observation trials - leaf spot on groundnuts and leaf
blight on maize;
(h)  nematode survey, on groundnuts;
(i)  ridging versus mounding, with yams.
5.        Although a number and variety of trials are conducted in the project
area, more applied research into problems peculiar to the project is required.
With the recent improvements in communications in Nigeria, accessibility
from the project areas to the established research institutions should improve,
and closer links between project research needs and work undertaken at the
research institutions should be possible.
Existing Research Institutions
6.        Except for normal research work carried out by faculties of agricul-
ture of the various Nigerian universities, three main institutions are engaged
in foodcrop research in Nigeria.
7.        The Institute of Agricultural Research (IAR).  This institute,
attached to the Ahmadu Bello University at Zaria, is situated in Samaru.
TAR was responsible for research in the whole of the former State of Northern
Nigeria, and therefore its main research efforts are concentrated on problems
in the more northerly areas of Nigeria. However, a number of its research
results could be applied to the project areas. Research work at IAR covers
cereals, grain legumes, oil seeds and cotton.
8.        The Federal Department of Agricultural Research (FDAR).  This
is part of the National Research Council with headquarters in Ibadan. Its
main station is in Moore Plantations, but it has a number of outstations
in various other parts of the country. Its area of influence, earlier confined
to the former Western State, is now gradually spreading throughout the country.
FDAR research activities cover cereals, such as maize and rice, root crops and
grain legumes. FDAR also provides support to Federal programs, such as the
National Accelerated Food Production Program (NAFPP) operated by the Federal
Ministry of Agriculture and Rural Development (FMARD). No formal link exists,
however, between FDAR and FMARD.
9.        The International Institute of Tropical Agriculture (IITA).  The
IITA is situated a few miles north of Ibadan. It is an international institu-
tion with responsibilities for research in low altitude tropics throughout
Africa. As such, it is one of the institutes benefitting from the Consulta-
tive Group on International Agricultural Research. However, because most
IITA work is carried out in Nigeria, it has a significant influence on agricul-
ture there. IITA works closely with FDAR and it even seconded some staff to
it. IITA was founded in 1970. In the short period since its establishment,
IITA has made some very significant strides in a number of areas such as root
crops (cassava and yams), maize, and grain legumes such as cowpeas and pigeon
peas.



SUPPLEDNT 7
Page 3
10.       No specific research stations or substations exist in the Lafia
project area, but there are two small substations of specialized Nigerian
research institutions in the Ayangba project area. The Cocoa Research Insti-
tute of Nigeria (CRIN) has a field station at Ochaja, where it studies such
crops as cocoa, kola, coffee and cashew. The Nigerian Institute for Oil
Palm Research (NIFOR) maintains a varietal trial at Acharu. Both operations
are, however, small and marginal, compared with research done in the insti-
tutes' main stations.
Project Applied Research
11.       Because of the lack of properly organized applied research specifi-
cally geared to the need of the project areas, there is a need to establish
an applied research unit under each project. This unit would be staffed by
qualified and experienced staff, but would maintain close liaks with existing
research institutions. The emphasis of the research activities would be
applied research and pure research activities would be avoided. Current
national trials undertaken in the project areas would be incorporated in the
projects' research programs and would receive improved supervision. Based on
experience with the existing trials, the project research officer would make
recommendations to the research institutions for their improvement. The
projects will apply for participation in international trials such as those
carried out by IITA, as well as continue to participate in national trials.
12.       The projects need to emphasize certain areas of research activity
in view of the distinct ecological conditions of the project areas as opposed
to those prevailing in the two other major ecological zones of the country;
areas which need special attention are detailed below.
13.       Variety Trials are needed to select improved varieties, especially
for those crops to be developed under the project, but also for those crops
not specifically included in project development proposals. For the latter,
few improved varieties have yet been found which would be suitable for the
area's environmental conditions and taste preferences, but further research
could change that in the future. In the process of selecting varieties,
consideration should be given to both their adaptability to the ecology of
the area and their ability to fit into the farming systems of the areas.
14.       Soil Fertility Studies.  The project areas' soils, derived from
sedimentary rocks, are quite different from those of the main research centers.
The projects, moreover, are geared towards improving the inherent low fertility
of the soils with inorganic fertilizers. There is, therefore, a need to
strike a compromise between a high NPK content of the soil and its needs for
micronutrients and organic matter for the optimum performance of the various
crops, and it is also necessary to examine the effect of increased soil
fertility on weed growth and competition. This latter aspect will be parti-
cularly important in the Ayangba area in view of the seriousness of spear
grass in the area.



SUPPLEMENT 7
Page 4
15.       Seasonal soil samples would be taken from sample farms and sent
for analysis to one of the research institutes. Results would be correlated
with fertilizer inputs, crop responses (yields), types and quantity of weed
growth, as well as with the cropping system followed on the sample farms.
The effect of alternatives to the bush/fallow system in relation to soil
fertility would also be studied, using such leguminous cover crops as colo-
pogonium, pueraria, centrocema, and stylosanthes either in single or in
mixture, or even pigeon peas as end-of-rotation crop, although the ability of
this crop to smother spear grass and other weeds may not be as high as cover
crops.
16.       Farming Systems Studies.  Although in theory included in existing
trials, the prevailing view is that present trials do not reflect the actual
farming systems of the project areas. This needs careful examination and
if necessary more appropriate experimental designs have to be made for the
project areas. Minimum tillage systems with potential for planting on the
flat and reduction in labor demands for weeding need to be investigated; and
ridging versus mounding for yams should also be investigated, at least for
yams, if planting with setts grown from seeds becomes viable in the project
area.
17.       Mechanization.  Since labor constraints determine to a large extent
the holding size in the project areas, there is a need to study the suitabi-
lity of hand-operated or small motorized farm equipment for the area. Inter-
esting work in this field is now in progress at IITA, and the projects could
benefit from these trials. However, it is not expected that these trials
would produce immediate results or suddenly increased demand in mechanization
by project area farmers. Therefore, except for proposals detailed in Supple-
ment 3, no mechanization is included under the project.
18.       Minor Crops.  A number of crops grown in the project area have
not been considered worthy of too much attention. Nevertheless, break-
throughs in technology might occur and induce applied research programs. For
instance, work on pest resistance in cowpeas in a number of research institu-
tions needs to be followed up by the projects, so as to test suitability of
these varieties for the project areas. The same is true for pigeon peas, the
new varieties of which were not yet accepted in the project area although the
crop itself is important in some parts of the Ayangba area. Other trials
should involve such legumes as winged beans, yam beans and lima beans, as
production potential of some of the varieties is great.
19.       Farm Management Studies.  There is an almost complete lack of
information on farm management practices which is needed to provide a better
understanding of the farmers' main problems. The project itself will make
major efforts to study the farm management systems through its evaluation
unit. The latter unit will therefore be specifically responsible for these
studies. Work on farm management studies is done elsewhere in Nigeria--there
is a proposed FAO project and in addition, graduate university students
show interest in specific studies. The latter would be of special interest



SUPPLEMENT 7
Page 5
and the projects would favorably regard any requests from Nigerian universi-
ties to study farm management systems in the project areas. For example,
since the Ayangba project is quite close to the University of Nsukka, it could
easily maintain close relations with the University.
20.       Applied research would be conducted on the projects' research plots.
The aim would be to have sufficient land available adjacent to the projects'
seed multiplication centers. The farm manager of the seed multiplication
center would also oversee labor used on the research plots and equipment would
be used both for seed multiplication and research. For reasons of efficiency,
therefore, there would be one project farm for both nucleus seed multiplication
and research. The project farm would be under a farm manager.
21.       However, trials would be undertaken not only on the project farms
but also on smallholdings to examine the applicability of research findings
under actual farm practices. Such trials would be very simple so that they
can be easily supervised by properly instructed extension staff.
22.       Project research will be under the responsibility of a senior agro-
nomist supported by a soil technologist and some research assistants (at
Agricultural Assistant level). The latter will be mainly responsible for
supervising farmers' trials.
23.       As mentioned above, the projects would also participate in national
research schemes and would work closely with the existing research institutes.
Data collected by the project will--if necessary-be processed in the existing
data processing facilities of the research institutes or agricultural facul-
ties of universities. In addition, the Agricultural Projects Monitoring,
Evaluation and Planning Unit (APMEPU) in Kaduna has an agronomy section
responsible for crop recommendations, liaison for research, etc. The project
research officers would be obliged to maintain close contact and liaison with
APMEPU and APMEPU's Chief Agronomist would organize regular meetings (at least
once every two months) of all project research officers. In addition seminars
on various crops could be organized in the different project areas. A lump
sum allowance has been made under each project to cover the costs of various
visits to research institutes and to allow for the organization of crop
seminars.



SUPPLEMENT 8
Page 1
NIGERIA
CENTRAL AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECTS
TRAINING
A. General
1.        The project's training program is of fundamental importance to the
long-term success of the development proposals it will initiate. An experi-
enced and qualified Chief Training Officer will, therefore, be recruited
and supported with adequate training staff to permit the conduct of a range
of training programs encompassing both the farming community and project
staff.
2.        The project's training program will consist of the following
components:
(a)  Institutional.  Establishing a training center at the
respective project headquarters. In Lafia this training
center would allow training of about 40 Agricultural
Instructors per annum. Under the Ayangba project the
existing Ochaja training center would be expanded and
improved to accomodate about 60 students.
(b)  Staff.
-    The provision of scholarships for senior staff;
-    Upgrading courses for various staff categories;
-    Residential courses for training Agricultural
Instructors;
-    Introductory courses for all project staff;
-    Refresher courses; and
-    Specialized training.
(c) Farmers.
-    Mobile units (two units in the Lafia project and
three in the Ayangba project) which would visit
two villages per day and which would include audio-
visual teaching aids in their methodology.



SUPPLEMENT 8
Page 2
Day courses at Farmers Service Centers.
Limited residential courses in the project training
center. This would be undertaken later in the
project period.
An allowance for production of programs over re-
gional radio networks.
A program to inform teachers about agricultural de-
velopment.
-    One mobile home economics team, phased later in the
project period.
3.        Adequate training facilities already exist for Agricultural Assist-
ants and Superintendents. Output of graduate students for the next few years
is detailed in Table 1 for the Lafia Project and Table 2 for the Ayangba
Project.
4.        Organization of agricultural training in Nigeria is detailed in
Chart 16326 attached to this supplement, and some details are given below.
5.        Diploma Courses.  Two-year diploma courses for Agricultural Super-
intendents and Higher Superintendents are provided by FMG. Diploma courses
relevant for project personnel are given at the following institutions:
-    Federal Division of Agriculture and Livestock Service
Training with facilities in Samaru, Kabba, and Kaduna.
Subjects taught include general agriculture, farm
management, agricultural mechanics, and horticulture.
-    Federal Soil and Water Conservation School in Jos.
-    Federal School of Agriculture located in Umudike/Umahia.
6.        All students receive a ten-week training session in official agri-
culture services. Students wishing to enter project service after completing
their course would be obliged to carry out in-course and practical work on the
projects during which their suitability for future employment would be as-
sessed.
7.        Certificate Courses.  Two-year certificate courses are designed
for Agricultural Asssistants and are given at the following FMG or State
Institutions:



SUPPLEMENT 8
Page 3
Federal School of Agriculture at Samaru offering
courses in the following subjects: irrigation and
agronomy, general agriculture, and agricultural
mechanics.
Federal School of Agriculture at Kabba for general
agriculture.
School of Agriculture at Yandev run by Benue State
Government for General Agriculture (Extension)
Certificate.
8.        The Yandev training schedule includes a three-month practical
assignment in agricultural services. Students undergoing certificate training
would also be encouraged to undertake practical work on the projects in which
their suitability for future employment would be assessed. The Yandev Center
includes 73 ha of farmland, and instruction includes practical work on seed
multiplication, research and production activities, which take place on an
additional 240 ha held by the Center. Buildings (classrooms and dormitories)
were built in 1973, but are inadequate for future needs. Construction of new
classrooms will commence before the end of 1976 but dormitory accomodation
remains a bottleneck. Dutch Aid has provided a few teachers and funds for
housing and equipment but continued Dutch financial assistance for Yandev
after completion of tour of duty of the present teachers is uncertain.
Principal subjects taught in Yandev's Agricultural Assistant (Extension)
Certificate Course are: agricultural extension, farm management, crop protec-
tion, agricultural engineering, horticulture, animal science and administration.
9.        In 1975-76 two refresher courses for agricultural staff were run:
one ten-day course on the operation of experimental work and one four-day
course on seed control and the use of chemicals. The availability of students
from Yandev is set out in Table 3.
10.       The Certificate Course can be preceded by a pre-technical course,
the aim of which is "to enable students with no scientific background hitherto,
to acquire the fundamental scientific principles upon which agriculture is
based." The subjects taught are biology, chemistry, geography, mathematics,
physics, and English.
11.       Instructor Certificate Courses.  The States operate three-term
certificate courses during the agricultural year (April-October) for Agricul-
tural Instructors. Plateau State operates two Farm Training Centers at
Gridan Adamu (Shemdam Division) and Abatie. Benue State operates a training
center at Ochaja near Ayangba.
12.       Farm Institute.  Plateau State operates a Farm Institute at
Nassarawa Division for advanced farmers. No school performance is required
to gain entry. However, throughput is very low and few who were schooled
at the Institute establish themselves as farmers in spite of encouragements
such as credit facilities, land provision, etc.



SUPPLEMENT 8
Page 4
B. Staff Training
13.       Senior Staff.  From time to time, in conjunction with the Project
Manager, the Training Officer will review the inservice training performances
of both trainees and trainers. This will be especially indepth for all
positions occupied by expatriates so as to ensure that disengagement of
expatriates will be able to proceed as scheduled without disruption in manage-
ment continuity. Following each review the number of staff to go to overseas
or Nigerian courses would be determined. Development of local management
capability would be an important objective of the project and training of
senior staff would be in that direction. Under the project allowance has been
made for overseas scholarships. However, the State Governments concerned
would ensure that Nigerian staff of high technical and managerial potential
were seconded or recruited to key local appointments.
14.       Intermediate Level Training.  In conjunction with the relevant
department head, from time to time the Training Officer will review with
divisional and sectional heads the performance of all intermediate staff. The
training officer will devise such training programs as may be necessary for
such staff and this may include assistance in taking correspondence courses or
by assuring the release of staff on bonded guarantee of continued employment
on their return, to permit them to attend more formalized training courses.
15.       A close liaison would be maintained with other agricultural educa-
tional and research institutions and direct contact between project staff and
members of such institutions would be promoted. Nevertheless, the main thrust
of training such staff will be centered on inservice training and the
Training Officer will monitor this process for all departments.
16.       Introductory Training.  The Training Officer will be responsible
for providing short introductory training sessions for all staff. Introduc-
tory training sessions will be held periodically throughout the year to
ensure that all incoming staff are adequately aware of the aims and objectives
of the project and of their own individual jobs within it.
17.       Residential Course for Extension Workers.  During the early years
of the project the main thrust of the project's training program would be
on the training of extension personnel (Agricultural Instructor level). A
residential center to accommodate 40 trainees would be established at Lafia.
For Ayangba, the existing center at Ochaja would be expanded and improved to
accommodate 60 trainees. The training program would be carried out under the
direction of the Training Officer. In Lafia the training team would consist
of three full-time training instructors assisted by two experienced but lower-
grade field workers who would assist specifically in teaching practical field
work. In Ayangba there would be four instructors and three field workers.
Training staff would be selected for their experience, field knowledge and
training techniques. At least one training instructor would be of diploma
level and the remaining two would be certificate holders; but all training



SUPPLElfENT 8
Page 5
instructors would possess wide experience and ability. Provision will be made
for other specialist instructors (either project staff or outsiders) covering
subjects, such as, for example, mechanics or carpentry, in which the resident
instruction team may not be skilled. Interns would receive a 45-week training
period (i.e., one year including holidays) beginning the third week of January
and ending the second week of December. Approximately 2/3 of training time
would be devoted to practical field work of which about 1/4 would consist of
intensive instructional work at the training center and the remainder would
take place on project demonstration and seed multiplication holdings.
18.       The training timetable is detailed in Table 4 and a curriculum is in
Appendix 1. This curriculum would encompass all aspects of the technical work
of extension workers and in addition would provide a wide background in the
overall aspects of agricultural development in the area. Each project division
would assist in training Agricultural Instructors by providing some inputs in
their specific fields.
19.       A certificate would be awarded to each successful trainee and
a high standard of passmark would be insisted upon. Such certificates would
be officially recognized as of equal value to those issued by other institu-
tions providing training for Agricultural Instructors and would thus have the
same career advancement value; the Training Officer would ensure that State
Governments accept these principles.
20.       Refresher Courses.  Refresher courses will be run as and when
required. Exact requirements cannot be known in advance, but will be de-
termined jointly by department heads and the Training Officer. It is ex-
pected that most refresher training will be inservice. Refresher courses
or sessions will be mostly technically oriented.
21.       Specialized Training.  Specific training programs will be conducted
for project staff in such fields as credit, marketing, forestry and fisheries.
These courses would be mainly the responsibility of the relevant head of
department or division, but would be coordinated with the Training Officer;
project training facilities would also be used for specialized training.
C. Farmer Training
22.       Mobile Units.  Farmer training will be carried out mainly at village
level by peripatetic training teams operating under the direction of the
Training Officer. No specific facilities would be provided at village level
and the training process would concentrate on field appreciation of cultural
practices being undertaken by the villagers themselves. Each peripatetic
training unit would be supplied with a van equipped with a loudspeaker system
and audiovisual aids, and would consist of a training instructor, and a
driver. Units would operate in the field for four days a week, using the



SUPPLEMENT 8
Page 6
week, using the fifth day for coordination and reporting to the Training
Officer and for vehicle and equipment maintenance and servicing. Two villages
would be covered by each unit on each field day. Two units would operate in
the first year and for Ayangba this would be increased to three in Year 2.
Thus approximately 60 villages could be visited per month which would permit
sufficiently frequent coverage to ensure that the agricultural calendar was
adequately covered for all project farmers. Field extension staff for the
area would attend visits of the peripatetic team and would thereby automati-
cally upgrade their own knowledge.
23.       Day-Courses.  A system of one-day courses would also be employed
as a tool of farmer training. Groups of farmers from a location together with
their agricultural extension personnel would be transported in the project
training bus to points outside their locations, where one-day training sessions
would be conducted. One day courses would be held in Farmers Service Centers,
but would include visits to progressive farmer plots or other demonstration
centers. Day courses would commence in Year 2.
24.       Residential Training.  It is envisaged that from Year 4 residential
training for farmers would be desirable and this could be conducted at a small
satellite training center attached to the project training center. Provision
for two 20-bed residential farmers training centers is provided in Year 4, by
which time the project would have trained adequate staff to man these centers.
It is likely that as the project progresses there will be a need for special-
ized farmer training (e.g. tractor or village water supply maintenance). This
would also be provided as necessary in the expanded training center through
one-week residential courses.
25.       Use of Mass Media.  The Training Officer would investigate ways
and means whereby the mass media could be utilized for farmer training.
In particular this applies to the use of the radio and to the inclusion
of programs specifically designed for project farmers. In addition, use
would be made of information services, newspapers, etc. Funds have been
provided to cover any financial requirement of utilizing such services.
26.       Training within the Education System.  The Training Officer would
establish contacts with secondary and primary teachers in the region with
the intension of ensuring that teachers become aware of the functions and
intensions of the project so that they would be better able to disseminate
the concepts of the project - not only in the classroom, but more especially
in their role as leaders in the village communities.
27.       Home economics.  Later in the project-period (Years 4 and 5), a home
economics team will be included in the training team. This would also operate
as a peripatetic team, and home economics instruction would be coordinated to
a large extent with the other mobile units so that home economics instruction
for women would take place at the same time as agricultural training for
men.

Appendix I
Page 1
NIGERIA
CENTRAL AGRICILTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECTS
Training Center: Curriculum for Al Training I/
General
1.        It is intended to commence staff training for Agricultural Instruc-
tors (AI) as soon as possible after project inception. Courses would have a
strong practical bias which will be increased towards the end of each 45-week
training period. Assessment of the suitability of trainees will be judged by
character and overall personal performance during training in addition to
written and oral examinations.
2.        Training Balance.  A balance will be maintained throughout each
course between practical and theoretical work which will be inter-linked to
the agricultural calendar. It is proposed that approximately three hours each
day will be devoted to practical work during lecture weeks and nearly one week
out of two to practical assignment.
3.        Permanent Evaluation.  A running evaluation would be maintained
throughout the course on trainee performance and the effectiveness of the
training course. The objectives will be to:
-    appraise and evaluate continuously the performance of
trainees in on-the-job situations;
-    improve curriculum content and training methods;
-    summarize training experience and responses; and
-    prepare future course work based on past findings.
4.        Instructor's Handbook.  Within two years of the commencement of
these training courses a handbook of technical and practical memoranda will be
prepared by training staff, in conjunction with other senior project staff,
which will be published in two forms: for use as a school book, and as a
professional instructor's handbook.
5.        Agricultural Training Calendar.  The training calendar will be
coordinated into the agricultural year to ensure effectiveness of classroom
and practical training. The training year will therefore be divided into four
sessions separated by one-week holiday breaks.
1/   The curriculum is presented as a schedule in Table 4.



SUPPLEIMENT 8
Appendix 1
Page 2
Period I - Pre-technical course, dry season and permanent activities;
Period II - Annual and early crops;
Period III - Late crops and extension work;
Period IV - Crop storage and marketing, extension work and management.
Syllabus
6.        The following syllabus represents the broad pattern of teaching
around which the Training Officer will build his actual training schedule:
A. Period 1
(a)  Introductory Courses.  These courses will introduce trainees to the
policies and functions of the project in its integrated role in the
area.   Trainees will learn how their specific role fits into the
overall purposes of the project. A curriculum will be prepared and
explained.
(b)  Plant Production
-    use of agricultural tools (hand tools, sprayers, etc.)
-    manures and fertilizers
-    seedbed preparation for vegetables and tree seedlings
-    plowing, ridging or heaping, preparation of seedbeds for crops
-    sowing, thinning, weeding, pest control application, etc.
-    nursery maintenance for tree crops
-    grading, seed dressing and storage for seed crops and cuttings
-    recognition of crop seeds, insecticides, and fertilizers, and
knowledge of their uses
-    recognition of main plant families.
(c) Livestock Production
-    recognition of principal grasses, legumes and poisonous weeds
-    weights and measures of ingredients, mixing and preparation of
animal feed



SUPPLEMENT 8
Appendix 1
Page 3
poultry feeding
small livestock feeding.
(d) Basic Sciences and Knowledge
-    carry out simple survey using chain and compass
-    accurately weigh volumes and measure surfaces
-    be competent in both metric and English systems
-    calculate input requirements for a plot
-    read and record meteorological data
-    write simple notes and design diagrams and drafts.
(e) Management
-    stores indenting, register maintenance, stores handling,
stacking and withdrawals, inventory preparation
-    operate a simple income and expenditure account.
(f) Extension Work
-    carry out a simple demonstration
-    use chalkboard.
(g) Practical Exercises 1/
-    record and report daily work and findings
-    use tools in order to repair a house or furniture;
handling and sharpening tools
-    use a brick press
-    make a fence and build simple shelter
-    maintain a bicycle.
1/   These proficiencies will be improved throughout the year.



SUPPLEMENT 8
Appendix 1
Page 4
B. Period 2
(a) Plant Production
-    physically perform all cultural practices for a variety
of crops on a plot of land over a full farming cycle
-    recognize main weeds
-    reconize main soils and know their characteristics.
(b) Livestock Production
-    recognize various breeds of cattle, small stock and
poultry, including imported breeds if necessary
-    carry out natural incubation with hens or turkeys
-    vaccinate chickens and administer pest and parasite
control medicines
-    take care of young small stock.
(c) Basic Sciences 1/
-    install and test a scale
-    make all calculations required in the job
-    use meteorological records to forecast and plan crop work
-    use flannel board
-    be a meeting chairman.
(d) Management
-    keep labor work-time book and salary book
-    manage an FSC store and keep books.
(e) Extension Work
-    carry out a demonstration for farmers
-    make an inquiry about farmers' supply needs.
1/   These proficiences will continue to be improved during the remainder
of the year.



SUPPLEMENT 8
Appendix 1
Page 5
B.   Period 3
(a) Plant Production
-    physically perform all cultural practices for a late
season crop
-    recognize and spray against insect and fungus diseases
-    carry out main works in an orchard
-    plant a fuelwood or pole plot
-    store early season crops.
(b)  Livestock Production
-    carry out all works in improved farmers' poultry
-    select male and female stock for breeding purpose;
castrate small livestock
-    prepare silage and hay for cattle and small livestock
-    store crop residues for feeding.
(c)  Management
-    improve mixed farming with new crops
-    calculate mixed farming needs and requirements
-    market (grade, weight, bag included) early crops
-    keep farmers' account book.
(d)  Extension Work
-    carry out a demonstration plot
-    prepare a meeting with village leaders and project
staff
-    lead information/demonstration meetings in farmers'
council.

C.   Period 4
(a) Plant Production
-    carry out all crop operations including harvesting,
threshing and storage
-    take care of soil erosion and soil fertility
-    carry out seed and cutting multiplication work; bud and
prune fruit trees
-    fire fighting and control
-    help forestry services
-    carry out test plot work.
(b) Livestock Production
-    prepare a project for farmers' stock improvement
-    recognize main diseases and call veterinary services
if necessary
-    help veterinary services in breeding cattle.
(c) Management
-    prepare a project with a farmer in order to improve
farming and farm output (including budget, work calendar,
labor requirements)
-    improve storage and crop marketing
-    know Government law and project procedures and enforce
them
-    help a farmer or a group of farmers to apply for a loan.
(d) Extension Work
-    use all extension aids:  placards, symbols, speakers,
camera pictures, etc.
-    lead all kinds of farmers' meetings

NIGERIA
CENTRAL AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECTS
MONITORING AND EVALUATION
General
1.        The two projects combined would involve an estimated 210,000 farm
families spread over an area of some 22,000 sq km cultivating 450,000 ha. 1/
This would be equivalent to a population the size of the Peoples Republic of
Congo and an area equivalent to twice the size of Gambia. However, the project
areas represent only 2.4% of the total area of Nigeria, and the population
represents only about 2% of Nigeria's population. Mlonitoring production in-
creases in this situation would be a formidable task, and will require a
considerable resource input. Furthermore, the Ayangba project area is divided
into three ecological zones and the Lafia project area is populated by people
of different tribes with different cultural affinities. This would call for
particularly careful evaluation of project progress.
Organization
2.        Project field evaluation units would be under the overall direction
of the Agricultural Projects Monitoring, Evaluation and Planning Unit (APMEPU)
at Kaduna. Work plans would take cognizance of APMEPU's experience with the
monitoring and evaluation of the three ongoing Northern Agricultural Develop-
ment Projects, financed by the Bank at Funtua, Gusau and Gombe. 2/ Each
project's Chief Evaluation Officer would, together with his Project Manager,
determine project priorities for evaluation. A work plan would then be agreed
between APMEPU and each project's Chief Evaluation Officer. It is important
that programs be both flexible and standardized: flexible enough to incorpo-
rate investigation of project-specific problems and sufficiently standardized
to use standard analytical computer programming and data processing techniques.
APMEPU's Steering Commitee, on which the Project Managers would sit, would
resolve any disagreements between project evaluation staff and APMEPU.
Staffing
3.        Each field evaluation unit would be headed by a Chief Evaluation
Officer who should have experience in collecting and analyzing micro-economic
data in a smallholder environment and a degree in agricultural economics,
preferably at the Ph.D. level. Some formal course work in sociology would be
an advantage. Above all, these persons would need to be imaginative, flexible
and capable of working with other management staff.
2
1/   Lafia: 9,400 km , harm families 60,000 and cultivated area 150,000 ha.
Ayangba: 13,150 km , farm families 150,000 and cultivated area 300,000 ha.
2/   Funtua Loan 1092-UNI, Gusau Loan 1099-UNI, Gombe Loan 1164-UNI.



SUPPLEMENT 9
Page 2
4.        In addition, each project evaluation unit would be staffed by
evaluation officers, senior enumerators, enumerators and clerks. A Senior
Evaluation Officer would be a deputy and assistant to the Chief Evaluation
Officer. Senior enumerators and enumerators would mostly be assigned to the
field. Where practical, every effort would be made to hire persons, such as
school teachers, on a part-time basis to assist in data collection, for
instance from farmers when they return home from their fields in the evenings.
The project evaluation unit would ensure that it does not become overloaded
with enumerators to the extent that surveys are designed primarily with the
object of keeping enumerators occupied, resulting in unnecessary and surplus
data collection.
5.        The Chief Evaluation Officer would liaise closely with the Head
of the Development Services Department and the Head of the Crop Development
Division (and other division heads as required), in deciding how best to
share the responsibility of data collection. It has long been the experience
in Francophone West Africa for extension staff to have responsibility for
limited data collection, a task they have in some locations done well, there-
by saving unnecessary employment of enumerators. In English-speaking West
Africa, extension agents have not been used for this purpose, but this pos-
sibility should be explored in the projects since there are no inherent
reasons why it cannot be done and the cost savings would be significant.
However, as the extension staff is being trained and expanded, this is clearly
not possible in the early years of the project as the baseline survey and
first mainline survey should be well organized. These surveys are important
as they are the base against which project progress will be evaluated.
6.        There is a limit on the number of field enumerators a Chief Evalua-
tion Officer can effectively supervise. The quality of data collection is
particularly important when sample sizes are necessarily a small proportion of
the population and where farming systems are complex, involving a large number
of input and output parameters. Evaluation of smallholders is particularly
prone to the 'garbage-in, garbage-out' syndrome. The number of field enumera-
tors would be limited to a maximum of 50 at Ayangba and 40 at Lafia, 1/ the
limit being stretched at Ayangba to cope with the particularly large project
area. For every seven enumerators, there would be a senior enumerator. The
Chief Evaluation Officer would have two evaluation officers working under
him.
Work Program 2/
7.        The Chief Evaluation Officers would be in post by March/April
1977. The first two months would be spent familiarizing themselves with the
work of APMEPU, visiting the ongoing Bank-financed Northern Agricultural
Development Projects and reviewing the literature on their project areas. In
particular, at Lafia, the Chief Evaluation Officer, in conjunction with APMEPU,
1/ This is regarded as the maximum number of enumerators one Senior Evalu-
ation Officer can supervise; employment of a second Senior Evaluation
Officer would not be justified.
2/   For a timetable see Table 1.



SUPPLEMENT 9
Page 3
would review the need for a short-term consultancy mission by an experienced
sociologist/anthropologist to make recommendations on the different approaches
to extension required by the several ethnic groups within the project area, in
particular the feasibility of the 'group' approach to farmers. Consulting
time has been included in project costs for this purpose. Consulting time has
also been included to assist with any start-up problems which have to be
tackled early in the project period.
8.        Following initial orientation, a listing of all villages in the
project areas would be made, with information on number of households, tribal
affiliation, infrastructure such as schools and markets, and, on a sample
basis, household size. This information would be analyzed quickly to assist
project management in locating Farmers Service Centers and priority areas for
project involvement. This listing is not only important for evaluation
purposes but for all project activities. Therefore the Project Manager and
other project staff would be involved although the actual execution of the
listing exercise would be best done under the control of the Chief Evaluation
Officer.
9.        A baseline survey similar to those instituted at the Northern
Agricultural Development Projects could be carried out during five months
at the end of 1977 and early 1978. The survey would provide data on the
following factors:
1.   family composition and employment
2.   land holdings and cropping patterns
3.   farm equipment owned
4.   farm input use
5.   farmers' credit
6.   crop use
7.   crop marketing arrangements/channels
8.   hired labor availability
9.   land tenure systems
10. group farming experience.
Items 1-6 would be collected through farmer questionnaires, and items 7-10
through structured questionnaires directed at informed local inhabitants such
as extension officers, school teachers, village heads, etc.
10.       Following the baseline survey, a detailed farm management study 1/
would be instituted to record farmers' activities, farm inputs and outputs over
1/   Also called mainline survey.



SUPPLEMENT 9
Page 4
a cropping cycle (one year), beginning in February/March 1978. In conjunction
with the basic recording of input/output data, a series of questionnaires
would be completed to investigate project-specific problems. Experience to
date with the Northern Agricultural Development Projects indicates that a
detailed farm management survey should not take place every year. Consequent-
ly, detailed farm management studies are proposed for Project Years 1, 3
and 5. This would allow comparison to be made between the years and an
estimate of project impact. However, flexibility would be maintained and
timing and frequency of surveys would be dictated by experience gained.
11.       The work plan would draw primarily on APMEPU's earlier experiences
with the monitoring and evaluation of the Northern Agricultural Development
Projects at Funtua, Gombe and Gusau, particularly with respect to:
1.   sample size for different types of surveys;
2.   length of recall period and data reliability;
3.   development of reliable indices of economic activity;
4.   use of broad-based on-off opinion surveys;
5.   monitoring of multiplier effects.
12.       Both projects have a number of component parts besides the principal
crop production activity, namely fisheries, charcoal industry, timber, live-
stock, etc. These components would be studied in detail in Years 2 and 4.
In order to encourage Nigerian research students to study aspects of the
project and familiarize themselves with what is proving to be a successful
form of development, the project would provide services where possible in the
form of transport, office accommodation, etc., to students wishing to investi-
gate facets of the projects. Research proposals would be vetted and approved
by the Chief Evaluation Officer and Project Manager in conjunction with
APMEPU. In particular, following the findings of the baseline survey, a
marketing and storage study would be organized in each project area, ideally
undertaken as part of a Nigerian researcher's thesis requirement. Such an
arrangement would have the benefit of being inexpensive as well as encouraging
operation-oriented research.
Project Costs
13.       Project costs for evaluation are detailed in the respective appraisal
reports.

NIGERIA
CENTRAL AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. Organization
1.        The projects would be executed by largely autonomous and independent
units under the State Ministries of Agriculture and Natural Resources. Proj-
ect organization would mainly consist of a Project Executive Committee and a
Project Management Unit. An edict establishing both the Project Executive
Committee and the Project Management Unit would be gazetted immediately
following negotiations. A pro-forma edict, to be updated to reflect specific
project requirements, is attached as Appendix 1.
2.        Details on project organization are in the respective appraisal
reports but the main outline is presented below.
3.        The Project Executive Committee would have full authority for
project execution under the Commissioner of Agriculture. It would approve
budgets, appoint senior staff, deal with major procurement matters and award
large contracts. Membership 1/ in this committee would be as follows:
(i)  Permanent Secretary, Ministry of Agriculture and Natural
Resources (Chairman);
(ii) Representative, Ministry of Finance and Executive Planning;
(iii) Representative, Ministry of Local Government;
(iv) Representative, Ministry of Works;
(v)  Representative, Water Board;
(vi) Representative, Ministry of Trade, Industry, and Cooperatives;
(vii) Representative Federal Department of Rural Development;
(viii)  Representatives of the project area appointed by the Military
Governor upon recommendation of the Local Government Council(s);
1/   Exact membership is in the appraisal reports, but the main membership
categories are detailed here.



SUPPLEMENT 10
Page 2
(ix) Chief Agricultural Officer;
(x)  Chief Veterinary Officer;
(xi)  Chief Conservator of Forests;
(xii)  Chief Fisheries Officer;
(xiii)  the Project Manager; and
(xiv)  project's Head of Administration Department (Secretary).
4.        Project organization is detailed in charts attached to the respec-
tive appraisal reports, but is almost identical for both projects. The
organization proposed is built upon the very successful projects implemented
in northern Nigeria. Project organization consists of four major departments:
(i) Administration Department;
(ii) Commercial Services Department;
(iii) Development Services Department;
(iv) Engineering Department.
In addition, there is an evaluation office and a control office which would
come directly under the responsibility of the Project Manager.  A description
of the major departments and divisions in the project organization is ;Lven in
Appendix 2.
5.        Relations with the local people would be maintained through divi-
sional advisory committee(s). Membership of this committee depends on local
circumstances and is detailed in the respective appraisal reports. In general,
the following would be members of this committee:
(i)  the Resident (Chairman);
(ii)  two representatives of the Local Government Council;
(iii)  a representative of the Cooperative Union;
(iv)  all district heads;
(v)  the four project department directors;
(vi)  representatives of other farmers groups (if they exist);
(vii)  Senior Project Administration Officer (as Secretary).



SUPPLEMENT 10
Page 3
6.        One of the principles of project organization is that all existing
facilities and functions of the Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources
would be incorporated in the project. There are, however, a few exceptions.
These are:
(i) management of forest reserves (excluded because they should
be organized on a commercial basis);
(ii) the Alloma Oil Palm Estate (in Benue State);
(iii) irrigation schemes (Plateau State).
7.        The core of project organization is the Farmers Service Center.
A number of FSCs will be built under each project and each FSC would cover a
region of about 15 km radius. FSC would consist of a fertilizer store (about
400 tons), small stores for other farm inputs, and offices. There would be a
small shed for teaching purposes and there would also be a demonstration plot.
Houses would be built in the neighborhood of FSCs to provide housing for
staff employed there. An Assistant Agricultural Superintendent would be in
charge of each FSC. He would also supervise extension staff deployed in the
region covered by the FSC. Other staff include a storekeeper, a salesman, and
a marketing assistant. The latter would only be employed at FSC level from
Year 3. Two tractors would be available at each FSC.
8.        Development Centers (DC) would be created to supervise five FSCs. It
is not envisaged that large infrastructure would be required for DCs as a few
simple offices would suffice. In most cases, existing facilities of MANR
could be used for this purpose. DCs would not be organized along divisional
boundaries which are subject to change anyway, but would be organized strictly
on the basis of supervising five FSCs in a certain region. Supervisory staff
at DCs would consist of the following:
(i) Agriculture Superintendent;
(ii) Sales Supervisor;
(iii) Marketing Advisor;
(iv) Credit Officer with some Credit Assistants.
As seasonal credit is not required with the present level of fertilizer sub-
sidies, credit operations would be restricted to medium- and long-term credit
and some trading facilities for fertilizer sales to farmer groups. Therefore,
these credit operations would be easily controlled at DC level. In any case,
credit staff would organize a system of weekly visits to each FSC in regular
rotation.



SUPPLEMENT 10
Page 4
9.        Communications are very important to assist in project implemen-
tation, and the basic tool used to organize instant communications would be
the radio. Different radio networks to be used in the project are:
(i)  a radio link between the project and the Agricultural Projects
Monitoring, Evaluation and Planning Unit (APMEPU) in Kaduna.
This would provide the project with instant access to the
telex facilities of APMEPU and with other projects in Nigeria;
(ii)  a radio network, which wculd connect all DCs with project
headquarters;
(iii)  a mobile radio network for the project's engineering
department; and
(iv)  a mobile radio network for the project's senior staff.
B. Staffing
10.       Experienced staff will be required for project implementation.  The
job descriptions and qualifications of senior project staff are in Appendix 3.
II.       There are likely to be four different types of recruitment for
project staff:
(i) secondment by the State Government;
(ii)  direct recruitment by the project in Nigeria;
(iii)  international recruitment under a direct contract
between the Government and the staff member;
(iv)  international recruitment under a World Bank secondment
contract.
12.       International recruitment will be required to insure that experienced
staff be available for project implementation. Attached at Appendix 4 is a
list of senior staff likely to be recruited internationally. However, if
well-qualified Nigerians were available for these positions, no international
recruitment would be required. It is, however, important that Nigerian staff
be available in time so as not to delay project implementation. Recruitment
target dates are given in Appendix 5.



SUPPLEHENT 10
Page 5
C. Start-up Plan and Post-Project Development
13.       The first full year of project activities would be in 1978.  This
might seem far away in the future but it should be remembered that to get
equipment and supplies in the project area by 1978, orders will have to be
placed almost a year in advance. 1977 is regarded as the project start-up
year and a detailed start-up chart is presented in the respective appraisal
reports. A summary of key start-up dates is in Appendix 6.
14.       The project development period covers the five-year period 1978-1982,
and it is important to consider what will happen with project organization by
the end of the project period. Basically, there are two alternatives: (i)
continuation of the proposed organization or (ii) dismantling and dividing
functions to various existing institutions. If a second phase project is
contemplated, either with Bank financing, other outside financing, or Federal
financing, then the project organization could be kept as proposed under this
project. The only change which should be contemplated would be to establish
the commercial services department as a separate legal entity which would,
nevertheless, be closely linked to the Project Management Unit. If no second
phase project is contemplated, then the Project Management Unit should be
dismantled in such a way as to safeguard the development initiated by the
project. In that case, the engineering department should be transferred
to the road maintenance and wells maintenance section of the Local Government
Council and/or the Ministry of Works. The commercial services department
should be established as a legal company which would continue to provide
credit, input delivery, and market services in the project area on a strictly
commercial basis. The administration department and development services
department would together form the regional branch of the Ministry of Agricul-
ture and Natural Resources. They would continue to provide the necessary
extension and other services in the project area.

NIGERIA
CENTRAL AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Draft Order for Project Establishment I/
1.        For general information, it is hereby notified that a Project
Executive Committee 2/ and a Project Management Unit 2/ have been set up with
the objectives and duties as hereunder stated.
2.        The Project Management Unit would be managed by the Project Manager
and its objectives should be:
(i)  development of .... LGC areas with a view toward increasing agricul-
tural production, promoting livestock and fisheries, developing
communal forest resources, and raising the living standards of
the rural population;
(ii)  construction of roads and water supplies associated with agricul-
tural development;
(iii)  development and management of a seed multiplication and research
capacity;
(iv)  training of project staff and farmers;
(v)  provision of farmers' service centers and stores;
(vi)  provision of extension, credit and marketing services;
(vii)  procurement and subsequent delivery of farm inputs;
(viii)  development of supporting infrastructure, such as houses,
buildings, offices and others, to support the above services;
(ix)  evaluation and monitoring of ongoing project actions; and
(x)  any other activities to ensure the success of the project.
1/   This appendix gives the draft of the edict which should be gazetted
to officially establish the Project Management Unit. As such, it is
based on earlier edicts used to establish Project Management Units for
the Northern Agricultural Development Projects. The edict as drafted in
this appendix gives only a broad outline and should be amended to reflect
specific project circumstances and legal requirements of the State.
2/   Exact names should be used, such as, for example, Lafia Project Manage-
ment Unit (LPMU).



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 1
Page 2
3.        The Project Management Unit would be controlled by the Project
Executive Committee and would be made up as follows:  ...........
4.        The Project Executive Committee would be responsible to the Com-
missioner of Agriculture and would have the following responsibilities:
(i) Policy
The Project Executive Committee would determine agricultural
and development policy and would consult with the State and Federal
Government and the World Bank on any major changes in policy which
may be required.
(ii) Finance
(a)  The Project Executive Committee would approve the annual
budgets and would ensure that sufficient Government funds
are readily available to the project;
(b)  The Project Executive Committee would appoint independent
auditiors;
(c)  The Project Executive Committee would submit audited
statements of account to the Commissioner of Agriculture
within four months of the closure of each financial year;
(d) The Project Executive Committee would approve the opening
of project bank accounts;
(e) The Chairman of the Project Executive Committee would
approve the monthly cash flow statements submitted by the
Project Manager;
(f) The Project Executive Committee would approve annually
the terms of lending to project farmers and pricing of
agricultural inputs on the basis of recommendations made
by the Project Management Unit.
(iii) Staff and Personnel Matters
In consultation with the manager of the Project Management Unit,
the Project Executive Committee would be responsible for the
appointment of all senior project staff and employees, terms and
conditions of employment, and annual review of salaries and
allowances.



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 1
Page 3
(iv)  Awards of Contracts
The Project Executive Committee would, on the advice of the
Project Manager, be responsible for approval of any single
contract valued at more than N 30,000.
(v)  Interdepartmental Coordination
The Project Executive Committee, through its members, would
be responsible for the coordination at the managerial level of
those issues that may affect the proper development of the
project.
5.        Meetings of the Project Executive Committee.
(i)  The Chairman would preside over all meetings of the Project
Executive Committee which he attends, and in his absence
members present would appoint one of the members to preside
over such a meeting;
(ii)  The Chairman of the Project Executive Committee would
invite members to meet at such time and place as he may
think suitable, provided that no more than three months
would elapse between any one meeting and the next. In
the absence of the Chairman, the Project Manager would
call the Project Executive Committee to meet whenever he
deemed it necessary;
(iii)  Eight members of the Project Executive Committee would
constitute a quorum of any meetings of the Committee. The
Project Executive Committee would base its decisions on a
majority of the votes of its members present in a meeting
with a quorum, and in case of a tie vote, the Chairman
would have the casting vote;
(iv)  The Project Executive Committee may call any person to
attend any of its meetings without that person having a
counted vote.
6.        The Project Management Unit would be responsible to the Project
Executive Committee for day-to-day operations of the project.  In particular,
it would be responsible for carrying out the objectives as stated in para 2.
The Project Management Unit would be managed by the Project Manager who would
report directly to the Chairman of the Project Executive Committee. The main
duties of the Project Management Unit would be:



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 1
Page 4
(i)- the execution of an agricultural development program within
the Local Government Councils of   .       ....., following the
policy guidelines of the Project Executive Committee;
(ii) the preparation of annual budgets and cash flows for approval
by the Project Executive Committee; in addition the Project
Management Unit would submit to the Chairman of the Project
Executive Committee monthly cash flow statements indicating
any changes in requirements for State Government funds due
to changes in expected expenditures and inflow anticipated
from other sources. Unless informed to the contrary, within
five days of submission of such statements to the Chairman
of the Project Executive Committee the Project Manager
would be authorized to make the proposed expenditures;
(iii) the control and operation of the project's revolving credit
fund;
(iv) the recruitment of project staff in consultation, when
necessary, with other Government departments, and for senior
staff with approval of the Project Executive Committee; the
advertising, negotiating, calling for tenders, and, after
approval from the Project Executive Committee for contracts
of more than N 30,000, for conclusion of such contracts with
persons and organizations either inside or outside the State
for the erection of buildings, the construction of civil
works, and-the supply of goods, services and materials as
may seem requisite for the proper discharge of its duties;
(v) the operation of project bank accounts;
(vi) the preparation of reimbursement claims against project
expenditure for submission to the Federal Government and
the World Bank.
7.        The Project Management Unit would administer an evaluation unit
that would be technically directed by a Federal-Government-controlled Agricul-
tural Projects Monitoring, Evaluation and Planning Unit estabished in Kaduna.
8.        The Project Management Unit would be responsible for the collection
and dissemination of market information to the farmers in the project area
and would cooperate with the State and Federal Governments or their agencies in
improving marketing practices. The Project Management Unit would also be
authorized to act as a Licensed Buying Agent or in any other equivalent
capacity to the Commodity Boards to be established (or similar marketing
organization which may be established).

9.        The Project Management Unit would prepare quarterly and annual
reports for submission to the Project Executive Committee, the State's Execu-
tive Council, the Federal Government of Nigeria, and the World Bank.
10.       The Project Management Unit would be delegated any additional
duties and powers by the Project Executive Committee to ensure the attainment
of the objectives as set out in this order.

NIGERIA
CENTRAL AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Description of Major Departments
Prolect ManaRement Unit
1.        Organization of the Project Management Unit is detailed in charts
attached to the respective appraisal reports. The Project Management Unit is
divided into four major departments. These are:
(i) the Administration Department;
(ii) the Commercial Services Department;
(iii) the Development Services Department; and
(iv) the Engineering Department.
In addition, there would be an Evaluation Unit and a Control Office.
Administrative Department
2.        This department would control all personnel matters, recruitment,
staff, transportation, office management, public relations and communications.
In addition, it would be responsible for the coordination of all project
procurement. The actual power to procure would rest with the Project Manager
and would be delegated by him to department heads. However, coordination must
be ensured and this would be affected in the Administration Department. A
major function of the Administration Department is the preparation of budgets
and the keeping of accounts. The department would also be responsible for
security. The department would work closely with existing Government agencies
to ensure proper transfer of staff in and out of the Project Management
Unit and would ensure adequate salary increments and performance and promotion
gradings. In short, it would ensure that personnel matters are managed
in the first place in accordance with all regulations of the State Government,
but secondly, to properly reflect additional incentives needed to attract
experienced staff to the project.
3.        Communications are very important for the successful implementation
of the project. The Administration Department would be responsible for the
operation of the various radio networks operated by the project; this would
mainly concern the radio network with Kaduna and the mobile project radio
network. The Engineering Department would have its own mobile network, as would
the Commercial Services Department, to link up the various Development Centers.
However, the latter network would have a link with the Administration Depart-
ment in headquarters.



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 2
Page 2
4.        In order to carry out its functions, the Administration Department
would have the following divisions:
(i) Accounting Division;
(ii)  Personnel Division;
(iii)  Housing Division;
(iv)  Transportation Division;
(v) Public Relations Division;
(vi)  Procurement Division;
(vii) Communications Division; and
(viii)  Security Division.
Commercial Services Department
5.        This department would be responsible for credit, input supply,
and marketing advisory services, and would also include the Produce Inspection
Division. The department would be operated along commercial lines and this
would be especially reflected in its accounting systems. The department would
mainly work through a system of Farmers Service Centers, five of which will be
under the supervision of a Development Center. More details concerning
the functions of the Commercial Services Department are in the respective
appraisal reports.
6.        In order to implement its functions, the Commercial Services Department
is divided into the following divisions:
(i) Credit Division;
(ii) Input Supply Division;
(iii) Marketing Advisory Service Division; and
(iv) Produce Inspection Division.
Development Services Department
7.        The Development Services Department is the main operational depart-
ment responsible for project implementation. It is made up of the following
divisions:
(i) Land-Use and Farm Advisory Division: This is the division which
will advise large farmers or groups of farmers about developing
their holdings. They will also be responsible for mapping;
therefore it is divided into the following sections:



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 2
Page 3
(a)  Photo Interpretation and Mapping;
(b) Tractor Hire Service; and
(c) Group Farm Advisory Service (including liaison with
cooperatives or other farmers' groups)
For more details, see Supplement 3.
(ii)  Crop Development Division:  This division is responsible for the
agricultural development component of the project and as such is
divided into the following sections:
(a) Extension;
(b) Seed Multiplication; and
(c) Experiments (applied research).
(iii)  Livestock Development Division:  This division is divided into the
following sections:
(a)  Veterinary Services;
(b)  Small Livestock (This section will promote improved practices
for such small livestock as poultry, goats and pigs);
(c)  (only in the case of the Lafia Project) Cattle Section
which will be responsible for management of the grazing
reserves and trade routes.
(iv) Forestry Division;
(v) Fisheries Division;
(vi)  Training Division:  This division is sub-divided into two sectionsr
(a) Staff Training; and
(b) Farmer Training.
Engineering Department
8.        This department is responsible for all civil works to be executed
under the project. As such, it is divided into the following divisions:
(i) Road Development Division;
(ii)  Water Development Division;



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 2
Page 4
(iii) Building Division; and
(iv) Central Workshop.
Control Office
9.        The Control Office will be established directly under the Project
Manager and will be responsible for store control. Internal audit could also
be a part of this office, but it was felt more appropriate to have the external
auditors much more involved in project control. Therefore internal audit
would not be required under the projects for the time being.
Evaluation Office
10.       This office would be part of the Project Management Unit and would
be administratively responsible to the Project Manager. However, technical
direction would be provided by the Agricultural Projects Monitoring, Evaluation
and Planning Unit which is established in Kaduna as part of the Federal
Department of Rural Development.
Coordination
11.       In order to provide coordination within the project, senior staff
would meet monthly to discuss project implementation. The following would be
members of this committee:
(i)  Project Manager (Chairman);
(ii) Head of Administration Department;
(iii) Head of Commercial Services Department;
(iv) Head of Development Services Department;
(v)  Head of Engineering Department;
(vi) Chief Credit Officer;
(vii) Chief, Input Supply Division;
(viii) Chief, Land-Use and Farm Advisory Division;
(ix) Chief, Crop Development Division;
(x)  Chief, Livestock Development Division;
(xi) Chief, Forestry Division;



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 2
Page 5
(xii) Chief, Fisheries Division;
(xiii) Chief, Training Division;
(xiv) Chief Road Engineer;
(xv) Chief Hydrologist; and
(xvi) Chief Accountant.
No formal regulations would be made for meetings of this committee and the
Project Manager would adjust membership in this "unofficial" committee to
fit the specific circumstances of the project.
12.       Within the Development Services Department the head of this department
would hold fortnightly meetings with his division chiefs to discuss day-to-day
operational questions in relation to the project. Major issues would be
referred to the Project Manager or to the project committee referred to in
para 11 above.
