CENTRAL AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECTS
Job Descriptions and Qualifications of Senior Staff
1.        The following job descriptions and qualifications of senior staff
are presented in this appendix:
-    Project Manager
-    Head of Administration Department
-    Head of Engineering Department
-    Head of Development Services Department
-    Head of Commercial Services Department
-    Chief Accountant
-    Roads Engineer
-    Hydrological Engineer
-    Building Supervisor
-    Workshop Engineer
-    Farm Management Advisor
-    Chief Agronomist
-    Head of Livestock Department
-    Forestry Expert
-    Fishery Expert
-    Training Officer
-    Evaluation Officer
-    Credit Officer
-    Input Delivery Officer



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 3
Page 2
-    Marketing Officer
-    Chief, Extension Service
-    Chief, Seed Multiplication
-    Chief Research Officer
2.        Appendix 4 lists those positions for which international recruitment
would be required, and Appendix 5 gives recruitment target dates.
Project Manager
3.        Experience and managerial capability would be the main criteria for
the selection of the Project Manager, although a formal qualification in
agriculture would be important. Extensive experience in successful agricul-
tural development work in the field, including development oriented projects,
preferably in Africa, would be required. A high degree of administrative
responsibility and experience in dealing with high level Government officials
and Government committees, and experience with extension in small farmer
communities would be necessary.
4.        The Project Manager would be responsible for the overall planning
and coordination of project activities. He would have direct responsibility
for the day-to-day operations of the project and for coordinating project
activities with those of Federal and State Government agencies and civil
authorities. His duties would include:
(a) planning and budgeting project operations;
(b) controlling all project funds (with the assistance of
the Chief Accountant);
(c) monitoring and controlling execution of project activities,
and establishing a cost analysis and record keeping
system which would enable project programs and achievement
to be monitored and meaningful evaluation to be made;
(d) ensuring proper personnel management (with the assistance of
the Head of the Department of Administration);
(e) coordination of all project activities;
(f) periodically reporting project progress to the Project
Executive Committee, to Government, and to other agencies,
through written reports and through the various project
committees;



SUPPLENENT 10
Appendix 3
Page 3
(g) training project staff and ensuring that a proper management
training program is successfully implemented; and
(h) other responsibilities as set out in the Project Management
Unit's terms of reference. 1/
5.        The Project Manager would be responsible to the Chairman of the
Project Executive Committee (the Chairman being the Permanent Secretary
of the Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources).
Head of Administration Department
6.        The Head of the Administration Department would be a senior civil
servant or have had equivalent experience in a major public corporation
or private company. He should have a thorough understanding of Government
procedures and should be sufficiently mature and resolute to handle staff
relations problems. He would head the Project's Administration Department.
7.        His duties would include:
(a) responsibility for all project personel management and
staff relations;
(b) control of project transportation;
(c) maintenance of office registry;
(d) control of office administration and development of
day-to-day procedural arrangements;
(e) handling public relations aspects of the project;
(f) training administrative staff;
(g) preparation and control of administrative budget;
(h) control and administration of project housing, office
space, and the project's guest house;
(i) coordination of procurement;
(j)  control of project communications.
8.        He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager.
1/   See Appendix 1 for draft edict.



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 3
Page 4
Head of Engineering Department
9.        The Head of the Engineering Department would have a degree in
civil engineering; some training in mechanical engineering would be an added
advantage. He would have substantial experience in the problems of rural
engineering in developing countries. A sound knowledge of and experience
in road and water engineering would be considered essential. He would have
had senior administrative responsibility in the engineering field and would be
accustomed to working with government agencies.
10.       His duties would include:
(a) control of all project engineering activities;
(b) the preparation and control of engineering department
budgets;
(c) the preparation of all tenders, contracts, and quantity
surveys for civil works;
(d) supervision of the design of project roads, buildings,
and water supplies;
(e) coordination of the different divisions of the
Engineering Department; and
(f) close liaison with other Government departments,
such as the Ministry of Works, and Local Government
Councils associated with the project.
11.       He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager.
Head of Development Services Department
12.       The Head of the Development Services Department would have a degree
in agriculture with specialization in tropical crop production. He would have
experience in broad-based agricultural development. Experience of livestock
development would be an added advantage, as would be experience with general
aspects of rural development. He should have had extensive experience in a
senior position in similar development projects which should demonstrate a
successful career and a proven record of obtaining quantifiable results. As
he would oversee the Training Division, he must have a good record and rele-
vant experience in training programs for extension workers and farmers.
13.       He would head the Development Services Department which would in-
volve responsibility for ensuring that the project's agricultural and related
policy is properly executed. He would therefore be responsible for overall
coordination and management of the six divisions in his department: the Land



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 3
Page 5
Use and Farm Advisory Division, the Crop Development Division, the Livestock
Development Division, the Forestry Division, the Fisheries Division and the
Training Division. His duties would include specifically:
(a) departmental budget preparation and control;
(b) quarterly and annual report writing on the department's
operations;
(c) collating technical input requirements and coordinating
these with the credit and supply services operating under
the Head of the Commercial Services Department;
(d) advising the Project Manager on departmental staff
requirements and, in conjunction with the Head of the
Training Division, developing suitable training
courses for staff and farmers;
(e) developing overall applied research policies within
the project;
(f)  advising the Project Manager on field operational
policies and annual program of development targets; and
(g) supervising and coordinating all development activities
of the various divisions under his control.
14.       He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager.
Head of Commercial Services Department
15.       The Head of the Commercial Services Department should possess
extensive experience in a managerial capacity in a commercial undertaking
(preferably agro-business) including rural credit, supply services and
marketing, which should demonstrate a successful career and a proven record of
obtaining quantifiable results. He should have a sound knowledge of account-
ing practices related to credit and supply services.
16.       His duties would include:
(a) preparation and control of the Commercial Services Department
budget;
(b) control of the revolving credit fund;
(c)  establishment of appropriate accounting services
for the credit division;
(d) operation of a chain of Farmers Service Centers for
the supply of inputs;



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 3
Page 6
(e)- provision of a marketing intelligence and advisory service;
(f) control over the produce inspection division;
(g) maintenance of a close liaison with the Head of the
Development Services Department to ensure the
coordination of supply and demand of farm inputs;
(h) maintenance of a close liaison with the cooperative
movement, or other farmers groups; and
(i) the training of staff in credit, supply and marketing
services.
17.       He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager.
Chief Accountant
18.       The Chief Accountant would have a degree in accounting with addi-
tional training or experience in business management. He should have exten-
sive experience in a senior position in a large public corporation or commer-
cial company. A knowledge of banking and credit procedures would be an
important attribute. Previous employment working with high-ranking government
officials would be an advantage.
19.       His main duty would be management of the Accounts Division and the
control of project funds on behalf of the Project Manager. His duties would
include:
(a) preparing budgets, cash flows and estimates of expenditures;
(b) ensuring correct application of project funds;
(c) assisting the Credit Manager in establishing an efficient
and meaningful system of recording agricultural credit
transactions;
(d) responsibility for store accounting, cash in- and outflow,
the project's bank accounts, and managing the project's
liquidity;
(e) liaison with the project's independent auditors to ensure
timely conduct of interim and year-end audits; and
(f) training of accounting staff.
20.       He would be directly responsible to the Head of the Department
of Administration, but would also maintain a close working relationship
with other department heads.



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 3
Page 7
Road Engineer
21.       The Road Engineer would be a qualified civil engineer and would
have substantial and successful experience in the design, construction and
maintenance of earth roads in tropical climates.
22.       His duties would include:
(a) advising the Head of the Department of Engineering
Services on road design, drainage and water disposal,
culverts and river crossing design;
(b) the direction of the machinery and equipment put at
his disposal for construction purposes;
(c) the preparation and maintenance of records adequate
to enable unit costs to be established;
(d) evaluation of road construction programs and techniques
used; and
(e) conducting a training program for engineers in road
design and construction methods.
23.       He would be directly responsible to the Head of the Engineering
Department.
Hydrological Engineer
24.       The Hydrological Engineer would be a qualified hydrologist having
a broad understanding of rural water development with specific experience
in surface and subsurface water extraction and retention in tropical climates.
25.       His duties would include:
(a) preparation and control of his division's budget;
(b) participation in inter-disciplinary studies and surveys;
(c) the siting of reservoirs and especially wells;
(d) supervision and coordination of well-construction teams;
(e) interpretation of hydrological and hydro-geological
computations of soil and geological. samples derived from
augering and well-digging operations;



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 3
Page 8
(f) conduct hydrological control of the wells under the
construction program;
(g) conducting hydrological reconnaissance of the project
area.
26.       He will be directly responsible to the Head of the Engineering
Department.
27.       It is envisaged that only one experienced hydrologist will be
recruited internationally to serve the two projects. He would be recruited
under the Lafia project, where he is likely to spend most of his time (2/3)
but he would also supervise hydrological activities in the Ayangba area.
Therefore, under the Ayangba project a Head of the Water Development Division
and under the Lafia project a Deputy Head of the Water Development Division
need to be appointed. These would be very experienced technical officers who
have worked in the hydrological field and have experience in supervising well
construction. An engineering degree would be advantageous in the case of the
Ayangba project.
Building Supervisor
28.       The Building Supervisor would have a construction background and
would have extensive experience in the construction industry, mainly buildings.
'de would have been in a senior supervisory position in private industry or
with government enterprises. Experience in overseeing should include at least
some years supervising, organizing and controlling various small contractors.
He should have knowledge in local tender procedures and have wide experience
in tendering, award, and subsequent control procedures.
29.       He would be responsible for supervising the building program under
the project, and his duties would include:
(a) acquiring land;
(b) supervision of site surveys and development of site plans;
(c) supervision and control of designs and bills of quantity;
(d) preparing tenders and contracts for construction of houses,
offices and other project buildings;
(e) day-to-day supervision of construction contractors;
(f) organizing small construction jobs for force account
operation by the project (if required).



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 3
Page 9
30.       He would be directly responsible to the Head of the Engineering
Department. As the projects building program is concentrated early in the
project development period, he would have a contract of no more than three
years if recruited early in the projects' start-up period.
Workshop Engineer
31.       The Workshop Engineer would have professional qualifications in
mechanical engineering. He would have substantial practical experience
especially in operating and maintaining heavy duty earth-moving equipment and
machinery.
32.       His principal duties and responsibilities would be supervising
the operation and maintenance of the project plant, equipment and vehicles.
His duties would include:
(a) establishing a workshop capable of maintaining project
plant equipment and vehicles;
(b) ordering and maintaining an appropriate supply of stores
and spare parts;
(c) ordering and maintaining an adequate and suitable fuel
supply; and
(d) conducting a training program for mechanical engineers.
33.       He would be directly responsible to the Head of the Engineering
Department.
Farm Management Advisor
34.       The Farm Management Advisor would have a degree in tropical agri-
culture including course work in land-use planning and farm management. He
would be an experienced farm advisor with substantial experience in tropical
agriculture advisory work. Experience in practical farm management would be
required. He should also have practical experience in photo-interpretation
and mapping and know the various techniques used in aerial photography.
35.       He would head the Land-Use and Farm Advisory Division and his
duties will include:
(a) preparation and control of the division's budget;
(b) operation of the photo-interpretation and mapping section;
(c) management of the tractor hire service unit;



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 3
Page 10
(d). provision of farm manageaent advice to farmers;
(e) preparation of physical and financial farm plans;
(f) establishment of a close liaison with the cooperative
movement, or other farmers groups; and
(g) training staff in farm management.
36.       He would be directly responsible to the Head of the Development
Services Department.
Chief Agronomist
37.       The Chief Agronomist would have a degree in tropical agriculture
and extensive practical experience in the field of agricultural extension and
its administration. Experience in agronomic research, and seed multiplication
and certification schemes would be a valuable attribute.
38.       He would be responsible for the extension, applied research, and
seed multiplication unit and his duties would include:
(a) preparation and control of the division's budget;
(b) setting out simple agronomic trials;
(c) multiplying breeder seed;
(d) maintaining a close liaison with existing research
institutions in the country such as the Institute for
Agricultural Research (IAR) in Samaru and the International
Institute for Tropical Agriculture (IITA) in Ibadan;
(e) organizing simple cultural and crop demonstrations;
(f) developing an outgrower second and third stage seed
multiplication scheme;
(g) undertaking seed certification and germination testings; and
(h) administering the project's agricultural extension service
to farmers.
39.       He would be directly responsible to the Head of the Development
Services Department.



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 3
Page 11
Livestock Specialist
40.       The Livestock Specialist would be head of the Livestock Division but
would principally be responsible for the Cattle Section of the division. He
would have a degree or equivalent training in animal husbandry and range
management. He should have extensive experience in the development of grazing
lands for cattle operations, preferably under tropical conditions. Field
experience with poultry, pigs, goats, and sheep would be advantageous.
41.       His duties would include:
(a) preparation and control of the division's budget;
(b)  establishment of four grazing reserves for the exclusive
use of pastoralists;
(c) provision of all necessary services for grazing reserve
establishment, including: initial survey and delineation,
survey of subdivisional blocks, control of grazing rights
within reserves, provision of adequate water supplies,
comprehensive veterinary coverage, improvement of range-
land productivity, maintenance of detailed records,
assistance in the development of market outlets;
(d) provision of all necessary services for the trade routes,
including survey, veterinary coverage, water supply, and
maintenance of records;
(e) maintenance of a close liaison with the Federal Department
of Livestock's Project Unit based at Kaduna;
(f) organization of a poultry exchange program for farmers; and
(g) supervision of the veterinary service.
42.       He would be directly responsible to the Head of the Development
Services Department.
43.       The livestock expert would be internationally recruited under
the Lafia project mainly to establish the grazing reserves, but he would also
be the Head of the Livestock Development Division under that project. How-
ever, he would aslo advise the Ayangba project on livestock development.
Therefore, a Head of the Livestock Division in the Ayangba project and a
Deputy Head of the Livestock Division in the Lafia project need to be re-
cruited. They should be very experienced veterinarian officers with exten-
sive experience in the sector in Nigeria and a degree or equivalent training
in animal husbandry.



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 3
Page 12
Silviculturist
44.       The Silviculturist should have a university degree or equivalent
in forestry or forest engineering. He should have substantial experience in
soft wood plantation work including nursery practice and modern planting
techniques; experience in a large forestry project or similar operation would
be advantageous.
45.       His duties would include:
(a) preparation and control of the division's budget;
(b) the planning and implementation of plantation and
research programs;
(c) the conduct of all silvicultural operations from the
nursery to final cropping;
(d) assistance in forestry mensuration;
(e) establishment of forest plantation records and of
the cropping system;
(f) assistance in the selection of technical forestry staff;
(g) responsibility for on-the-job training of technical
forestry personnel and the review of laborers and
staff situations;
(h) responsibility for the cost benefit analysis of
plantation programs.
46.       He would be responsible directly to the Head of the Development
Services Department.
47.       Only one expert would be recruited internationally under the Ayangba
project, but he would work equally in the Lafia project. Two Deputy Chiefs
for the Forestry Divisions in the two projects need to be appointed. They
would have formal training and qualifications in forestry and substantial
experience in the sector including work in large plantations.
Fisheries Specialist
48.       The Fisheries Specialist would have a university degree or suitable
professional qualifications. He would be a qualified and experienced fishery
expert with substantial experience in inland fisheries. He should have proven
administrative ability. He would be a practical and training-oriented man.



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 3
Page 13
49.       The Fisheries Specialist would head the Fisheries Division in
the Ayangba project and his duties would include:
(a) preparation and control of the fisheries budget;
(b) establishment of a Fisheries Training Center;
(c) construction and management of fish lagoons;
(d) provision of technical advice to the Fisheries
Departments of the Ministries of Agriculture in
Benue and Plateau States on long-term planning for
development of State fishery resources.
(e) providing extension services to fishermen on
the catching, processing and marketing of fish.
50.       He would be directly responsible to the Head of the Development
Services Department. His duty station would be Ayangba.
51.       Only one expert would be recruited internationally; he would,
however, also supervise the fishery component of the Lafia project. A Deputy
Head of Division needs to be appointed in Ayangba and a Head of Division in
Lafia. The expert for the Ayangba project would need better qualifications
than the expert for Lafia, as the component for the latter is small. A
background in fisheries with formal training would be essential, and substan-
tial experience in inland and river fishing would be required.
Training Officer
52.       The Training Officer would have a degree in tropical agriculture
and would have substantial experience in training both senior and junior
agricultural staff and farmers. He should be a good organizer and should be
able to communicate effectively.
53.       His responsibilities would include:
(a) preparation and control of the training budget;
(b) managing the training division;
(c)  developing detailed training courses for project junior
staff;
(d) operating the project Training Center;
(e) developing the peripatetic training concept for farmers;



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 3
Page 14
(f) organizing overseas scholarships for senior staff;
(g) conducting the periodic review of inservice training
and the training of Nigerian staff by expatriates;
(h) liaising with other divisions for specialized training
courses;
(i) coordinating a home economics training unit into the
project's training program for farmers.
54.       He would be directly responsible to the Head of the Development
Services Department.
Evaluation Officer
55.       The Evaluation Officer should be a graduate, preferably at Ph.D.
level, in agricultural economics, with some course work in sociology. He
should have experience in collecting and analyzing micro-economic data in
a smallholder environment. Above all, flexibility and imagination are re-
quired, and the capability to work with smallholders.
56.       He would direct field enumeration units attached to the project.
He would supervise enumerators, implement evaluation programs set out in
conjunction with the Agricultural Projects Monitoring, Evaluation and Planning
Unit in Kaduna, feed back the acquired information for centralized analysis
and further study the results. He would design and organize project-specific
surveys.
57.       He would be technically responsible to the Head of the APMEPU
Evaluation Section and administratively responsible directly to the Project
Manager.
Credit Officer
58.       The Credit Officer would be a professionally qualified accountant
with a sound working knowledge of the establishment and working of rural
credit institutions. He would have a record of sound administrative ability.
Experience in the formation of farmers' credit groups or societies and in
working with these organizations, and/or cooperatives, would be an advantage.
59.       His prime responsibility would be the control of project farmer
credit operations, which would involve the disbursement of loans in kind and
their recovery.
60.       His responsibilities would include:
(a) formation of a Revolving Credit Fund as outlined in
the project appraisal report;



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 3
Page 15
(b) establishing an efficient accounting and recording system
for farmers' loans;
(c) liaison with the Evaluation Unit to provide data and infor-
mation needed in project evaluation;
(d) liaison with the Input Delivery Officer over the supply of
farm inputs obtained under credit arrangement; and
(e) training of credit staff.
61.       The Credit Officer would be responsible to the Head of the Commer-
cial Services Department.
Input Delivery Officer
62.       The Input Delivery Officer is responsible to the Head of the Commer-
cial Services Department for the supply of all inputs to farmers.
63.       A suitable candidate would have worked in commercial enterprises or
in Government or para-statal bodies which operate large supply programs, and
have experience and proven ability in the fields of procurement, stockkeeping;
salesmanship, and handling and accounting for goods.
64.       Whilst the credit aspects of input supply to farmers would be
handled by a separate section, the Input Delivery Officer would be expected
to liaise closely with the Credit Officer and to be fully acquainted with
the operation of the credit mechanism.
65.       The Input Delivery Officer would be responsible for the procurement
and delivery of farm inputs, and for overall management and maintenance of a
number of Farmers Service Centers. He would also be responsible for conduc-
ting an inservice training program of salesmen and storekeepers.
66.       He would be assisted by a team of salesmen and storemen, and should,
therefore, possess the character and administrative ability to effectively
control such a team.
Marketing Officer
67.       The Marketing Officer is responsible to the Head of the Commercial
Services Department for the operation of a project marketing information
service.
68.       He should preferably be a degree holder in economics, business
administration or other fields appropriate to the marketing function, and
have substantial experience in marketing operations. However, a non-degree-
holder possessing extensive cotmmercial marketing experience could be consi-
dered for the post.  He should have a sound knowledge of storage practi(e&s
and potential in tropical climates.



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 3
Page 16
69.       The Marketing Officer would be responsible through the Head of
the Commercial Services Department for the supply of a regular flow of market-
ing information to the Project Manager, Head of the Development Services
Department, and other interested officers, and especially to project farmers.
70.       The Marketing Officer would be responsible for creating a more
disciplined and informed market structure in the project areas to cater
for both existing and incremental production. This would require a close
liaison with Marketing Organizations, Government Ministries, traders, and
farmers. The post therefore, requires an officer of maturity and experience.
71.       He would also be responsible for:
(a) Maintaining close links with main recording centers and
particularly with market sections in other development
schemes;
(b) Establishing a marketing news recording and diffusion system:
(c) Examining and overhauling, in close liaison with the
Federal Ministry of Trade and Industry, the standards for
weights and measures being used in the project areas;
(d) Considering, in conjunction with the Head of Development
Services and other appropraite officers, the crop storage
requirements of project farmers;
(e) Providing a specialist service for farmer groups wishing
to extend their produce sales to wholesale and retail levels;
(f)  Promoting the improvement of statutory market arrangements
in the area;
(g) Examining and promoting the improvement of produce transportation
services to the project areas;
(h) Liaising with Cooperative Unions, Primary Societies, farmer groups;
and
(i) Examining and recommending other ways and means of improving
on crop processing facilities.
72.       The Marketing Officer would be assisted in his task by a team of
Marketing Advisors and Assistants and should therefore possess administra-
tive experience and capability in handling this team.



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 3
Page 17
Chief Extension Officer
73.       The Chief Extension Officer would be responsible to the Chief
Agronomist for the agricultural extension services provided to farmers.
74.       He will be an experienced agriculturist holding a degree in agri-
culture or similar qualifications and will have had extensive experience
of practical agricultural extension methods.
75.       He should be a person of drive and initiative capable of reacting
to new methods of agricultural promotion particularly in regards to the
integration of agricultural extension services with those of credit, input
supply, marketing research, and training.
76.       He will be expected to maintain a close liaison with all the above
sections and especially to ensure that the extension service provides a
bridge between the farmer and the technical information and supply services
operated by the various sections of the project.
77.       The Chief Extension Officer will be in charge of a large number of
extension workers whom he will be expected to inspire with personal leadership
and administrative control.
Chief Seed Multiplication Unit
78.       The Chief of the Seed Multiplication Unit will be responsible to the
Chief Agronomist for the operation of the project's seed multiplication unit
and for the control and issue of seed to farmers.
79.       He will be responsible for controlling, approving, and handling
all seed grown under multiplication programs by farmers. The officer will
therefore be an experienced agriculturist possessing a degree in agricul-
ture and having substantial experience in seed production or plant breeding.
80.       He will be expected to maintain a close liaison with research and
plant breeding institutions in Nigeria in conjunction with the Chief Research
Officer and under the direction of the Chief Agronomist.
81.       He will be expected to maintain accurate records of seed production,
handling and storage costs, so that these may be passed on to purchasers.
Chief Research Officer
82.       The Chief Research Officer will be responsible to the Chief Agrono-
mist for conducting the project's crop research programs. These will
essentially be of an applied nature and will be conducted in conformity with
the policies of other research institutions in Nigeria.



SUPPLEMENT 10
Appendix 3
Page 18
83.       The Chief Research Officer will be a degree-holding agriculturalist
preferably having specialized in botany and having substantial practical
experience in the field of agricultural crop research and plant breeding.
84.       He will be expected to lay down field trials in close liaison
with the Chief Extension Officer and Chief of the Seed Multiplication Unit to
determine agronomic policy for all crops scheduled for inclusion within
the project. He will work closely with existing research institutions in
Nigeria.

SUPPLEMENT 11
NIGERIA
CENTRAL AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECTS
MARKETS AND PRICES
A. Market Outlets Today 1/
Background
1.        Until recently 2/ official market support and intervention in
Nigeria have been confined to a narrow sector of export crops while food
crops which represent over 85% of agricultural output have been marketed
almost entirely within the private sector. Against a background of actual
or apparent self-sufficiency, the export, or scheduled, crops came to be
disproportionately stimulated as sources of cash in a basically subsistence-
oriented economy.
2.        Since the end of the Civil War, a substantial rise in oil-ori-
ginated income and an increasing market-dependent population have caused
food prices to rise sharply. Cash returns from foodcrop production in-
creased relative to export crops and led to both an absolute decline in
scheduled crop plantings and to a smaller proportion of the non-industrial
scheduled crops being marketed through the statutory system. 3/
3.        Against this background FMG has introduced a series of changes aimed
at reforming and revitalizing the Marketing Board system and stimulating pro-
duction of desirable crops.  Overall policy continues to be further developed,
but steps so far announced 4/ have been directed towards linking scheduled
crop prices more directly to world market levels through abolishing produce
taxes and protecting farmers against heavy market overheads; and improving
price stability for domestic crops through wider distribution of fertilizer,
seed and credit to all sectors of agriculture. Further changes have been
announced (described in section B) which, if effected, will dramatically
change the marketing system in existence today as it is described below.
1/   At time of appraisal and for the remainder of 1976.
2/   For proposed changes see Section B.
3/   Recent substantial increases in scheduled crop prices are expected
to reverse this trend.
4/   Not including the announcement made on June 30, 1976 which included
the proposals for drastic changes in the Marketing Board system.



SUPPLEMENT I1
Page 2
Scheduled Crop Marketing
4.        The Nigerian Produce Marketing Company Limited (NPMC), a private
company wholly owned by FMG, is responsible for domestic and export market-
ing of scheduled crops, with initial purchase and assembly performed through
a series of franchise operations negotiated between NFMC and the various
Marketing Boards. 1/ Under the 'reformed system' the Marketing Boards have
the status of agents only; 2/ crop purchase finance is advanced by the Central
Bank through NPMC and an allowance paid in the form of a difference between
official producer prices and NPMC takeover prices to cover administrative
overheads and operating expenses. Their income is totally dependent on turn-
over and allowances are therefore fixed annually on the basis of anticipated
purchases. Provision exists for NPMC to make good unavoidable losses such as
could result from below-target throughputs.
5.        Produce taxes have been abolished and the consequent shortfalls in
State revenue are compensated by FIG payments equal to the amount of tax
that would have been collected under the old tonnave-related system.
6.        Determination of producer prices for scheduled crops is the preroga-
tive of the Head of State, advised by the Technical Committee on Producer
Price Fixing; those for annual crops are announced pre-planting, usually in
the Budget Speech, and those for tree crops before the turn of the year or at
some time more appropriate to the harvesting cycles.
7.        Marketing Boards.  Prior to 1972, scheduled croD marketing in
both project areas was the responsibility of the Northern States Marketing
1oard (NSMB). In 1972, however, the former Benue Plateau State Government
established the Benue Plateau Marketing Board (BPMB) in order to introduce
policies and practices specifically geared to the conditions within the
State and the requirements of State farmers.  Since the creal-on of additional
States, the geographical responsibility of the Marketing Boards has remained
in a state of flux (but see section B). BPMB has changed its business address
to the Plateau Marketing Board but still conducts operations throLg'. I.s
self-accounting area office at Makurdi, in the former Benue-Plateau divisions
which now lie within the Benue State. Likewise the divisions contained by the
Ayangba project, which was formerly part of Kwara State, are still served by
NSMB.
1/  Marketing Boards operate on a regional basis and each Marketing
Board covers all scheduled crops.
2/   Earlier Marketing Boards used to be independent with NPMC acting
as their sales agent.



SUPPLEMENT 11
Page 3
S.        Buying Arranagements.  Crop purchases from farmers are made by
Licenced Buying Agents (LBA) serving gazetted buying points. Producer
.prices are uniform at all gazetted points, LBA commercial incentive being
provided by a block allowance per ton of produce, plus a transport dif-
ferential on a fixed ton/kilometer basis. Prior to dispatch to the
Marketing Board depots, all produce is subject to inspection and grading
by staff of the Ministry of Agriculture Produce Inspection Division; crops
accepted as being of exportable quality are check weighed, tied, tagged
and appropriately labeled. Check gradings and weighings are made on
delivery, responsibility for shortfall resting with the LBA.
9.        In order to obtain the necessary Certificate of Registration and
the Trading License, 1/ a prospective LBA must provide the minimum of a
standard covered store and a platform scale, and provide proof of bank credit-
worthiness for obtaining necessary crop financing; due to LBA misuse of
credit provided by the Marketing Boards, this facility has not been provided
since 1971.
10.       Market Problems.  The relatively small number of buying points in
relation to the extent of production results in an active assembly trade
occuring between farmgate and gazetted buying station.  This is frequently
carried out by sub-agents of the LBA who buy from farmers using cash advanced
by LBA and subsequently "sell" to their principals at the gazetted markets.
Under these circumstances the difference between official and farmgate prices
can be substantial, and this problem is compounded by both claimed and actual
lack of finance among LBAs who are required to provide their own working
capital without the backing of NPMC guarantees.
11.       For many scheduled crops, volume of transactions in the project
areas is extremely low (see Table 1) and is insufficient to support satis-
factory buying arrangements while expansion of foodcrop marketing has pro-
vided an increasingly attractive alternative employment for traders' funds.
BPMB attempted to ameliorate this problem by creating an official buying team
to operate in areas where farmers complained of inadequate LBA service. Apart
from the difficulties of volume which still confront farmers (quantities
offered have to be on a truck load basis) this policy could result in LBA
abandoning the less attractive rural markets altogether, and serving only the
more accessible points, leaving BPMB to directly handle the high-cost supplies.
12.       Operating costs of the Marketing Boards are also high in relation
to the tonnage of produce handled; indicative administrative and trading
costs of BPMB are given in Tables 2 and 3 and exclude NPMC overheads. Under
the reformed system farmers are protected against these high costs but the
effective removal of the profit motive is likely to prove a disincentive to
improving management and operational efficiency of the Marketing Board.
1/   For which a fee of US$4.00 each is charged.

Food CroD Marketing
13.       The extensive nature of both supply and demand has resulted in
a large number of somewhat unconnected markets. Tne degree of intermarket
relationship differs both geographically and between crops, but is generally
greatest for those having few rmiain supplying areas, such as cowpeas, and
least where there are many localized resources of supply such as occurs with
maize and, in some cases, yams.
14.       Most markets are efficient in themselves, but although in some cases
this efficiency has been shown to extend throughout a market area 1/ there is
evidence that many individual markets operate in almost startling isolation
from each other, particularly in urban centers. 2/ To some extent price
irregularity reflects the high cost of short haul transportaton and thinness
of supply and demand in a typical market; however non-availability of accurate
information also contributes to this situation and severely inhibits the de-
velopment of seasonal as well as daily marketing decisions and operations.
Difficulties over obtaining and disseminating marketing information are
magnified through goods being sold by volume rather than weight.  Measures of
similar description can vary in capacity, 3/ while natural weight also differs
between crops. For grains and pulses, the smallness of the basic unit of
trade relative to that of the monetary system, results in very coarse price
movements.
15.       Seasonal production effects are magnified by dispersed and frag-
mented output; furthermore, while crops grown mainly for sale tend to be
marketed very shortly after harvest, residual quantities produced primarily
for home consumption may be held off the market for some months, frequently
until the prospects for the following season become clear. Under these
circumstances, balanced judgement of available supply is virtually impossible.
16.       Food crop marketing thus tends to be conducted on a hand-to-mouth
basis with minimal stocks held in the marketing channels at any one time;
this passive approach by traders results in seasonal and interseasonal
storage being carried out mainly on the farm. In the past a number of
small, officially sponsored, cereal storage schemes were introduced with
the aim of assisting markec stability and reducing storage losses. These
have been generally unsuccessful for a number of reasons, including use of
unsuitable types and methods of storage and stock finance problems. Conse-
quently, initial assembly is still almost entirely performed by traders whose
naturally stronger bargaining position is sometimes further enhanced by
farmers' urgent cash needs.
1/   See "The Performance of the Staple Food Grain Marketing System in
Northern Nigeria" H.M. Hays Jr. and J.M. McCoy.
2/   "Price Trends of Major Agricultural Commodities in the western
State" Agricultural Planning and Marketing Division, MaYR, Ibadan,
Western State.
3/   The Lafia Local Government Council (Corn 'Marketing Standard Measure) Rules
stipulate that in a legally established market, threshed grain may only
be traded in accordance with the standard measure issued to a market
master. A standard measure of similar description can be of different
capacity in another division.



SUPPLEMENT 11
Page 5
17.       The Market.  Probably the equivalent of 25% of Nigeria's population
is wholly market-dependent, although a larger share of total production may
be actually traded, most of the difference being in the form of interchange
at village level. Thus each 1% in production could cause a 4% change in
marketed supply with an even greater effect on price levels, particularly
on the downside, because of apparently low price elasticities; the impact
of changes in supply would tend to be further magnified by poor short-term
price correlation between market centers. It must, however, be borne in
mind that with a still primarily subsistence-oriented production effort,
seasonal differences alone can cause output variations in excess of 5%
between one year and the next. In many cases, however, seasonal variation
and producer cash requirements result in considerable consumption elasticity
which to some extent tends to stabilize marketed quantities, particularly
in past years.
18.       Government intervention into the food crop market has been restricted
to strategic grain purchases, but total storage capacity is insignificant at
present. Under the Third Five-Year Development Plan, the strategic grain re-
serve is targeted to reach 250,000 tons.  Responsibility for grain acquisition
and maintenance presently rests with the Ministry of Agriculture's Produce
Inspection Division but control under the Third Plan proposals is expected to
change, either to the recently formed National Grains Company Limited or to a
reorganized marketing board structure.
B. The New Marketing Svstem 1/
19.       Reorganization of the Marketing Boards has been a pressing need for
some time and this was heightened by the creation of the new states. It was
felt undesirable to create Marketing Boards for each of the 19 States because
of the likely enormously expensive overheads, wastages and duplication of
efforts. Previously the Marketing Boards were geared to supplying raw mate-
rials for the world market. The main objective of the proposed new Co=modity
Boards would be to encourage the production and organize the marketing of
Nigeria's major agricultural commodities, and home consumption and local
processing would be promoted rather than export of raw material.
20.       The new commodity marketing system will consist of a price-fixing
authority and seven Commodity Boards which will operate on a nationwide basis.
These seven Commodity Boards are:
-    Cocoa Board, also responsible for tea and coffee;
-    Groundnut Board, also to look after soyabeans, benniseed,
sheanut and ginger;
-    Cotton Board, also to deal with kenaf and similar fibers;
1/   As announced in a broadcast to the nation by the Nigerian Head of
State on June 30, 1976.



SUPPLEMENT 11
Page 6
-    Palm Produce Board, to deal with palm ':ernel, palm oil
and copra;
-    Rubber Board;
-    Grains Board, to handle all food grains; and
-    Root Crop Board, to dealt with tubers and root crops.
Headquarters of each Commodity Board will be located as near as possible
to the area of greatest production of the major commodity. Each Commodity
Board will provide guaranteed minimum prices, but farmers and plantations
are free to sell to consumers or local processors outside the relevant
Commodity Board.
21.       In order not to create a great distruption in marketing, the
Commodity Boards will be established as soon as possible. NPMC and the
existing Marketing Boards will be phased out during the first quarter of
1977.
22.       The effects of these complete reorganizations of the marketing
system and especially the effects of floor prices on project proposals
cannot be assessed until the new system is operational. However, the
Marketing Advisory Service will closely follow this development and dis-
cuss implications from time to tim.e with the Bank.
C. Market Outlook
23.       SuDDlY and Demand.  There have beer. a number of attempts to
quantify present and future supply and demand in Nigeria. 1/  A11 recognize
the inadequacy of the existing data base to conduct such analyses and use
different current and projection parameters in their calculations. These
projections are summarized in Table 4 and could support substantially
1,   - Olayide, S.O., "A Quantitive Analysis of Food Requirements, Supplies

different conclusions, though much of the relative change in the future
surplus/deficit position results from assumptions regarding productivity
improvements. Convincing arguments have been presented against the optimism
of FMG estimates, 1/ while the West Africa Foodgrain Study relies least on
nonquantifiable assumptions 2/ and could prove most accurate in relative
(trend) if not in absolute terms.
24.       As a benchmark for comparison, the following order of magnitude
should be borne in mind. Present annual consumption, expressed in terms of
grain equivalents 3/, is believed to be in the order of 175 kg per head per
year. During the 1960s, food consumption is estimated to have grown at
just under 3% yearly, rising to over 6% after the Civil War and projected
at around 6% for the Third Plan Period. Assuming an average population of
80 million for the next decade, a 5% demand growth would mean roughly an
extra 700,000 tons grain equivalent being required each year. If presently
estimated root cereal proportions are maintained 4/ this would break dowr.
to roundly 380,000 tons of cereals and 1.1 million tons of roots and tubers.
At present average yields this would require additional cultivation of over
700,000 ha each year. Any trend away from roots towards cereals, unless
accompanied by important yield increases, would require an enlargement
of the figure.
25.       Measured against human requirements, quantities of grain used in
animal feed are very small; approximately half of the about 100,000 tons of
feed presently consumed is raw grain, the balance being by-products of the
brewing and milling industries, and high protein meals. Although demand
for livestock products is expected to increase, the effect on overall
requirements for starchy staples is likely to be relatively unimportant, 5/
although impact on individual crops, particularly maize and dwarf sorghum,
could be significant.
1/   Nigeria Staple Foodgrain Projections, oo. cit.
2/   While others are based largely on estimates on income growth and
demand elasticity, this study centers primarily on estimated growth
in urban (and hence market-dependent) population and its impact on
the present Nigerian food balance.
3/   For a given weight, grains contain between 3.25 and 4.0 times as
many calories as roots and tubers.
4/   About 95 kg grain and 270 kg roots.
5/   Over the remainder of the decade a 20% compound growth rate for
animal feed would add an average of less than 50,000 tons per
year to the total demand for starchy staples.

D. Prices
26.       Domestic Prices.  Data on domestic crop prices in Nigeria are
limited and unreliable. Ministry of Agriculture crop and weather reports
provide a monthly record of retail prices in selected markets but enumeration
procedures are inadequate 1/ while differentials between wholesale and farm-
gate price are not collected. Despite guaranteed prices for the scheduled
crops, a large proportion of surplus production, except for cotton and palm
kernels, also enters the domestic market. This situation has largely resulted
from rising domestic food prices compared with those for export crops, but
continues despite substantial recent increases in guaranteed prices. Market
prices collected during appraisal were often at or below guaranteed price
levels, a situation that tends to reflect deficient marketing board services
rather than noncompetitive prices.
27.       For crops not traded in quantity on the world market, existing
farmgate prices have been assembled both by reference to crop and weather
report records (Appendix 1) and by consultation with cooperative staff,
farm association officials and large farmers; check weighings of local
measures were also made in the Ayangba area. Conversions to farmgate
were made on the basis of estimated retail overheads, local transport
along a 50 km radius, and costs of assembly and bagging (Appendix 2). For
millet, sorghum and rice, farmgate prices have been checked against imports
from world markets into main demand centers in the country. Close and
acceptable correlation occurs except for rice which remains something of
an enigma.  Rice has the status of a luxury food in Nigeria and market
prices are unrelated to those of the world market, despite recent removal
of import tariffs; this is reflected in the high prices paid for paddy by
the Rice Company, Jos (Appendix 2, Table 3).
28.       Price Forecasts.  Evaluation of the supply/demand estimates 2/
(Table 4) indicates quite clearly that stimulated food crop production
would be effective import substitution, in contrast to the officially
marketed output of scheduled crops which would be essentially export directed.
Considerations of world market conditions are thus likely to govern the overall
prospect for production, modified as necessary by circumstances specific to
Nigeria.
29.       Grains.  Combined incremental production of grain crops by Year 5
of the project amounts to about 58,000 tons; this is small in relation to
projected grain deficits even in the company of other Government efforts to
1/   Information is collected on prices for local measures and converted
into prices per ton. Standard weights are used in this conversion
and vary substantially (up to 100%) between divisions. Size of
the local measure results in coarse price differences, this being
most noticeable when enumerators changed to the metric system.
During assembly, marked and abrupt price changes pass unquestioned.
2/   Except for FMG estimates.



SUPPLEMENT 11
Page 9
stimulate production. 1/ For maize, sorghum and rice, IBRD forecasts have
been made as the base for calculation; rice presently supports a price incon-
sistent with world trade or its nutritional worth and is expected to decline
during project development. For millet, economic pressure from other crops is
expected to result in reduced millet plantings 2/ despite the importance of
the crop as an early source of grain, particularly in Lafia; this trend is
also predicted in the three northern projects and prices could improve in
relationship with those for other cereals. Much of the early planted maize is
harvested green, the bulk of the later crop being taken through to grain; in
both cases prices are expressed in grain equivalent terms. Higher losses
occur with the early crops due to severe problems posed by climatic conditions
at and after harvest.
30.       Cowpeas.  Cowpeas are an extremely important source of vegetable
protein within the area and the country as a whole. The crop is widely traded
but suffers badly from pre- and postharvest infestation which adversely
affects producer returns. Despite successive increases in value over the past
few years, future prices are expected to decline slightly in line with the
general trend.
31.       Benniseed.  There is a small but steady local food trade for benni-
seed which is fairly widely retailed throughout the area. The main market,
however, is as a scheduled export crop, and prices are forecast on this basis.
Benniseed attracts a rather premium position amongst oil seeds on the world
market. 3/ In the absence of IBRD forecases, NPMC current export price
estimates have been used but trends have been pegged to those for groundnuts.
32.       Roots and Tubers.  Root and tuber crop development represents the
main thrust of both projects and combined incremental production amounts to
330,000 tons; this, however, only represents just over 10% of the West African
Foodgrain Study projected shortfall in Nigeria, but judgment of impact on the
domestic market must depend on production developments elsewhere in the coun-
try. Roots and tubers are presently an unimportant component of development
projects in Nigeria, though distribution of improved cassava cuttings has
begun in the western states. However, in view of Third National Development
Plan proposals for starch processing from cassava, 4/ incremental market
surpluses are expected to be readily absorbed into the economy. Current
relative prices for cassava (as gari) compared with the grain crops generally
reflect its caloric contents and forecasted prices are therefore expected to
move in line with those for maize.
1/   Operation Feed the Nation, The National Accelerated Food Production
Project and the IBRD assisted northern projects.
2/   Returns to labor are low and prospects for improvement limited.
3/   Some countries require a minimum proportion of sesame oil in
domestic cooking oils and sesame seed is also used in the bakery
trade.
4/   A plant is under consideration for Lafia.

33.       In contrast, yams contain only a quarter as many calories as
maize yet weight for weight trade roughly at par.. In addition, there are
marked price differentials between centers of production and demand which
are far in excess of normal transport components.  The intrinsic appeal
of yams, a prized traditional food, seems unlikely to decline, while the
backing of a sound market intelligence service could substantially i=Drove
producer returns.  Prices have therefore been based on the lowest recail
market records and are assumed to be maintained under the projeccs.  Storage
loss can be high for yams and no new cechnology is available to reduce th-s;
prices have therefore been discounted to reflect this.

a flat rate of N 0.05 per tor.b   for long haul and N 5.00 per ton for sho-t
haul has been used. Statutory crop marketing costs are those presently
ruling; bags have been charged at current new price.
